### Module 1.2: Administrative Onboarding
- **ID:** mod_1_2
- **Subtitle:** Complete all paperwork efficiently Set up systems and tools Remove day-one confusion
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Onboarding Checklist**  
Administrative onboarding sounds boring, but getting this wrong costs you money. If your CRM isn't set up, you can't log leads. If your design software isn't working, you can't pull proposals. We're going to move through the checklist quickly and completely. Your documents include your contractor agreement, tax paperwork, compliance forms, and if applicable your non-compete. Get them signed and submitted today.

**Tools & Systems Setup**  
After paperwork, you'll set up your tools. This means your CRM account \u2014 log in and confirm it works. Your design software like Aurora or HelioScope \u2014 install it and test it. Your company communication tools, calendar links, and mobile apps for route planning. Do not leave today without testing every login. One credential failing on your first real appointment will cost you a deal.

### Module 1.3: Company Culture & Expectations
- **ID:** mod_1_3
- **Subtitle:** Clarify behavioral standards Set performance benchmarks Explain support systems
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Daily Schedule Expectations**  
Your most valuable asset is your golden hours. That's two pm to seven pm \u2014 the window when homeowners are home and receptive. During golden hours, you knock doors or make calls. Nothing else. Admin happens in the morning between eight and ten. Team calls and training run from ten to noon. This structure exists because it works. Top reps protect their golden hours like they protect their income \u2014 because those hours ARE their income.

**Weekly KPIs**  
Here are the numbers you're aiming for every week. One hundred to one hundred fifty doors knocked, or an equivalent number of outbound calls. Ten to fifteen appointments set. Five to eight appointment sits. And by week three, one to three closed deals per week. These aren't arbitrary targets \u2014 they're the activity levels that produce consistent income. If your numbers are off, we'll use these benchmarks to diagnose where the leak is.

**Manager Support Structure**  
You are not doing this alone. Your manager will check in with you daily for the first two weeks. Ride-alongs are available whenever you need a live model to follow. The team channel is open for real-time questions. Use these resources aggressively in your first weeks. The reps who grow fastest are the ones who ask the most questions, not the ones who try to figure it all out themselves.

**Compensation Deep Dive**  
Understanding how you get paid removes anxiety and replaces it with focus. Commission is typically paid per kilowatt installed, or as a flat rate per deal \u2014 depending on your company's structure. There are bonuses and accelerators for hitting volume. There is also a chargeback policy for cancellations, which is why we qualify hard and prevent buyer's remorse from day one. Ask your manager for the exact figures for your market today.

### Module 1.4: Solar Industry Overview
- **ID:** mod_1_4
- **Subtitle:** Understand the solar market landscape Know your competition Grasp the urgency (why now?)
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Why Solar, Why Now**  
The residential solar market in the United States is one of the fastest-growing industries in the country. The three primary reasons homeowners go solar are utility bill inflation, energy independence, and home value increase. Utility rates have gone up six to eight percent annually for the last two decades with no sign of stopping. That is the foundation of every conversation you'll have. You are offering homeowners a hedge against something that is guaranteed to keep rising.

**The Federal Tax Credit**  
The federal Investment Tax Credit \u2014 the ITC \u2014 gives homeowners a thirty percent credit on the total system cost. On a thirty-thousand-dollar system, that's nine thousand dollars back in their first tax filing. This credit is locked in through 2032. That's not forever. Every year a homeowner waits is a year of paying full utility rates AND a year closer to the credit potentially changing. The ITC creates real, legitimate urgency in your conversations.

**Common Solar Myths**  
You will hear these three myths constantly, and you need to be ready. First \u2014 solar doesn't work in cloudy states. False. Germany is one of the cloudiest countries in the world and leads in solar adoption. Panels produce electricity from daylight, not direct sunlight. Second \u2014 panels are too expensive. With financing, most homeowners pay less per month than their current utility bill from day one. Third \u2014 the technology will be better next year. The technology has improved every year for forty years. Waiting always costs more than it saves.

### Module 1.5: Solar Technology Fundamentals
- **ID:** mod_1_5
- **Subtitle:** Speak with technical authority Answer homeowner questions confidently Avoid sounding like a typical \
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**How Solar Works \u2014 The Simple Flow**  
Here is how a solar system works in plain language. Sunlight hits the panels. The panels generate DC electricity \u2014 direct current. The inverter converts that DC power into AC \u2014 alternating current \u2014 which is what your home actually uses. Any excess power you produce flows back to the utility grid, and you receive credits for it through a program called net metering. At night, or on cloudy days, you draw from the grid and use those credits. It's a closed loop that works in your favor.

**System Components**  
A typical solar system has five main components. The panels themselves \u2014 monocrystalline panels are the most efficient and most common in residential installs. The inverter, which converts power \u2014 string inverters are standard, microinverters are premium and monitor each panel individually. The racking system that mounts panels to the roof. The monitoring system, which lets homeowners track production via an app. And the meter, which measures what you consume versus what you produce.

**Warranties You Must Know**  
Warranties are not a detail \u2014 they're a closing tool. The panel performance warranty guarantees eighty percent output for twenty-five years. The inverter warranty runs ten to twenty-five years depending on the brand. The workmanship warranty covers the installation itself for ten years. When a homeowner asks what happens if something breaks, your answer is immediate and confident \u2014 here's what's covered, here's for how long, and here's who handles it. That confidence builds trust.

**What Solar Does NOT Do**  
Being honest about limitations is what separates consultants from salespeople. Solar will not eliminate your utility bill one hundred percent \u2014 connection fees and local charges remain, typically ten to twenty dollars per month. Solar will not power your home during a grid outage unless you have a battery. And solar does not pay you money directly, unless you're in a state with SREC programs. Setting these expectations upfront prevents cancellations and builds long-term trust.

### Module 1.5a: Understanding Utility Bills & Net Metering

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 1.5A — Solar Physics Made Simple

**Insert after existing Module 1.5 | Day 1 — Onboarding & Foundation**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Reps don't need a physics degree to sell solar. But they do need enough technical understanding to sound credible when a homeowner asks: "How does this thing actually work?" Module 1.5A gives reps the backend knowledge of how sunlight becomes electricity — explained in a way that stays simple, accurate, and immediately usable in the field.

### Why It Matters
Technical credibility is an early trust signal. In the first few minutes of a homeowner conversation, reps are being assessed: Do they actually know what they're talking about? Reps who can explain solar physics simply and confidently — without over-engineering the explanation — immediately differentiate themselves from reps who give a vague pitch and move on. This is the difference between a consultant and a canvasser.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module sits early in Day 1 because everything downstream — inverter knowledge, site engineering awareness, financial presentations, battery conversations — requires a foundation of how solar actually works. Reps who skip or rush this module will feel shaky on technical questions for the rest of the curriculum.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Explain in plain language how sunlight is converted into usable electricity in a residential solar system.
2. Define DC and AC electricity and explain why the conversion between them matters.
3. Describe why solar production varies throughout the day, across seasons, and in different weather conditions.
4. Explain the impact of shading, roof orientation, and panel tilt on system production — without using engineering jargon.
5. Deliver a simple, confident solar physics explanation that a homeowner can understand and remember.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — How Sunlight Becomes Electricity: The Simple Story
The goal here is not to teach physics — it is to give reps a mental model they can draw on and explain conversationally.

**The core process in plain language:**

Solar panels are made up of photovoltaic (PV) cells. When sunlight hits these cells, it knocks electrons loose from their atoms. That movement of electrons is electricity — specifically, a flow of electrons in one direction, called Direct Current (DC).

That DC electricity then travels from the panels through wiring to an inverter, which converts it into the type of electricity that homes use (Alternating Current, or AC). From there, the electricity flows through the home's electrical panel and powers whatever is running — lights, appliances, HVAC, etc.

Any electricity the system produces beyond what the home is using in real time is either sent to a battery for storage, or sent to the grid (where the homeowner typically receives a credit under net metering programs).

**Simple summary for reps to remember:**
Sun hits panels → electrons move → DC electricity → inverter converts to AC → powers the home → excess stored in battery or sent to grid.

**Teaching point:** Reps do not need to explain photovoltaic cell chemistry to homeowners. They need to be able to say: "Sunlight knocks electrons loose in the panel — that movement of electrons is electricity. The inverter converts it into the kind your home runs on. That's the whole chain."

### Section 2 — DC vs. AC: Why It Matters and How to Explain It
This is a common homeowner question — and one that trips up reps who don't have a simple answer ready.

**DC (Direct Current):**
- Electricity flows in one direction only
- What solar panels produce naturally
- Also what batteries store
- Not directly usable by most home appliances

**AC (Alternating Current):**
- Electricity that alternates direction rapidly (60 times per second in the US)
- What the utility grid delivers
- What virtually every home appliance is designed to use
- What the inverter converts DC into

**Why reps need to understand this:**
- It explains why an inverter is a required component of every system
- It explains why a battery (which stores DC) also needs a component to convert to AC when it discharges
- It helps reps answer: "Why do I need an inverter? Can't the power just go straight from the panels to my house?"

**Simple rep language:**
"Solar panels produce power in a form called DC — direct current — which flows in one direction. Your home runs on AC — alternating current — which is what the grid delivers. The inverter is the translator between those two. Without it, the power from your panels couldn't run your appliances."

### Section 3 — Why Solar Production Changes: The Daily and Seasonal Curve
One of the most important things a rep can explain is why a solar system doesn't produce at a constant rate. Homeowners who don't understand this may feel misled when they see production data.

**The daily production curve:**
- Solar production starts at or near zero at sunrise
- Ramps up as the sun rises higher in the sky
- Peaks around solar noon (not always 12 PM clock time — depends on geography and time of year)
- Ramps back down through the afternoon
- Reaches zero at or before sunset

The shape of this curve is why production estimates are modeled in kilowatt-hours per day, per month, and per year — not as a constant rate.

**Why peak production matters for TOU rate conversations:**
- Peak solar production happens around midday
- Peak electricity cost in TOU markets happens in the late afternoon and evening
- This mismatch is why battery storage creates additional value in TOU markets (connects to Modules 4.1A and 4.4A)

**Seasonal variation:**
- Summer: Longer days, higher sun angle = more daily production
- Winter: Shorter days, lower sun angle = less daily production
- Annual production estimates average these seasonal differences
- Good system designs account for both the best and worst production months

**Teaching point for reps:** When a homeowner asks "why did I produce less in December?" — the answer is not that the system is broken. It's that winter days are shorter and the sun is lower in the sky. Reps who know this avoid homeowner panic and cancellations.

**Simple rep language:**
"Your system will produce more in summer when days are long and the sun is high, and a bit less in winter. That's completely normal — just like how your utility bill goes up in summer when the AC runs more. The annual estimate we're looking at averages both out."

### Section 4 — What Reduces Production: Shading, Orientation, Tilt, and Weather

**Shading:**
- Shade is the single biggest variable that can reduce a system's production below design estimates
- Even partial shade on one panel can significantly reduce output, depending on inverter type (this connects to Module 1.5B on microinverters vs. string inverters)
- Sources of shading: trees, chimneys, vents, neighboring structures, antennas
- Shade impact is why site surveys include a shade analysis
- **Rep awareness:** If a roof has obvious shading from large trees during the sales visit, this is worth noting and setting expectations around — do not oversell production on a heavily shaded roof

**Orientation (Azimuth):**
- Solar panels produce the most energy when they face true south in the northern hemisphere
- East- or west-facing panels produce meaningfully less than south-facing panels
- North-facing panels are generally not viable for solar in the northern hemisphere
- Slight deviations from true south (e.g., southeast or southwest) are common and manageable — production estimates account for this
- **Rep awareness:** Knowing roof orientation before or during the sales visit helps set accurate production expectations

**Tilt:**
- The optimal tilt angle for a solar panel roughly equals the latitude of the location (e.g., for a home at 35° latitude, a tilt of approximately 35° is ideal)
- Most residential roofs have pitches between 15° and 40° — generally within a workable range
- Flat roofs require mounting equipment to achieve the optimal tilt
- **Rep awareness:** Very low-pitch or very high-pitch roofs may produce less than average — the site survey and design process will quantify this

**Weather:**
- Clouds reduce but do not eliminate production — diffuse sunlight still generates some power
- Rain can actually improve production temporarily by cleaning dust off panels
- Extreme heat slightly reduces panel efficiency (counterintuitive but true — panels are tested at 25°C and produce slightly less at higher temperatures)
- Snow can temporarily block panels but most panels shed snow quickly due to their angle and the smooth glass surface
- **Rep awareness:** Homeowners in cloudy climates (Pacific Northwest, New England) can still benefit from solar — production is lower but so are production estimates

### Section 5 — How to Explain Solar Simply at the Door and at the Table
The goal of this module is not to turn reps into technicians. It is to give them the fluency to explain solar naturally when homeowners ask.

**At the door (30-second version):**
"Basically, the panels on your roof capture sunlight and convert it into electricity your home runs on. Any power you don't use goes to the grid and you get a credit for it, or it can go into a battery for later. The system works silently all day and you just see the difference on your bill."

**At the table (2-minute version):**
Walk through the simple chain: sun → panels → DC → inverter → AC → home. Mention that production varies by time of day and season — and that the estimate already accounts for that. Briefly note how their specific roof's orientation and any shading affects the design.

**When a technical question comes up that you can't answer:**
"That's a great question — I want to make sure I give you accurate information rather than guess. Let me get that answer from our technical team and get back to you." This is always a better answer than an inaccurate one.

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The simple chain (sun → panels → DC → inverter → AC → home) should be something every rep can say from memory by the end of this session. Drill it.
- The "why production varies" section is operationally critical — reps who don't understand this will field angry homeowner calls every December asking why their system is "broken."
- Shading awareness early in a rep's development prevents the most common over-promise on production. Reinforce: if you can see obvious shade on the roof during a sales visit, set expectations now.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "The panels produce the same amount all day." → No. Production follows a bell curve tied to sun position.
- "Clouds mean zero production." → No. Diffuse sunlight still generates power — less, but not zero.
- "If the roof isn't perfectly south-facing, solar won't work." → Not true. Deviations are common and accounted for in design. East/west roofs produce less but are still viable in many markets.
- "The panels produce AC power directly." → No. Panels produce DC. The inverter converts to AC. This distinction matters for understanding why inverters are required.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Do not teach photovoltaic cell physics at the molecular level. "Sunlight knocks electrons loose and that movement is electricity" is sufficient and accurate.
- Do not have reps memorize production curves by month. The concept matters; specific percentages don't.
- Do not turn the DC/AC section into an electrical engineering lesson. Keep it at: "DC flows one way, AC alternates — inverter translates between them."

**Where to slow down:**
- The production variability section. Have reps physically draw the daily production bell curve on paper. A visual they create themselves sticks better than one they're shown.
- The shading section. Have reps think about a neighborhood they work in and name 2–3 common sources of roof shade they've seen. Make it concrete.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "How Does This Actually Work?"
**Homeowner type:** Curious but skeptical — wants to understand before they'll listen to anything else
**Setup:** During a door knock, a homeowner who seems interested says: "Before you go any further — can you actually explain to me how solar works? Like, step by step. I'm an engineer and I've heard a lot of vague answers."
**Skill being tested:** Delivering a technically accurate but appropriately simple explanation of the solar production chain under mild pressure from a credentialed skeptic.
**Pass criteria:** Rep correctly walks through the full chain (sun → panels → DC → inverter → AC → home), handles the DC/AC distinction accurately, and delivers the explanation calmly and naturally without becoming defensive or over-technical.

---

### Scenario 2 — "Why Is My Neighbor's Bill Still High?"
**Homeowner type:** Aware that a neighbor has solar but skeptical because the neighbor still has a large bill
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "My neighbor went solar two years ago and their bill is still pretty high. How is that supposed to work? Is the system not doing anything?"
**Skill being tested:** Using production variability knowledge (orientation, shading, TOU rates) to explain why solar outcomes vary — without disparaging the neighbor's system or the competitor who installed it.
**Pass criteria:** Rep identifies at least two plausible reasons a solar homeowner might still have a high bill (shade, orientation, TOU mismatch, oversized usage), explains them simply, and uses the opportunity to segue into what a properly designed system for this homeowner would look like.

---

### Scenario 3 — "My System Produced Way Less This Month"
**Homeowner type:** Post-install homeowner who is worried — referred by a previous customer
**Setup:** A homeowner who installed solar 6 months ago (referred by one of your customers) reaches out concerned: "My system produced about 30% less this month than last month. Is something wrong with it?"
**Skill being tested:** Using seasonal production knowledge to explain the variation calmly and accurately — distinguishing normal seasonal decline from a potential system issue.
**Pass criteria:** Rep correctly identifies that the decline is consistent with seasonal production variation (e.g., transitioning from summer to fall/winter), explains the daily and seasonal curve simply, and either confirms the decline is normal or appropriately flags it for technical review if the numbers seem off.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
What type of electricity do solar panels produce naturally?
- A) Alternating Current (AC)
- B) Direct Current (DC)
- C) Both AC and DC equally
- D) Neither — they produce heat, which is converted to electricity
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
Explain in 2–3 sentences why a solar system produces more electricity in July than in December in most of the continental United States.

**3. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner's roof faces due east. Compared to a due-south-facing roof of the same size, how will this affect solar production?
- A) No effect — orientation doesn't matter once the panels are designed correctly
- B) Production will be somewhat lower than a south-facing roof, though east-facing systems are still viable in many markets
- C) Production will be higher because east-facing panels catch the morning sun before it gets hot
- D) East-facing roofs cannot support solar installation
*(Correct answer: B)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner who installed a solar system in March calls in December saying their production has dropped significantly compared to the summer months. They're worried the system is malfunctioning. What should the rep say?
*(Expected answer: Explain the seasonal production curve — shorter days, lower sun angle in winter naturally reduce production. This is expected and the annual production estimate already accounts for seasonal variation. If production is within the expected range for winter, the system is likely functioning normally. The rep should check the monitoring data to confirm before making any technical assertions.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
Solar panels produce _______ electricity, which flows through wiring to the _______, which converts it into _______ electricity that home appliances can use.
*(Correct: DC / Direct Current; inverter; AC / Alternating Current)*

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"Let me ask you something. If a homeowner stops you at the door and says, 'Before we go any further — explain to me how solar actually works' — what do you say? Not the pitch. Not the financial story. The actual physics. [Pause.] If you hesitated, you're in the right place. By the end of this session, every one of you will be able to answer that question in 30 seconds, clearly and confidently. No textbook language. Just the real answer."

**Concept Explanation (12–15 min)**
Walk through the full chain using the visual on the slide. Stop at each component and ask: "Who can tell me in one sentence what this part does?" Build the chain piece by piece. Don't move forward until the group can say the whole chain from memory.

**Example (5 min)**
Draw the daily production bell curve on a whiteboard in real time — have reps watch it built from scratch. Then ask: "If the utility charges peak rates from 4 PM to 9 PM, and solar production peaks at noon — what does that tell us about battery storage?" Let a rep answer. This connects the physics directly to the financial conversation without forcing it.

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 1.5A-1 (The Simple Chain) — fill in the blanks from memory, then write the one-sentence version for the door. Have 3–4 reps read their door-version aloud. Give specific feedback: too long, too technical, or just right?

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"You're not learning this to become engineers. You're learning this so that when a homeowner asks how it works, you have a real answer — not a vague one. That's the difference between a rep who sounds like they know what they're talking about and one who sounds like they're reading a brochure. One of those reps gets invited in. The other doesn't."

---

---


#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Introduction**  
Understanding how homeowners are currently billed\u2014and exactly how solar changes that equation\u2014is the financial foundation of every solar pitch. Module 1.5A trains reps to read utility bills fluently and explain net metering (NEM) in simple, accessible language. If a rep cannot confidently explain how a utility bill works, they cannot credibly explain why solar is a better financial alternative.

**Learning Objectives**  
The homeowner's utility bill is the 'before' picture. You cannot sell the 'after' if you don't understand the 'before'.

Key Components to Always Locate:
1. Total kWh Usage (monthly and annual if available)
2. The Rate Plan (e.g., Tiered, Time-of-Use)
3. The Total Amount Due
4. Delivery vs. Generation/Supply charges (in deregulated markets)

Never ask the homeowner to interpret their own bill. The rep should glance at the bill and quickly identify these four things. Taking a bill and looking confused destroys credibility instantly.

**Section 2: Tiered vs. Time-of-Use (TOU) Rates**  
Tiered Pricing: The more you use, the more you pay per unit.
- Baseline tier: Cheapest power (e.g., first 500 kWh)
- Tier 2/3: More expensive power (e.g., 501+ kWh)
- The pitch: \

**Section 3: Net Energy Metering (NEM) Explained**  
NEM is the engine that makes solar financially viable for grid-tied homes.

The simple explanation:
\

**Section 4: The 'True-Up' Bill**  
Introduce the importance of understanding the 'before' picture.

**The Anatomy of a Utility Bill**  
Highlight the 4 key components: Usage, Rate Plan, Total Due, Delivery vs. Supply.

**Tiered vs. Time-of-Use (TOU)**  
Compare the two primary rate structures. Discuss peak vs. off-peak.

**Net Energy Metering (NEM)**  
Explain NEM using the 'bank account' analogy: credits and debits.

**1:1 vs. Partial Credit NEM**  
Explain regional differences in net metering policies.

**The True-Up Bill**  
Explain annual reconciliation and non-bypassable base connection charges.

**Introduction (List)**
- Fluently identify the key components of a utility bill: rate plan, tiered vs. Time-of-Use structure, delivery vs. supply charges, and total kWh usage.
- Identify 'peak' and 'off-peak' hours visually and explain their financial impact to a homeowner.
- Explain the core concept of Net Energy Metering (NEM) in plain, non-jargon language.
- Differentiate between 1:1 net metering and partial/avoided-cost net metering (and know which applies in their market).
- Identify when a homeowner's current utility rate makes them an exceptionally good (or poor) candidate for solar.

**Section 1: The Anatomy of a Utility Bill (List)**
- Annual billing cycle vs. monthly billing cycle.
- The 'True-Up' is the annual reconciliation of what was produced vs. what was consumed.
- Setting expectations: A solar bill doesn't usually look like zero every month. There are usually base connection fees ($10-$20/mo) that cannot be offset.

- **Slide:** Net Energy Metering (NEM)

#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Review the sample utility bill provided by your trainer. Identify the Total kWh usage for the month, the Rate Plan (Tiered or TOU), and the Total connection/base fee.
- In your own words, summarize Net Energy Metering (NEM) as if you were explaining it to a homeowner who has never heard the term.
- A homeowner shows you a bill that is highly Tiered, where they pay 15\u00A2 for Tier 1, 28\u00A2 for Tier 2, and 45\u00A2 for Tier 3. How do you explain the financial benefit of solar to them?
#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 1.5b: Solar Financial Structures

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 1.5B — Inverter & Battery Fundamentals

**Insert after Module 1.5A | Day 1 — Onboarding & Foundation**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Homeowners ask about hardware. They've read about microinverters. They want to know if a battery is worth it. They've heard that certain brands are better. Module 1.5B gives reps the working knowledge to handle these conversations confidently — without crossing into engineering territory or making promises about equipment performance that they can't guarantee.

### Why It Matters
Hardware questions are credibility tests. A rep who answers "what's a microinverter?" with "it's better than the other kind" has just lost the room with any homeowner who did their homework. A rep who can explain the difference simply and accurately — and can describe what a battery actually does and doesn't do — is the rep that gets trusted. Hardware fluency at the rep level is not about technical depth; it is about honest, clear communication.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module directly follows 1.5A (Solar Physics) and sets up 1.7A (Site Engineering Awareness). It also feeds into Day 4's battery economics conversations (Modules 4.4A) and Day 5's post-sale pipeline awareness (Module 5.5B), where MPU and equipment issues arise.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Explain what an inverter does in plain language — and why every system needs one.
2. Describe the key differences between string inverters and microinverters — and when each is more appropriate.
3. Explain what a solar battery does, how it stores energy, and when it discharges.
4. Set accurate homeowner expectations about battery backup — what it can and cannot do.
5. Know what reps should never promise about hardware performance or backup capability.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — What an Inverter Does (And Why It's Not Optional)
Building directly on Module 1.5A: solar panels produce DC electricity. Home appliances run on AC electricity. The inverter is what converts DC to AC. Without an inverter, the solar system cannot power the home.

**Key inverter facts every rep needs:**
- Every grid-tied solar system has at least one inverter
- The inverter is also responsible for safety disconnects — in most systems, if the grid goes down, the inverter shuts off the solar system automatically to protect utility workers (this is why solar-only systems don't provide backup power during outages — a key point for homeowner conversations)
- Inverter lifespan is typically 10–15 years for string inverters; microinverters are often warranted for 25 years
- Inverter quality and brand matter — but reps should reference their company's approved equipment list rather than making unsolicited brand comparisons

**Simple rep language:**
"The inverter is the brain of the system. It converts the power your panels produce into the kind of electricity your home can actually use. It also manages the connection to the grid and handles safety shutoffs."

### Section 2 — String Inverters vs. Microinverters: The Rep-Level Comparison
This is one of the most common technical questions reps face, and one of the most commonly fumbled.

**String Inverter:**
- One central inverter handles the output from multiple panels connected in a series (a "string")
- If one panel is shaded or underperforming, the entire string's output is reduced to the lowest-performing panel's level
- Generally less expensive as a system component
- Easier and less expensive to service or replace (one unit vs. many)
- Works well on simple, unshaded roofs with consistent orientation

**Microinverter:**
- Each panel has its own small inverter attached directly to it
- Each panel operates independently — shade on one panel does not affect others
- Generally more expensive than string inverter systems
- Panel-level monitoring — production data available for each individual panel
- Better performance on complex roofs with multiple orientations or shading challenges
- Typically warranted for 25 years (longer than most string inverters)

**Power Optimizer (a middle option worth knowing):**
- DC optimizers attached to each panel, paired with a string inverter
- Similar shade tolerance to microinverters but at a different price point
- Less common but used by some major companies and worth recognizing if homeowners ask

**How to explain string vs. micro to a homeowner:**
"The difference comes down to how the panels work together. With a traditional system, all your panels are linked — if one gets shade, the whole string slows down. With microinverters, each panel works independently, so shade on one doesn't affect the others. Which one is right for your roof depends on your specific situation."

**What reps should NOT do:**
- Do not blanket-recommend microinverters for every homeowner — your company's design team makes this call based on the site
- Do not disparage string inverters as "inferior" — they are the right solution for many roofs
- Do not promise specific performance advantages without verified design data

### Section 3 — What a Battery Actually Does
This is where many reps have either too much misinformation (overselling backup) or too little (unable to explain the product at all).

**The battery's actual function:**
- A solar battery stores excess electricity that the solar system produces during the day
- That stored energy can then be used at night or during periods when the solar system isn't producing
- In TOU markets, the battery allows homeowners to use their stored solar power during expensive peak hours instead of buying from the grid
- In outage scenarios, a battery with the right configuration can power specific circuits or appliances when the grid is down

**How batteries store energy:**
- Solar panels produce DC electricity
- Batteries store DC electricity
- When the battery discharges, it sends electricity through the system's inverter (or a dedicated battery inverter) which converts it to AC for home use
- Modern battery systems (like the leading residential products on the market) are integrated AC-coupled or DC-coupled systems that manage this automatically

**Battery capacity basics:**
- Battery capacity is measured in kilowatt-hours (kWh) — how much total energy the battery can store
- A typical residential battery stores between 10–15 kWh per unit
- A typical US household uses 25–30 kWh per day — meaning one battery cannot power a whole home for 24 hours at normal usage
- Batteries are often paired in multiples for greater backup capacity or for homes with higher usage

### Section 4 — Backup Expectations vs. Misconceptions
This is the most compliance-critical section of the module. Overpromising battery backup capabilities is one of the fastest ways to generate cancellations and complaints.

**What a battery CAN do (accurately stated):**
- Power selected circuits or appliances during a grid outage, if the system is configured for backup
- Store excess solar production during the day for use at night
- Reduce evening peak electricity purchases in TOU markets
- Provide partial whole-home backup depending on consumption habits and battery size

**What a battery CANNOT do (by default or without proper configuration):**
- Power an entire average home indefinitely during a grid outage
- Automatically power the whole home if only "critical load" backup is configured
- Fully replace a generator for high-consumption scenarios (well pumps, HVAC, EV charging)
- Provide backup if the system is not specifically configured for islanding/backup mode

**The #1 backup misconception to address proactively:**
Many homeowners assume that having a battery means their home will run normally during a power outage. This is often not accurate. Backup capability depends on:
- Battery size (kWh)
- Which loads are on the backup circuit (critical load panel vs. whole home)
- How much the homeowner uses during the outage
- Whether solar is producing during the outage to recharge the battery

**Safe rep language for backup conversations:**
"A battery can absolutely provide backup power — the question is for which appliances and for how long. The system is typically designed to keep your most important circuits running — things like your refrigerator, lights, and phone charging — rather than the whole home at full load. If whole-home backup is a priority for you, we'll want to make sure the design accounts for that."

### Section 5 — Battery Basics at Rep Level
Reps don't need to know battery chemistry. They need to know enough to have an honest, accurate conversation.

**Key battery facts for reps:**
- Depth of Discharge (DoD): Most modern batteries should not be discharged to 0% — they're designed to operate within a usable range (usually 90–100% DoD for leading lithium-based batteries)
- Cycle life: Batteries are rated for a certain number of charge/discharge cycles — typically thousands over their warrantied life
- Warranty: Most residential batteries carry a 10-year warranty — reps should reference the specific warranty for their company's approved equipment
- Degradation: Like solar panels, batteries lose some capacity over time — typically modest over the warranty period

**What reps should never promise about batteries:**
- Do not promise a specific backup duration ("Your battery will last 3 days") without a detailed load analysis
- Do not promise that the battery will power the whole home
- Do not quote specific battery pricing without going through the company's proposal process
- Do not compare competitor battery products unless you have current, verified specifications

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The backup misconception section needs to be delivered with conviction. Overpromising backup is a recurring source of post-install complaints and cancellations. Every rep needs to understand this before they have battery conversations in the field.
- The string vs. microinverter comparison should be taught as "here's how to explain the difference to a homeowner" — not "here's which is better." The company's design team makes equipment decisions based on the site, not the rep's preference.
- Battery capacity math is useful for setting realistic expectations. Walk reps through the simple calculation: 10–15 kWh stored ÷ average daily home usage = partial coverage. Make this real.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "Microinverters are always better — I should always recommend them." → Not always. The site determines the right inverter type. Reps who over-recommend one type create design conflicts.
- "If you have a battery, you'll have power during an outage." → Depends on system configuration, battery size, and load. Never say "you'll have power during an outage" without specifying what's on the backup circuit.
- "The battery charges from the grid too." → Some systems allow this (and it can be a feature for TOU optimization), but it varies by system. Do not assume.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Reps do not need to know specific battery brands or chemistry types beyond what their company uses.
- Power optimizer vs. microinverter distinctions are secondary — teach the core string vs. micro concept first and let power optimizers be a "bonus" for reps who want to go deeper.
- DoD, cycle life, and degradation are reference points for credibility — not talking points reps need to lead with.

**Where to slow down:**
- The battery backup expectations section. Have reps write down what they previously believed about battery backup before this module, then compare to what they now know. The gap is often significant.
- The "what not to promise" list. Read through it as a group and have reps identify which items they might have been tempted to say before this training.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "I Read That Microinverters Are Way Better"
**Homeowner type:** Did research online, confident in what they read, slightly testing the rep
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "I read a bunch of reviews and microinverters are clearly superior to string inverters. Why is your proposal showing a string inverter? Is that a cheaper option you're trying to push?"
**Skill being tested:** Explaining the string vs. microinverter distinction accurately, defending the company's equipment recommendation without being defensive, and not over-promising that a microinverter would definitively be better for this homeowner.
**Pass criteria:** Rep explains the difference between string and micro correctly, identifies that the right choice depends on the specific roof and shading situation, defers to the design team's recommendation with confidence, and does not disparage string inverters.

---

### Scenario 2 — "Will My House Have Power During Outages?"
**Homeowner type:** Lives in an area prone to outages; this is a primary concern
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "We lose power at least three or four times a year. If I get solar with a battery, will my house stay on when the grid goes down?"
**Skill being tested:** Setting accurate battery backup expectations without underselling (making it sound like it does nothing) or overselling (promising full-home outage coverage).
**Pass criteria:** Rep accurately explains that backup capability depends on system configuration and what loads are on the backup circuit, avoids promising full-home indefinite backup, asks what appliances are most critical to the homeowner during an outage, and explains how the backup system would be designed around those priorities.

---

### Scenario 3 — "How Long Will the Battery Keep My House Running?"
**Homeowner type:** Analytical, wants numbers before committing
**Setup:** Homeowner asks: "If I get a 13.5 kWh battery and the power goes out at night, how long will my house run on it?"
**Skill being tested:** Using battery capacity basics to give an honest, appropriately qualified answer — without either refusing to engage with the math or giving a false precision answer.
**Pass criteria:** Rep correctly explains that the answer depends on how much power the home is using during the outage, walks through the basic math (battery kWh ÷ hourly usage = approximate hours), is honest about the difference between critical load backup and full-home backup, and does not give a specific hours number without qualifying it against the homeowner's actual usage.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
Why does a solar system automatically shut off during a power outage in most configurations (without battery backup)?
- A) The solar panels stop producing when there is no sunlight
- B) The inverter disconnects from the grid automatically as a safety measure to protect utility workers
- C) The utility company remotely turns off the system
- D) The system's battery drains within seconds of an outage
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
A homeowner has a south-facing roof with no shade and a simple, single-pitch design. A homeowner next door has a complex roof with multiple orientations and shading from mature trees. Which inverter type might be more appropriate for each — and why?

**3. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner asks: "If I get a battery, will my house have power if the grid goes down?" The most accurate response is:
- A) "Yes — that's exactly what the battery is for."
- B) "No — batteries don't provide backup power."
- C) "It depends on how the system is configured and which circuits are on your backup panel. Let's talk about what appliances matter most to you during an outage."
- D) "Batteries only store power for the next day — they're not designed for outages."
*(Correct answer: C)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner says: "I want to make sure my battery keeps my whole house running for at least 48 hours during an outage. Can you guarantee that?" What should the rep say?
*(Expected answer: The rep should be honest — a standard residential battery system is not designed to power a whole home at full load for 48 hours without solar recharge. The rep should explain what a battery can realistically do, ask about the homeowner's critical loads, and explain that the system can be designed around specific backup priorities. The rep should not make the guarantee.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
A string inverter processes electricity from multiple panels connected in a _______, meaning shade on one panel can affect the output of the _______. A microinverter is attached to _______ panel individually, so shade on one panel does not affect _______.
*(Correct: series / string; entire string; each; the others)*

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"Quick scenario: A homeowner who clearly did their research before you knocked says, 'I've been reading about microinverters — why doesn't your proposal have them?' What do you say? [Pause for answers.] Or: 'If I get a battery, my whole house will stay on during outages, right?' What do you say to that? These are not trick questions. They're real questions you will hear. Today you're going to have real answers."

**Concept Explanation (15 min)**
Walk through inverter types using the visual comparison. Move directly into battery fundamentals. Pause at the battery backup section and spend disproportionate time here — this is where the most field damage happens from overselling. Ask reps: "What did you previously believe about battery backup before today?" Use their answers to frame the correction.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's the backup math. A typical residential battery holds about 10–15 kWh. A typical home uses 25–30 kWh per day. So one battery covers roughly half a day of normal usage. But during an outage, people often reduce their usage — not running the dishwasher, not charging the car — so a single battery might last a full night. That's honest. What's not honest is telling someone their battery will run their house for 48 hours. Walk through that math with the homeowner. They'll respect you for it."

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 1.5B-3 (Backup Expectation Correction) — do this as a group rewrite exercise. Read each original statement aloud and have the group call out what's wrong with it before anyone writes the correction. This creates active engagement rather than passive note-taking.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Hardware questions are credibility tests. The homeowner isn't trying to stump you — they're trying to figure out if you're the real deal. The rep who gives an honest, simple, accurate answer passes that test every time. The rep who oversells or fumbles fails it — even if the homeowner doesn't say so out loud. They just stop trusting you. Know your hardware. Know what to promise. Know what not to."

#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Introduction**  
Solar is not a one-size-fits-all financial product. Module 1.5B trains reps on the primary ways a homeowner can pay for solar: Cash, Loan, and Third-Party Ownership (Lease / PPA). The goal is not to bias the rep toward one specific product, but to teach them how to match the right financial vehicle to the homeowner's specific financial situation and goals.

**Learning Objectives**  
The fundamental premise of most solar financing:
\

**Section 2: The Federal Investment Tax Credit (ITC)**  
What it is: A federal tax credit (currently 30% under the IRA) for a percentage of the total solar system cost.

CRITICAL COMPLIANCE: Salereps are NOT CPAs. You cannot guarantee a homeowner will receive the credit or tell them how to file it. You MUST use phrasing like:
- \

**Section 3: Product Matchmaking**  
Cash Purchase:
- Best for: Homeowners with liquidity, looking for maximum long-term ROI. They keep the ITC.

Solar Loan:
- Best for: Homeowners who want ownership and the ITC, but want to leverage financing to achieve a Day-1 low monthly payment.
- Keep in mind: Dealer fees mean the financed price is higher than the cash price to buy down the interest rate.

Lease / PPA (Third-Party Ownership):
- Best for: Homeowners on fixed incomes, retirees without tax liability (cannot use the ITC), or those who just want the absolute lowest monthly rate with zero maintenance responsibility.
- The financing company keeps the ITC and passes the savings down via a lower monthly rate.

**Module 1.5b Slide Deck**  
Introduce the concept of matching the product to the homeowner.

**The 'Swap Your Bill' Concept**  
Explain replacing a forever utility bill with a fixed payment.

**Federal ITC Compliance**  
Crucial compliance boundaries: Never give tax advice.

**Option 1: Cash Purchase**  
Maximum ROI, maximum upfront cost.

**Option 2: Solar Loans & Dealer Fees**  
Ownership without the upfront cost. Explain the 18-month re-amortization.

**Option 3: Third Party (Lease / PPA)**  
Best for those who cannot utilize the ITC or want zero maintenance liability.

**Introduction (List)**
- Explain the core mechanics of a Cash purchase, Solar Loan, Solar Lease, and Power Purchase Agreement (PPA).
- Identify the ideal homeowner profile for each financial product.
- Explain the Federal Solar Investment Tax Credit (ITC) accurately, without giving illegal tax advice.
- Understand the difference between 'dealer fees' (buy down rates) and the 'cash price' of a system.
- Position the 'Swap Your Bill' concept effectively.

### Module 1.6: Identity Shift: Consultant vs. Salesperson
- **ID:** mod_1_6
- **Subtitle:** Reframe the role Build integrity-first mindset Prevent burnout from rejection
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Salesperson vs. Consultant**  
There's a fundamental difference between a salesperson and a consultant. A salesperson pushes a product and chases a commission. A consultant diagnoses a problem and prescribes the right solution \u2014 even if that solution is walking away. In solar, this distinction matters because homeowners can feel which one you are within sixty seconds of opening the door. Your job is to enter every interaction as a consultant. You are restructuring someone's utility spending and protecting their family from inflation. Act accordingly.

**The Integrity Creed**  
The integrity creed is not optional \u2014 it's the foundation of a sustainable career. Never promise savings you can't guarantee. Never exaggerate return on investment. Disqualify aggressively \u2014 a bad fit today is a cancellation tomorrow, and cancellations hurt your income. And always remember that your long-term reputation is worth more than any single commission check. The reps who build six-figure incomes are the ones homeowners refer to their neighbors because they were treated with respect.

**Reframing Rejection**  
In solar sales, you will hear the word no more than any other word. Every single top performer in this industry hears no over a hundred times per week. The shift you need to make is this \u2014 no does not mean I reject you. It means not right now, or this isn't a fit. Both of those are useful information. Track your no's because they're the path to your yes's. A rep knocking forty-five doors statistically closes one deal. That means every door \u2014 including every no \u2014 is worth about seventy-eight dollars.

**Energy Management**  
This career requires emotional energy, and you have to manage it intentionally. Don't take rejection personally \u2014 the homeowner doesn't know you, and they're not rejecting you. They're protecting their time and their home. Celebrate small wins: an appointment set is a win, even if no deal follows. A meaningful conversation is a win. Reset between every door. The rep who treats door forty-five with the same energy as door one becomes the rep closing at a rate that changes everything.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Identity Shift: Consultant vs. Salesperson: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 1.7: Utility Bill Mastery: Introduction
- **ID:** mod_1_7
- **Subtitle:** Read and interpret a utility bill Identify qualified homeowners instantly Understand offset percentages
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Anatomy of a Utility Bill**  
The utility bill is your most powerful qualifying tool. Here's what to look for. The total monthly kilowatt-hour usage \u2014 this tells you how much power the home consumes. The total charges, broken into supply, delivery, and fees. The rate structure \u2014 is it tiered, flat, or time-of-use? And the twelve-month usage history, which tells you whether this is a seasonal household or a consistent consumer. You should be able to read a utility bill fluently in under two minutes.

**Qualification Thresholds**  
Not every homeowner is a solar candidate. The minimum thresholds you're looking for are six hundred kilowatt-hours of monthly usage, a monthly bill of at least one hundred dollars, and annual usage above eight thousand kilowatt-hours. Below these numbers, the system size becomes too small to justify the cost and financing. Qualifying hard early saves you hours of wasted presentations and protects your close rate.

**Red Flags on a Bill**  
Some things on a utility bill should make you pause. A past-due balance can signal credit issues that will affect financing. Budget billing hides the homeowner's true usage by averaging it out \u2014 always ask for the actual usage breakdown. And if the usage seems very low for the size of the home, dig deeper. It could mean they rent part of the home, they're planning to move, or another party controls the usage. Look for these before investing two hours in a presentation.

**Understanding Offset Percentage**  
Offset percentage is how much of a homeowner's energy usage the solar system will produce annually. A one hundred percent offset means the system produces exactly what the home uses in a year. Most consultants recommend sizing at one hundred five to one hundred ten percent to account for panel degradation and future usage increases. If you size at eighty percent offset, the homeowner still has a meaningful utility bill. You'll use offset percentage in every proposal conversation from Day Four forward.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Utility Bill Mastery: Introduction: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

### Module 1.7a: Basic Site Engineering Awareness

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 1.7A — Basic Site Engineering Awareness

**Insert after Module 1.5B | Day 1 — Onboarding & Foundation**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Reps are often the first set of eyes on a potential solar installation. They visit the home before the site survey team does. They see the roof, the electrical panel, the attic access, the shading conditions. Module 1.7A trains reps to look at a site with informed awareness — to recognize red flags that could affect the project timeline, cost, or feasibility, and to know when to set expectations vs. when to escalate.

### Why It Matters
The single most common source of post-close surprises in the solar pipeline is a site issue that could have been identified — or at least anticipated — during the sales visit. Main panel upgrades, roof condition problems, shading issues, and structural constraints don't appear from nowhere. They're often visible to a trained eye. Reps who notice these things early protect their deals, protect their homeowners, and protect their own credibility.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module completes the Day 1 technical foundation block (1.5A → 1.5B → 1.7A) and directly prevents the post-sale pipeline issues covered in Module 5.5B. It also sets up the site survey expectations taught in Module 3.7B. Reps who develop site engineering awareness early in their training will have significantly fewer MPU surprises, roof condition escalations, and redesign delays.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Assess a roof's basic solar suitability based on orientation, tilt, shading, and available space.
2. Identify the difference between 100-amp and 200-amp electrical service and explain why it matters for solar installations.
3. Recognize common electrical panel red flags that may indicate an upgrade is needed.
4. Understand basic conduit path considerations and how they can affect installation complexity and cost.
5. Know when to set homeowner expectations vs. when to escalate to the site survey team — without guessing or making promises.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — Reading the Roof: What Reps Can Observe
A rep doesn't need to climb on a roof to assess its solar suitability. Most relevant information is visible from the street or yard.

**Roof planes:**
- A roof plane is a single, flat surface section of the roof
- Most residential roofs have multiple planes facing different directions
- Solar panels should only be proposed for planes with adequate sun exposure (typically south, southeast, or southwest facing in the northern hemisphere)
- Reps should identify which planes are south-facing during the sales visit

**Azimuth (orientation):**
- Azimuth is the compass direction a roof plane faces
- True south = 180° azimuth = maximum annual production
- Southeast (135°) and southwest (225°) produce meaningfully less but are viable
- East (90°) and west (270°) produce notably less — and should be designed and estimated accordingly
- North-facing planes are typically not used for solar
- **Rep field habit:** Use a compass app on your phone to quickly confirm south-facing orientation during the site visit

**Tilt (pitch):**
- Standard residential roof pitches range from about 15° to 45°
- Most are well within the workable range for solar
- Very flat roofs (under ~10°) may require tilt mounting systems
- Very steep roofs (over ~45°) can create safety and equipment access challenges
- **Rep observation:** If the roof looks unusually steep or flat, note it and let the survey team confirm implications

**Available roof space:**
- Panels require clear, obstacle-free roof space
- Typical obstacles that reduce available space: skylights, vents, HVAC equipment, chimneys, dormers, satellite dishes, solar tubes
- Building codes require setbacks from roof edges (typically 3 feet from each edge in most jurisdictions) and from ridge lines
- A roof that looks large from the street may have far less usable panel space than expected once setbacks and obstacles are accounted for

**Roof age and condition:**
- Panels are designed to last 25+ years — ideally the roof underneath should not need replacement during that period
- Signs of aging or damage that a rep might observe: missing or curling shingles, moss or algae growth, sagging sections, evidence of previous repairs or patching
- **Rep guidance:** If a roof shows clear signs of significant age or damage, set expectations that the site survey will assess roof condition before confirming installation — do not make promises about proceeding on a roof you haven't verified

### Section 2 — 100A vs. 200A Service: The Most Important Electrical Distinction for Reps
This single distinction can save or cost deals. Reps who understand it can set expectations proactively; reps who don't are caught off guard by MPU cost surprises.

**What electrical service amperage means:**
- The electrical service to a home is rated by how much current (amperage) it can safely carry
- Older homes: often have 100-amp (100A) service panels
- Modern homes: typically have 200-amp (200A) service panels
- Very large or newer homes: may have 400-amp service

**Why it matters for solar:**
- Most solar installations — particularly those with batteries — require 200-amp service to safely accommodate the additional electrical load
- If a home has 100-amp service, a Main Panel Upgrade (MPU) is typically required before the solar system can be installed
- MPUs add cost (typically $1,500–$5,000+ depending on market and scope) and time (typically 2–6 additional weeks for permitting and utility coordination)
- If an MPU is a near-certainty and the rep doesn't flag it, the homeowner will feel blindsided when operations discovers it during the site survey

**How to identify panel amperage without being an electrician:**
- The main breaker in the electrical panel is labeled with the amperage rating — look for "100A," "150A," or "200A" printed on the breaker
- The service entrance cable size can also indicate amperage — but reading wire gauge is less reliable for non-electricians
- **Rep field habit:** When possible, briefly look at the main electrical panel during the sales visit. If the main breaker says 100A, mentally note this for expectation-setting
- When in doubt: flag it for the survey team, do not guess

**What to say to the homeowner:**
"I noticed your panel may be 100-amp service — I want to make sure we flag this for our site survey team because depending on the system size, an upgrade may be needed. It's something they'll confirm during the survey, and I'd rather you know about it now than be surprised later."

### Section 3 — Breaker Space Awareness
Even within a 200-amp panel, available breaker space matters for solar installation.

**What breaker space means:**
- The electrical panel has a fixed number of breaker slots
- Solar installations require additional breakers (for the system interconnection)
- Battery systems require additional breakers as well
- If the panel is full or near-full, a panel expansion or sub-panel may be needed — adding cost and complexity

**What reps can observe:**
- An open panel (if the homeowner permits a quick look) will show whether there are open breaker slots or whether the panel is packed full
- A panel that is completely full with every slot occupied is a potential flag
- **Rep guidance:** Note whether the panel appears to have open breaker space. Don't make a technical determination — flag it for the survey team

**Simple rep awareness:** "Is there room in the panel?" is not a question reps need to answer definitively. It is a question reps need to know to ask — and to make sure the survey team addresses.

### Section 4 — Conduit Path Considerations
Conduit is the protected pathway that solar system wiring runs through from the panels (on the roof) to the inverter and electrical panel (usually in the garage or utility area).

**Why conduit path matters:**
- A simple, short conduit path (e.g., panels on a roof directly above the garage where the inverter will be installed) = low complexity, lower installation cost
- A complex conduit path (e.g., panels on the front of the house, inverter in the garage on the opposite side, with multiple floors and finished walls in between) = higher complexity, potentially higher cost and longer installation time
- Exposed conduit on the exterior of a home can be a visual concern for some homeowners — HOAs in some areas restrict exterior conduit runs

**What reps can observe:**
- Where are the solar panels likely to be installed? (Which roof plane)
- Where is the main electrical panel? (Which side of the home, which floor)
- What is the path from one to the other? (Is it direct through an attic? Through finished living space? Along the exterior?)
- **Rep guidance:** If the conduit path is clearly complex (front-of-house panels, rear-of-house panel, two-story home with finished interior), note it. Flag it for the site survey team and set the expectation that installation complexity may affect the timeline.

### Section 5 — Detached Structures and Subpanels
Some homeowners want to include detached structures — garages, workshops, accessory dwelling units — in their solar project.

**Relevant considerations:**
- A detached structure typically requires its own electrical subpanel to receive solar-generated power
- Running conduit and wiring from the main home to a detached structure adds cost and complexity
- Some jurisdictions require separate permits for detached structure electrical work
- HOAs may restrict exterior wiring between structures

**Rep guidance:**
- If a homeowner mentions a detached garage or workshop during the conversation, ask whether they want that structure included in the solar project
- Note the distance and path between the main home and the detached structure
- Flag it for the site survey and design team — do not quote expanded project scope without design team input

### Section 6 — When to Escalate Instead of Guessing
The most important skill this module develops is knowing the boundary between observation and assessment. Reps observe; engineers and surveyors assess.

**Always flag to the survey team (do not make determinations):**
- Any sign of significant roof damage or unusual age
- 100-amp service panels
- Full or near-full electrical panels
- Complex or unusual conduit paths
- Detached structures the homeowner wants to include
- Any structural concern about the roof (sagging, unusual deflection)

**Set homeowner expectations proactively when you observe flags:**
- "The site survey team will take a closer look at [specific item] — I want to make sure we're accurate on the design before we finalize everything."
- "I noticed [specific flag]. I want to flag this for our survey team because it may affect the project scope or timeline. Better to know now than later."

**Never guess or make commitments on:**
- Whether a roof can structurally support panels
- Exact MPU cost or timeline
- Whether specific shading will reduce production by a specific percentage
- Whether a panel is definitively 100A or 200A if you cannot see the main breaker label clearly

**Teaching point:** The rep who spots a flag and says "I want to make sure our survey team takes a look at that" is more trustworthy than the rep who gives a confident answer they're not qualified to give. Homeowners respect honesty about the boundaries of expertise.

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The 100A vs. 200A panel distinction is the single highest-value item in this module. Reps who can identify a 100A panel during a sales visit and proactively set expectations have just protected themselves from one of the most common post-close trust-breakers.
- The "when to escalate instead of guessing" section should be the closing emphasis of the module. Reps need permission to say "I want to make sure our survey team looks at that" rather than guessing. Give them that permission explicitly.
- This module should ideally include photos or real examples from your market. Generic descriptions of roof conditions or panel types are less memorable than actual photos from real installs.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "I'm not an electrician, so I shouldn't look at the panel." → Reps don't need to assess the panel — they just need to note the main breaker amperage label. That's a visual observation, not an electrical assessment.
- "If I flag a potential issue, the homeowner will get cold feet." → The opposite is more often true. Homeowners who learn about potential issues from the rep — before the survey — feel informed and respected. Homeowners who learn about them from operations — after signing — feel blindsided.
- "The design team will catch everything at the survey anyway." → True, but the survey happens after signing. Post-signing discoveries are much more disruptive than pre-signing awareness.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Reps do not need to learn to read structural engineering specs or electrical diagrams.
- Azimuth math (exact degree calculations) is not necessary — directional awareness (south vs. east vs. west) is sufficient.
- Don't turn this into a roofing module. The rep's job is to notice, not to diagnose.

**Where to slow down:**
- The 100A vs. 200A distinction. Have reps look at photos of electrical panel main breakers with different amperage ratings. Make sure they can identify the relevant information visually before moving on.
- The "flag vs. guess" boundary. Have reps practice saying the flagging language aloud until it sounds confident and natural — not hesitant.

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "The 100-Amp Panel Discovery"
**Homeowner type:** Eager to move forward, not aware of the panel issue
**Setup:** During a sales visit, the rep notices the main panel is clearly labeled 100A. The homeowner hasn't asked about it. The rep is about to present the proposal, which includes a battery.
**Skill being tested:** Proactively flagging the panel issue to the homeowner in a way that is honest, calm, and doesn't kill the deal — while setting accurate expectations about potential MPU cost and timeline.
**Pass criteria:** Rep raises the panel observation naturally and proactively (rather than waiting for operations to discover it), explains what it may mean without guaranteeing specific cost or timeline, frames it as "something our survey team will confirm," and maintains the homeowner's confidence in the process.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The Shaded Roof"
**Homeowner type:** Enthusiastic about solar, roof has obvious shading from mature trees
**Setup:** The rep is doing a site visit. The homeowner's south-facing roof has two large mature trees casting afternoon shade across approximately 40% of the primary roof plane. The homeowner hasn't mentioned this as a concern.
**Skill being tested:** Observing and flagging a significant shading issue proactively — without killing the homeowner's enthusiasm or making a production estimate the rep isn't qualified to make.
**Pass criteria:** Rep identifies the shading concern, explains why it matters in simple terms, notes that the site survey will include a detailed shade analysis, and sets an expectation that the system design may need to account for this — possibly through microinverters or a revised panel layout.

---

### Scenario 3 — "Should I Include My Detached Garage?"
**Homeowner type:** Enthusiastic, wants to maximize the system, asks about including a detached structure
**Setup:** The homeowner says: "We also have a detached garage out back — can we include that in the solar system? We run a woodworking shop out there and the electricity bills are high."
**Skill being tested:** Handling a scope expansion request with accurate awareness of the additional complexity — without shutting down the idea or making design commitments the rep isn't qualified to make.
**Pass criteria:** Rep acknowledges the question positively, explains that including a detached structure is possible but adds complexity (separate subpanel, conduit run, permitting considerations), notes the approximate distance from the main home, and commits to flagging it for the site survey and design team before adding it to the proposal.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
During a sales visit, a rep notices the main electrical panel's main breaker is labeled "100A." What is the most appropriate next action?
- A) Tell the homeowner the project cannot proceed without an upgrade
- B) Ignore it — the site survey team will figure it out
- C) Proactively flag it to the homeowner as something the site survey team will assess, explain it may affect project scope or cost, and note it in your visit summary
- D) Quote a specific MPU cost range to the homeowner so they can budget accordingly
*(Correct answer: C)*

**2. Short Answer**
A rep is at a sales visit and the homeowner's south-facing roof has two large mature trees casting significant afternoon shade on the primary roof plane. What should the rep observe, note, and say to the homeowner?

**3. Multiple Choice**
Which roof orientation produces the most annual solar energy in the northern hemisphere?
- A) East-facing
- B) West-facing
- C) North-facing
- D) South-facing
*(Correct answer: D)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner asks the rep: "Is my roof strong enough to hold the solar panels?" The rep has no structural engineering background. What should the rep say?
*(Expected answer: The rep should be honest — structural assessment is done by the site survey team, not the sales rep. Appropriate language: "Great question — our site survey team does a structural assessment as part of the process, so we'll have a clear answer after that visit. I haven't seen a roof in your area that couldn't support panels, but I want to make sure we verify it properly rather than guess.")*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
A home with _______ amp electrical service is at higher risk of requiring a Main Panel Upgrade before solar installation, which can add approximately $_______ to $_______ in cost and _______ to _______ weeks of additional timeline.
*(Correct: 100; 1,500; 5,000+; 2; 6 — note to trainers: localize cost figures to your market)*

## 8. Slide Deck Outline

1. **Title Slide** — "Basic Site Engineering Awareness: See More, Promise Less, Close Better"
2. **Why Site Awareness Matters** — The most common source of post-close surprises is visible at the sales visit
3. **Reading the Roof: Orientation** — Azimuth and why south-facing maximizes production; visual compass
4. **Reading the Roof: Tilt** — Workable range; flat vs. steep outliers
5. **Reading the Roof: Available Space** — Setbacks, obstacles, and how they shrink usable roof area
6. **Reading the Roof: Condition** — Signs of aging or damage reps can observe from the ground
7. **Shading: The #1 Production Variable** — Common shade sources; why shade analysis is critical
8. **100A vs. 200A Service: The Most Important Distinction** — What to look for; what it means; what to say
9. **Breaker Space Awareness** — Why panel fullness matters; what reps can observe
10. **Conduit Path Considerations** — Simple vs. complex paths; what to note
11. **Detached Structures & Subpanels** — What it means to include them; what to flag
12. **Flag vs. Guess: The Rep's Most Important Skill** — What to observe, what to say, what NOT to determine
13. **Field Observation Checklist** — Printable rep tool
14. **Key Takeaways + Module Recap**

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"Here's a question. How many of you have had a deal hit an MPU surprise after the close — where the homeowner found out they needed a panel upgrade and nobody had mentioned it before? [Pause for hands.] And how many of you, if you had known to look, could have seen that the panel was 100-amp during the sales visit? [Let that land.] That's what this module is about. Not turning you into electricians. Teaching you what to look for and what to say when you see it."

**Concept Explanation (15–18 min)**
Walk through the roof assessment section using real photos from your market if possible — shaded roofs, different orientations, different conditions. Then move into the electrical panel section. Bring a printed photo of a 100A and a 200A main breaker label side by side. Make the visual recognition concrete. Cover conduit paths and detached structures briefly.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's the scenario. You're at a sales visit. You walk into the garage and notice the main panel. The main breaker says 100A. The homeowner hasn't brought it up. You have two choices: flag it now, or let it go and hope the survey team catches it. If you let it go and the homeowner signs — then six weeks later operations calls them with a $3,500 MPU surprise — whose name is on that conversation? Yours. Here's what you say instead: [walk through the flagging language]. That one sentence protects the deal, protects the relationship, and protects your credibility."

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 1.7A-4 (Field Checklist Build) — have reps build their own field observation checklist from memory, then compare with a partner. Combine the best items into a shared checklist. This becomes a tool reps actually keep and use.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"You are the first set of eyes on every site. Before the survey team gets there, before the engineers look at the satellite imagery, before anyone pulls a permit — you were there. What you notice, what you flag, and what you say determines whether this homeowner is protected or surprised. That's not a small responsibility. That's what separates a rep who builds a business from a rep who just closes deals."

#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Introduction**  
The most common source of post-close friction is a site condition discovered at survey that the rep should have seen at the sales visit. Module 1.7A teaches reps 'Site Engineering Awareness' \u2014 not how to be an electrician or structural engineer, but how to observe key indicators on the roof and electrical panel to surface red flags early.

**Learning Objectives**  
Orientation (Azimuth): South-facing roofs produce the most energy. East/West are viable but produce less. North-facing roofs are typically unacceptable in the Northern Hemisphere.

Tilt/Pitch: Very steep roofs or completely flat roofs require special mounting hardware and may have safety implications for the install crew.

Condition & Shading: Are there curling shingles? Heavy moss? Is there a mature oak tree covering the southern roof face in shadow? Shading is the #1 variable that alters the production estimate.

**Section 2: The Electrical Panel**  
100A vs. 200A Service: The most critical observation. A 100-amp panel often requires a Main Panel Upgrade (MPU) to handle the backfeed of a modern solar array. 

What to look for: The number stamped on the main breaker. 

Breaker Space: Even a 200A panel can be a problem if every single slot is completely full (requires a subpanel or line-side tap).

**Section 3: The 'Flag vs. Guess' Boundary**  
The highest-risk mistake a rep can make is guessing how much an upgrade will cost or promising it won't be needed.

Correct Flagging Language: \

**Module 1.7a Slide Deck**  
Introduce the concept: 'See more, promise less, close better.'

**Reading the Roof**  
Discuss Azimuth (South = Best), Tilt, and Shading.

**Electrical Panel Awareness**  
Focus on identifying 100A vs 200A service.

**The Flag vs. Guess Boundary**  
Teach the exact scripting for pointing out an issue without quoting a cost or making a promise.

**Field Observation Checklist**  
Review the items every rep should check before leaving the property.

**Introduction (List)**
- Identify the optimal roof orientations (azimuth) for solar production and explain why it matters.
- Visually identify roof shading issues and understand how they impact production estimates.
- Distinguish visually between a 100A and 200A main electrical panel.
- Understand the 'Flag vs. Guess' boundary: flagging potential issues for the survey team without diagnosing or quoting costs prematurely.
- Create a personal pre-survey field observation checklist.
