### Module 1.8: Day 1 Wrap-Up & Preview
- **ID:** mod_1_8
- **Subtitle:** missing_from_source
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Day 1 Key Takeaways**  
Let's lock in what you covered today. You are administratively onboarded and system-ready. You understand how solar works at a technical level that builds confidence. You can read a utility bill and identify a qualified lead. And you've adopted the mindset of a consultant, not a salesperson. These are not small things. Most reps start their first week without this foundation. You're already ahead.

**Tonight's Homework**  
Before tomorrow, complete three assignments. Watch two short videos on net metering \u2014 links are in your training portal. Read the top objection handling guide your company has provided \u2014 it's five pages, read it fully. And practice explaining how solar works to someone at home. Record yourself on your phone. Listen back. You'll hear what needs tightening. This kind of self-review is one of the fastest ways to improve.

**Tomorrow's Preview**  
Tomorrow is one of the most important days in the program. You'll learn homeowner psychology \u2014 why people resist solar, and what to do about it. You'll build your territory strategy. You'll learn the Solar Conversation Framework, which is the backbone of every interaction you'll ever have. And you'll practice micro objection handling \u2014 the skill that separates reps who get appointments from those who get doors closed in their face. Come ready to work.

## DAY 2: Prospecting & Territory Domination
### Module 2.1: Homeowner Psychology: Why People Resist Solar
- **ID:** mod_2_1
- **Subtitle:** Understand the psychological barriers homeowners bring to every solar conversation Recognize resistance as normal human 
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Why Understanding Psychology Matters**  
Before you can persuade anyone of anything, you need to understand how they think. The most common mistake in solar sales is treating every homeowner like they simply need more information. They don't. They need trust, time, and the right framing. This module will change how you walk up to every door. Reps who understand why homeowners resist close twice as many deals as those who only know what to say.

**Status Quo Bias**  
Homeowners are not actively looking for a solar salesperson. When you show up at their door, their brain's default mode is to preserve the status quo. Change feels risky. Inaction feels safe \u2014 even when the current situation is objectively worse. This is called status quo bias. Your job isn't to push homeowners toward a decision. It's to guide them toward understanding, and let the decision emerge naturally from the numbers.

**Contractor Trust Barriers**  
Most homeowners have been let down by a contractor before. A roofer who disappeared after the deposit. An HVAC company that overcharged. A home warranty that covered nothing. That history lives in the background of every conversation you have. The homeowner isn't just evaluating solar \u2014 they're evaluating you as a person and whether you'll be different. Slow down. Don't push for information or commitment before you've earned it. Every genuine moment of rapport is a deposit in their trust account.

**Solar Industry Skepticism**  
The solar industry has earned some of its bad reputation. Aggressive door-to-door tactics, misleading savings promises, and companies that went bankrupt mid-install have made some homeowners deeply skeptical of any solar rep \u2014 regardless of how you present yourself. The solution is to acknowledge this proactively. Name the elephant in the room. Reps who say \u2014 I know solar reps have a certain reputation, and here's why I do things differently \u2014 build trust faster than those who pretend the skepticism doesn't exist.

**Loss Aversion**  
Nobel Prize-winning research shows that people feel the pain of a loss about twice as intensely as the pleasure of an equivalent gain. In practice this means: framing solar as preventing a loss is more powerful than framing it as gaining a benefit. A homeowner who could save two hundred dollars per month isn't moved by gaining two hundred dollars \u2014 they're moved by not losing two hundred dollars every month to a utility that doesn't deserve it. Use this framing. It works.

**Decision Fatigue**  
By the time you knock on a door at five pm, that homeowner may have made thousands of decisions that day. Decision fatigue makes people default to no \u2014 not because they don't want solar, but because saying no requires less mental effort than evaluating a proposal. Your response to this is to make every next step easy and low-risk. Keep your door pitch under sixty seconds. Don't ask for a big decision at the door \u2014 ask for a small one. An appointment. That's it.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Homeowner Psychology: Why People Resist Solar: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 2.2: Territory Strategy & Planning
- **ID:** mod_2_2
- **Subtitle:** Choose high-probability neighborhoods Leverage install proximity Build neighborhood authority
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Cloverleaf Strategy**  
The cloverleaf strategy is simple and powerful. You start from a recent solar install in a neighborhood and work outward in concentric circles. When you knock on the next door and say \u2014 your neighbor on the corner just went solar and I'm following up in the area \u2014 you immediately have social proof. The homeowner's first thought is: someone I know already did this. That transforms the conversation from cold to warm before you've said another word.

**Territory Selection Criteria**  
Not all neighborhoods are created equal. The sweet spot for solar is homes valued at two hundred fifty thousand dollars or more, with roofs between five and fifteen years old, served by a utility with strong net metering policies, and in HOA-friendly areas. Avoid co-op utilities if your area has poor net metering agreements. Use Zillow, county records, and your company's install database to pre-qualify neighborhoods before you spend a single golden hour in them.

**Building Neighborhood Authority**  
The goal is to saturate one area before moving to another. Become the solar rep in that zip code. When multiple neighbors have seen you, talked about you, or seen your yard signs \u2014 the next conversation opens at a much higher trust level. Some advanced reps host neighborhood info sessions once they have three or more installs on a block. That kind of neighborhood authority is what eliminates the need for cold prospecting over time.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Territory Strategy & Planning: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

### Module 2.3: Daily Efficiency Math & Time Management
- **ID:** mod_2_3
- **Subtitle:** Understand revenue per hour Maximize golden hours Eliminate time waste
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Math Behind the Door**  
Let's run the math on a typical door-knocking day. Industry averages show one conversation per three doors knocked, one appointment per five conversations, and one close per three appointments. That means forty-five doors equals one close on average. At an average commission of thirty-five hundred dollars, and five golden hours per day, that's roughly three hundred eighty-nine dollars per hour worked. Every door you knock \u2014 whether they answer or not \u2014 is worth about seventy-eight dollars. That reframe makes rejection a lot easier to process.

**Daily Non-Negotiables**  
Your golden hours \u2014 two pm to seven pm \u2014 are for one thing only: prospecting. No admin. No errands. No long phone calls. During these five hours, your only job is to knock doors or dial numbers, set appointments, and advance conversations. The reps who protect this window build income. The reps who let it get eaten by distractions wonder why they're not closing. Set a minimum of two appointments per golden-hour session. Anything less and your pipeline will run dry within two weeks.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*

### Module 2.4: The Solar Conversation Framework: Introduction
- **ID:** mod_2_4
- **Subtitle:** missing_from_source
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The 7-Step Framework Overview**  
Every solar conversation you'll ever have \u2014 at the door, on the phone, or inside a home \u2014 follows the same seven-step arc. Step one: Pattern Interrupt. Step two: Rapport. Step three: Curiosity Question. Step four: Problem Awareness. Step five: Utility Bill Discovery. Step six: Qualification. Step seven: Appointment or next step. You may move through some steps faster than others depending on the homeowner, but you never skip a step. This framework is your GPS. Use it every single time.

**Framework at the Door \u2014 60 Seconds**  
At the door, you compress the full framework into sixty to ninety seconds. Break their autopilot with a neighbor reference or something unexpected \u2014 that's your Pattern Interrupt. One human moment before business \u2014 that's Rapport. Ask a simple curiosity question about their energy situation. Help them see a problem they've been tolerating. Ask about their monthly electric bill \u2014 that's your Discovery. Confirm they own the home and how long they've lived there \u2014 that's Qualification. Then close on a specific appointment time. Sixty seconds. Seven steps. Done.

### Module 2.5: Door Knocking Mastery
- **ID:** mod_2_5
- **Subtitle:** Overcome door anxiety Master the 30-second pitch Set appointments efficiently
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Pre-Knock Preparation**  
What happens before you knock matters as much as what you say at the door. Appearance: clean, professional, name badge visible. Materials: your tablet charged, business cards in your pocket, and any one-pager you use. And mindset: before you ever raise your hand to knock, reset. Expect that most people won't be interested. Celebrate the conversations, not just the appointments. The rep who walks to each door with calm confidence closes more than the rep who walks up hoping for a yes.

**The 30-Second Opener \u2014 3 Variations**  
You have three proven openers. The Neighbor Reference opener leverages a nearby install to create social proof immediately. The Utility Bill Audit opener positions you as someone doing free energy assessments in the area \u2014 low threat, high curiosity. The Install Proximity opener works when you have active job sites nearby \u2014 you're introducing yourself as part of a crew already on the street. Memorize all three. Use whichever fits the neighborhood and the moment.

**Appointment Setting Close**  
When you're ready to close on an appointment, don't ask if they want one. Give them a choice between two times. I have Thursday at six or Saturday at ten \u2014 which works better for you? This is called an assumptive binary close. You're not asking if they want an appointment. You're assuming they do and asking which time works. This small language shift increases appointment conversion dramatically. Practice it until it feels natural.

*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Door Knocking Mastery: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

### Module 2.6: Micro Objection Handling: Getting Past the Brush-Off
- **ID:** mod_2_6
- **Subtitle:** missing_from_source
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**What Micro Objections Really Are**  
Most objections at the door are not real objections. They are automatic, reflexive responses designed to end the interaction as quickly as possible. The homeowner isn't thinking deeply about solar \u2014 they're trying to get back to their life. Your response should never be to argue or defend. The golden rule of micro objections is this: don't argue, don't defend \u2014 ask one curious question and invite them back into the conversation.

**Not Interested \u2014 The Response**  
When someone says not interested, respond with curiosity, not defense. Try: Totally fair \u2014 quick question, have you already looked into solar, or just haven't gotten around to it yet? If they haven't looked, that opens the door to explain why most people in the area qualify. If they already looked, ask what stopped them from moving forward. That one question reopens the entire conversation. Never argue with not interested. Make it a question.

**I'm Busy, Leave Info, Already Have Solar**  
Three more common ones. I'm busy right now \u2014 tell them you're not pitching today, you're just booking free consultations. Two minutes to pick a time, then you do all the prep work. Just leave me some info \u2014 the info won't mean anything without their specific numbers. Redirect to booking. Already have solar \u2014 lead with curiosity, ask how it's working out, and pivot to battery storage upgrades or referrals. Every brush-off has a response. Know yours before you knock.

**Spouse, Renting, and Bad Experience**  
Need to talk to my spouse \u2014 validate it immediately and ask if the spouse is home now. If not, find a time when both are available. I don't like presenting to one spouse because it just doesn't make sense any other way. We're renting \u2014 solar is for homeowners, but pivot to referrals. Do they know neighbors who own? Bad experience \u2014 lead with full empathy. Ask what happened. Listen completely. Then ask if they'd be open to fifteen minutes just to see if things have changed.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Micro Objection Handling: Getting Past the Brush-Off: How will you apply this?

### Module 2.7: Phone & Virtual Appointment Setting
- **ID:** mod_2_7
- **Subtitle:** Convert inbound leads to appointments Handle cold calls professionally Master tonality & pacing
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Speed to Lead**  
When an inbound lead comes in, your response time is everything. Call within five minutes. If they don't answer, leave a voicemail and send a text simultaneously \u2014 multi-touch. Research shows lead conversion drops dramatically after the first thirty minutes. The homeowner submitted a form while they were curious and motivated. Your job is to reach them while that energy is still there. Every minute you wait, the window closes.

**Phone Discovery & Appointment Set**  
When you reach them, open with energy and reference their inquiry. Then move into three to five discovery questions: What's your average electric bill? How long have you been in the home? Have you looked into solar before? Is your spouse involved in decisions? From there, transition directly to booking. Tell them you'll pull their utility data and roof layout before you meet, so the call is fully customized. Then give two specific time options and lock them in.

### Module 2.8: Anti-Sales Framing & Trust Building
- **ID:** mod_2_8
- **Subtitle:** Position as consultant, not salesperson Use disarming language Build instant rapport
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Anti-Pitch Opener**  
The fastest way to lower a homeowner's guard is to say something they didn't expect from a solar rep. Try: I'm not here to sell you anything today. I'm just here to see if it makes sense. That one sentence removes the pressure they were bracing for. It shifts the dynamic from adversarial to collaborative. Follow it immediately with permission-based questions: Is it okay if I ask you a few things first? Mind if I pull up your roof real quick? You're building micro-agreements before you've asked for anything significant.

**Disqualification Framing**  
One of the most powerful things you can say early in a conversation is: Honestly, solar doesn't make sense for everyone. Let me first figure out if you're even a fit. This is disqualification framing, and it works through reverse psychology. By suggesting they might not qualify, you activate the homeowner's desire to qualify. You've created a challenge they now want to pass. You've also pre-positioned yourself as selective \u2014 and selective consultants are trusted more than desperate salespeople.

## DAY 3: Discovery, Psychology & In-Home Mastery
### Module 3.1: In-Home Positioning & Environmental Control
- **ID:** mod_3_1
- **Subtitle:** Control the appointment setting Build subconscious authority Eliminate distractions
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Pre-Arrival Checklist**  
Before you walk in the door, do three things. Confirm the appointment two hours before by text and call. Arrive five minutes early and park in front of the home. From your car, assess the roof \u2014 look for shading, pitch, and condition before you ever ring the doorbell. This recon saves you time inside and lets you ask smarter questions. Walking in prepared is not optional \u2014 it's the standard of a professional consultant.

**The Power Seat Strategy**  
Where you sit in the home matters more than most reps realize. Your goal is the kitchen or dining table \u2014 not the couch. At the table, position yourself at the head or corner. Homeowners sit to your side, which creates a collaborative dynamic instead of an adversarial face-off. Ask them to face away from the TV. Request that pets are settled and kids are occupied. These aren't rude requests \u2014 they're professional ones. Your opening frame should be: I'm going to ask a lot of questions first, then show you numbers. If it makes sense, great. If not, I'll tell you.

#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS

#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 3.2: Personality Profiling
- **ID:** mod_3_2
- **Subtitle:** Identify personality type in 2 minutes Adapt communication style Avoid mismatches that kill deals
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The BOLT Personality Types**  
The BOLT framework gives you four personality types to quickly identify and adapt to. Bull \u2014 dominant, direct, fast. They want the bottom line immediately. Owl \u2014 analytical, detail-oriented, skeptical. They want data, warranties, and specs. Lion \u2014 enthusiastic, social, emotional. They want to connect personally and feel good about the decision. Turtle \u2014 steady, risk-averse, hesitant. They need social proof, safety, and zero pressure. You'll typically know which type someone is within two minutes of entering their home.

**Adapting to Each Type**  
For the Bull \u2014 skip the small talk, show the ROI fast, get to the close. For the Owl \u2014 bring data, show the degradation curve, quote specific warranties and engineering reports. For the Lion \u2014 ask about their family, make the conversation personal, celebrate the environmental and legacy impact. For the Turtle \u2014 move slowly, emphasize how many neighbors have done this, remove any feeling of pressure, and let them feel they're in control of the pace. Most couples are a mix \u2014 adapt to both, starting with whoever is more resistant.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Personality Profiling: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 3.4: Tactical Empathy & Mirroring
- **ID:** mod_3_4
- **Subtitle:** Build deep rapport instantly Make homeowners feel heard Uncover hidden objections
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Mirroring \u2014 Repeat the Last Three Words**  
Mirroring is one of the most powerful rapport-building tools that exists. When a homeowner says something \u2014 worried about the cost \u2014 you simply repeat their last two or three words as a question. Worried about the cost? They'll naturally elaborate. You haven't said anything \u2014 you've just opened a door. This technique forces them to say more than they planned to, and in doing so they often reveal the real concern underneath the surface one.

**Labeling & Calibrated Questions**  
Labeling means naming the emotion you're observing. It sounds like you're frustrated with your utility company. It seems like you're concerned about the commitment. When you name emotions accurately, homeowners feel heard \u2014 and when people feel heard, they trust. Pair labeling with calibrated questions \u2014 open-ended questions that start with what or how. What would saving a hundred dollars a month allow you to do? What's making you consider this now? These questions build motivation instead of putting homeowners on the defensive.

### Module 3.5: Question Architecture: The Discovery Sequence
- **ID:** mod_3_5
- **Subtitle:** Uncover true pain points Build urgency Qualify financial readiness
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Questions About Current State**  
The first four discovery questions focus on the homeowner's current situation. What is your average electric bill? Has it been going up the last few years \u2014 they almost always say yes. What is the most you've ever paid in a single month \u2014 this anchors a high number in their mind. And what do you hate most about your utility company \u2014 this is an emotional trigger that surfaces frustration they've been carrying for years. These questions don't just qualify the lead \u2014 they build the emotional foundation for your presentation.

**Questions About Future State & Decision-Making**  
The next set of questions looks forward and qualifies the decision. What happens if rates go up another six percent next year? How long are you planning to stay in this home? Are you thinking about an electric vehicle? These build urgency and future-proof the conversation. Then move to decision-making questions. Who else needs to be part of this decision \u2014 identify blockers early. If the numbers make sense, is there any reason you wouldn't move forward \u2014 smoke out hidden objections before you've built the proposal. And confirm credit range and timeline. These twelve questions are your entire pre-presentation checklist.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Question Architecture: The Discovery Sequence: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 3.6: Spouse & Decision-Maker Dynamics
- **ID:** mod_3_6
- **Subtitle:** Prevent \
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Golden Rule \u2014 Never Present to One Spouse**  
This rule has one exception: a confirmed single decision-maker. If a spouse is not home, do not present. Reschedule. Presenting to one spouse and closing without the other is the number one cause of next-day cancellations. The logic is simple: the absent spouse hears a summary with missing information, gets concerned, and kills the deal. The rep who insisted on presenting to one spouse caused that cancellation. When in doubt, say: I don't like to move forward unless both of you are there \u2014 when's a good time for both?

**Equalizing Both Spouses**  
Throughout the presentation, make eye contact with both homeowners equally. Ask both for input \u2014 and specifically direct questions to the quieter spouse. Does that make sense? \u2014 look at both. Sound good so far? \u2014 wait for both to nod. Any concerns? \u2014 let both voice objections. If one spouse is clearly skeptical, address them directly and early: I can tell you're the careful one \u2014 that's a good thing. What's your biggest concern? Handling skepticism head-on in front of their partner builds credibility with both of them.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Spouse & Decision-Maker Dynamics: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

### Module 3.7: Roof & Site Assessment
- **ID:** mod_3_7
- **Subtitle:** Spot deal-killers early Set realistic expectations Avoid costly site surveys for unqualified homes
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Roof Inspection Basics**  
During or before every appointment, walk the property and assess the roof. The four things you're evaluating are shading, condition, pitch, and material. Heavy shade from trees is a serious production killer \u2014 if it covers more than forty percent of the usable roof area, the system may not economically justify itself. A roof needing replacement within five years should be addressed before install \u2014 panels have to come off during a re-roof and that costs extra. And the ideal roof is south-facing between fifteen and forty degrees of pitch on asphalt shingles.

**Electrical Panel & HOA Considerations**  
Ask to see the electrical panel \u2014 usually in the garage or basement. Two hundred amp service is ideal. One hundred amp may require a main panel upgrade costing two to five thousand dollars \u2014 that affects your proposal. Red flag brands like Zinsco or Federal Pacific need replacement before solar and should be disclosed. For HOA properties, confirm solar is permitted and ask about any aesthetics restrictions. Most HOAs legally cannot deny solar under state laws, but they may control placement. Get this information before you finalize the design.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Roof & Site Assessment: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

---

### Module 3.8: Disqualification Mastery: When to Walk Away
- **ID:** mod_3_8
- **Subtitle:** Protect time and company resources Build trust by being honest Avoid future cancellations
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Top Disqualification Scenarios**  
Walking away from a bad deal is one of the highest-integrity moves a consultant can make \u2014 and it's also good business. The top scenarios where you disqualify: homeowner is moving within three years, credit score is below six hundred, the roof needs replacement first, heavy shading makes production unviable, usage is below four hundred kilowatt-hours per month, or the homeowner has unrealistic expectations like eliminating their bill to zero. In every case, be direct. Say the honest thing. Recommend the right path. Then ask to follow up when conditions change.

### Module 3.7a: Technical Discovery Questions

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 3.7A — Technical Discovery Questions

**Insert after existing Module 3.7 | Day 3 — Discovery & Site Awareness**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Most reps discover a homeowner's motivations. Fewer reps discover a homeowner's risks. Module 3.7A upgrades the rep's discovery process to surface technical and project-level concerns earlier in the conversation — before they become surprises in the pipeline. The goal is not to turn discovery into an interrogation or a technical audit. It is to weave smart questions into natural conversation so that reps arrive at the proposal stage with a clearer picture of what they're actually selling into.

### Why It Matters
Every pipeline surprise — the MPU that nobody flagged, the HOA that slowed the permit by six weeks, the roof that failed the survey — was discoverable at the sales visit. A rep who asks the right questions early is a rep who sets the right expectations, designs the right system, and closes deals that actually complete. Technical discovery is not a barrier to closing. Done well, it is a trust-builder.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module sits on Day 3 within the discovery block and connects directly backward to Module 1.7A (Site Engineering Awareness) and forward to Module 3.7B (Site Survey Prep for Reps). The questions taught here are the field application of the site awareness concepts introduced on Day 1. Reps who internalized Module 1.7A will immediately recognize why these questions matter.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Ask natural, non-intrusive questions about roof age, electrical service, and site conditions that surface potential project risks before the survey.
2. Identify homeowner signals that indicate HOA involvement, contractor history, or permitting sensitivities — and respond appropriately.
3. Discover future load growth (EVs, pool equipment, additions) that may affect system sizing recommendations.
4. Surface outage history and backup priorities in a way that opens the battery conversation naturally.
5. Document technical discovery findings accurately so the survey team and operations have the context they need.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — Why Technical Discovery Is Different from Standard Discovery
Standard discovery focuses on motivation: why does this homeowner want solar, what's driving their interest, what does their current bill look like? This is essential and stays in the V1 curriculum.

Technical discovery adds a second layer: what is the condition of this home and site, and what project-level factors might affect the timeline, cost, or feasibility of what we're proposing?

**The rep's job in technical discovery:**
- Not to diagnose or assess — that is the survey team's job
- Not to disqualify homeowners based on what they surface — that is rare and never the rep's call
- To gather enough context to set accurate expectations, inform the proposal, and hand off to the survey team with the right information

**The tone of technical discovery:**
Technical questions should feel like a rep taking genuine interest in the homeowner's home — not like a checklist being read from a clipboard. The rep who says "Before I put together your proposal, I want to make sure I'm recommending the right system for your specific home — can I ask a few things about the house?" sounds like a professional. The rep who launches into a 10-question interview sounds like an auditor.

**Teaching point:** One well-placed technical question often reveals three things at once. A question about roof age surfaces both condition (is the roof viable?) and ownership timeline (how long does this homeowner plan to stay?). Technical discovery is efficient when done well.

### Section 2 — The Roof Age and Condition Question Bank
These questions help reps identify whether the roof is likely to pass the site survey — and whether an MPU or roof-related delay is probable.

**Core questions:**

*"Do you know roughly how old your roof is?"*
- What you're surfacing: Roofs under 5 years have the best shot at a clean survey; roofs over 15–20 years may flag for replacement before install
- How to follow up if old: "Good to know — our survey team does a quick roof check as part of the process. I'll make sure they take a close look so there are no surprises later."
- How to follow up if recent: "That's great — a newer roof makes the whole process smoother."

*"Have you had any repairs or issues with the roof recently?"*
- What you're surfacing: Prior repairs may indicate specific vulnerabilities; leak history may indicate structural concerns
- Note: This question often opens broader homeowner conversation about the home's condition history — listen for anything significant

*"Do you know what type of roofing material you have — composition shingles, tile, metal?"*
- What you're surfacing: Most systems work on most materials; clay or concrete tile requires specific mounting hardware and may affect installation cost and timeline; flat roofs require different mounting approaches
- Rep awareness: Different materials = different labor complexity. Flag for the survey team if non-standard.

*"Is there any part of the roof that's had leaks or areas you've been meaning to get looked at?"*
- What you're surfacing: Known problem areas are almost always discovered at survey anyway — surfacing them now avoids the post-close surprise
- Follow-up: "I appreciate you mentioning that — our survey team will note it so it's on their radar."

### Section 3 — The Electrical Panel Question Bank
These questions help reps identify MPU risk before the survey team does — and set expectations proactively.

**Core questions:**

*"Do you know if your home has a 100-amp or 200-amp electrical panel?"*
- What you're surfacing: 100A panels frequently require a Main Panel Upgrade before solar installation
- Most homeowners don't know the answer — that's fine. Follow up with: "No worries — it's usually written right on the main breaker inside the panel. If it says 100, that's something our survey team will want to flag."
- If they know it's 100A: "Good to know — depending on the system size, that may need to be upgraded. Our survey team will confirm, and I'll make sure you know before anything surprises you."

*"Has your home had any electrical work done in the last few years — panel upgrades, service changes, anything like that?"*
- What you're surfacing: Recent electrical work may mean the panel is already updated (good news); conversely, unpermitted work can complicate solar permitting
- Note: If the homeowner mentions unpermitted electrical work, flag this for the survey team without alarming the homeowner

*"Do you have any large electrical loads in the home — electric vehicle chargers, a hot tub, electric heating, a large workshop?"*
- What you're surfacing: High-demand loads may already be stressing a 100A panel; they also affect system sizing and may require a panel upgrade regardless of solar
- This question also opens system sizing conversations naturally

*"Is your panel easy to get to — in the garage, the utility room, or somewhere more tucked away?"*
- What you're surfacing: Panel accessibility affects installation ease and cost; panels in unusual locations (attic, crawlspace, difficult-to-reach utility rooms) can complicate the conduit path
- This question sounds casual but surfaces real site complexity

### Section 4 — Outage History and Backup Priority Questions
These questions open the battery conversation naturally — without making it feel like a sales tactic.

**Core questions:**

*"How often do you lose power in your area? Seasonal storms, rolling blackouts, anything like that?"*
- What you're surfacing: Outage frequency and homeowner sensitivity to power loss — both are direct battery fit signals
- Follow-up for frequent outages: "That's actually something a battery system is designed for — it's worth talking through whether that makes sense in your situation."
- Follow-up for rare outages: "Good to know — then the battery conversation is really more about the financial side than backup. There's an interesting story there with the rate structure in your area."

*"Are there any appliances or parts of your home that are really critical to keep running if the power goes out — medical equipment, refrigeration, anything like that?"*
- What you're surfacing: Critical load priorities directly affect backup system design and the homeowner's emotional investment in storage
- Note: Medical equipment mentions are high-sensitivity — handle with care and do not make specific backup promises without verified system design

*"When you think about energy independence, is that something that matters to you — or is this more about the financial side for you?"*
- What you're surfacing: Energy independence motivation signals battery openness; pure financial motivation may mean battery needs an economic case rather than a resilience case
- This question also helps reps calibrate the rest of the presentation

### Section 5 — Future Load Growth Questions
These questions help reps size the system correctly and surface upsell opportunities naturally.

**Core questions:**

*"Are you planning to get an electric vehicle in the next few years — or do you already have one?"*
- What you're surfacing: EVs are one of the largest load additions a household can make; a system sized for current usage may be undersized within 2–3 years if an EV is added
- Follow-up: "That's worth factoring into the design — an EV can add 2,000–4,000 kWh per year to your usage, which changes what the right system size looks like."

*"Any plans to add to the house — additions, finished basement, a pool or pool equipment?"*
- What you're surfacing: Major home additions add electrical load; a pool pump alone can add 1,500–3,000+ kWh per year
- Follow-up: "Good to know — if we're sizing the system today, it makes sense to account for that so you don't end up undersized in a couple of years."

*"Are you currently on all gas appliances — heating, water heater, cooking — or have you been thinking about switching any of those to electric?"*
- What you're surfacing: Electrification trend; homeowners moving from gas to electric appliances significantly increase their electrical load and their solar value story
- Note: This is an increasingly common conversation as electrification programs grow in many markets — reps should be aware of incentive programs in their area related to heat pumps, electric water heaters, etc.

### Section 6 — HOA, Permitting, and Contractor History Questions
These questions surface project-level friction points that can affect timelines and homeowner experience.

**Core questions:**

*"Is your home part of an HOA?"*
- What you're surfacing: HOA involvement adds a review and approval step to the process (covered in Module 5.5B)
- Follow-up: "Good to know. HOAs typically have a review process for exterior modifications like solar — most states protect your right to install, but there's still a process our team will need to manage. It usually adds a few weeks to the timeline, and I'd rather you know that now."

*"Have you had any issues getting permits or approvals for work on your home in the past?"*
- What you're surfacing: Homeowners with difficult permit histories in their jurisdiction (unusual municipalities, strict HOAs, complex AHJs) may need expectation-setting on timeline
- Also surfaces homeowner anxiety about bureaucratic processes — which helps the rep tailor the post-sale communication approach

*"Have you had any other contractors come by to look at solar, or gotten any quotes?"*
- What you're surfacing: Competitive landscape; prior quotes may have set pricing or product expectations the rep needs to understand and address
- How to handle: Not a confrontational question — frame it as wanting to make sure you're giving them the right comparison, not just another pitch
- If they've had multiple quotes: "That's helpful context — I'd love to make sure what I'm putting together for you is actually tailored to your home rather than a generic proposal."

*"Have you worked with any solar companies before, or do you know anyone who has?"*
- What you're surfacing: Prior experience (good or bad) with solar installation or sales; referral network; potential pre-existing objections based on others' experiences
- If negative experience: Take it seriously and use it to differentiate — don't dismiss it

### Section 7 — Detached Structures and Property Complexity Questions
These questions surface scope and complexity factors that affect proposal accuracy.

**Core questions:**

*"Is that structure in the back a garage, a workshop — is that part of your property?"*
- What you're surfacing: Detached structures the homeowner may want to include — which add complexity (see Module 1.7A)
- Follow-up: "Would including that be something you'd want as part of the system?"

*"Do you have a pool or any other electrical equipment outside the main home?"*
- What you're surfacing: High-draw exterior loads that should be included in usage and sizing calculations; potential conduit routing complexity

*"Is the property on well water or city water?"*
- What you're surfacing: Well pumps are high-draw loads that significantly affect battery backup sizing and critical load prioritization; important for backup design if battery is being considered

### Section 8 — How to Weave Technical Discovery Into Natural Conversation
The worst version of technical discovery sounds like an intake form. The best version sounds like a curious, knowledgeable rep learning about someone's home.

**The transition into technical discovery:**
"Before I put together a recommendation for you, I want to make sure I'm designing the right system for your home — not just a generic proposal. Can I ask you a few things about the house?"

**Pacing principles:**
- Ask one question at a time — let each answer breathe before moving to the next
- Listen for what the answer reveals beyond the literal response — a homeowner who says "the roof is old" and then pauses is telling you something more than the age
- Use what you learn — reference it later in the presentation: "Given what you mentioned about the panel, we'll want to make sure the design team accounts for that."

**The documentation habit:**
After every discovery conversation, reps should document:
- Roof age/condition signals
- Electrical panel type (if known)
- Outage history and backup priority level
- Future load plans (EV, additions, electrification)
- HOA involvement
- Competitive landscape
- Any red flags for the survey team

This documentation is what makes the survey handoff effective — and what Module 3.7B is built around.

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The tone of technical discovery is everything. These questions should feel like a rep taking genuine interest in the home — not running an inspection checklist. Practice delivery is essential for this module. Written exercises alone are not sufficient.
- The connection between technical discovery and post-sale outcomes is the core argument for why this matters. Every question in this bank exists because a rep not asking it caused a problem downstream. Make that connection explicit.
- Documentation is not optional. A rep who surfaces an MPU risk verbally but doesn't document it has not actually helped the survey team. Build the habit of written notes in this module.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "If I ask too many questions, the homeowner will think I'm trying to find problems." → The opposite is usually true. Homeowners who feel like a rep actually studied their home trust the proposal more.
- "I'll just let the survey team figure it out." → The survey team works after signing. Pre-survey discovery protects the deal and the relationship.
- "These questions are going to slow down my close." → Technical discovery should take 5–10 minutes woven into the overall conversation. It is not a separate interrogation phase.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Reps do not need to ask every question in this bank on every visit. They need to know which questions to prioritize for the homeowner and property in front of them.
- The roof and panel questions are highest priority for every visit. The others (EV, HOA, outage history) should be asked when relevant based on what the rep observes or the conversation naturally opens.

**Where to slow down:**
- The delivery of the electrical panel question. Many reps feel awkward asking about a homeowner's panel. Practice this specific question until it sounds completely natural.
- The documentation section. Have reps practice writing a discovery summary from a role-played conversation — this builds the habit before they're in the field.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "The Quiet Discovery"
**Homeowner type:** Reserved, doesn't volunteer information, answers questions with one or two words
**Setup:** The rep is in the discovery phase with a homeowner who is interested but guarded. The homeowner has given minimal information so far. The rep needs to surface roof age, panel type, and outage history without making the conversation feel like an interrogation.
**Skill being tested:** Asking technical discovery questions in a warm, conversational way that draws out a guarded homeowner — using follow-up questions and genuine interest to expand short answers into useful information.
**Pass criteria:** Rep successfully surfaces roof age (or flags that it's unknown), electrical panel type or likely range, and outage history within a natural conversation flow. Questions feel interested, not intrusive. Rep documents the key findings at the end.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The HOA Complication"
**Homeowner type:** Enthusiastic about solar but has a complicated HOA history
**Setup:** During discovery, the homeowner mentions they're in an HOA and volunteers: "They've turned down two of our neighbors for solar already." The rep needs to handle this revelation without killing the homeowner's enthusiasm or making promises about HOA approval.
**Skill being tested:** Responding to an HOA risk disclosure with accurate information, honest expectation-setting, and maintained homeowner confidence — without dismissing the concern or overselling HOA outcomes.
**Pass criteria:** Rep acknowledges the HOA situation seriously, explains the general process (most states protect solar rights, HOA review is a step not a barrier in most cases), sets honest timeline expectations, and commits to flagging it for the operations team without guaranteeing approval.

---

### Scenario 3 — "The EV and Load Growth Conversation"
**Homeowner type:** Forward-thinking, interested in whole-home electrification
**Setup:** During discovery, the homeowner mentions they have one EV, are considering a second, and are thinking about replacing their gas furnace with a heat pump. They ask: "Can solar cover all of that?"
**Skill being tested:** Using load growth discovery to have an accurate, credible conversation about system sizing — without over-promising or under-delivering on what solar can accommodate.
**Pass criteria:** Rep correctly identifies that EV, second EV, and heat pump represent significant additional load, accurately explains that system sizing should account for this future usage, does not promise a specific system size without going through the design process, and positions the discovery information as something that will improve the accuracy of the proposal.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner says their roof was "put on when we moved in — that was 1996." What is the most appropriate rep response?
- A) "That roof is too old for solar — we'll need to talk about a replacement first."
- B) "That's fine — most roofs can support solar regardless of age."
- C) "Good to know. Our site survey team does a roof assessment as part of the process — I'll make sure they take a close look so we don't run into anything unexpected."
- D) "I can't move forward with a proposal until we know the roof condition."
*(Correct answer: C)*

**2. Short Answer**
A homeowner mentions they're planning to add a pool and get an electric vehicle within the next two years. Why does this matter for the solar system design, and how should the rep handle it in the proposal process?

**3. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner says they're in an HOA that has "been difficult before." The best rep response is:
- A) "Don't worry — HOAs can't legally block solar in most states."
- B) "That may be a problem — I'll need to check with our team before we can proceed."
- C) "Good to know. Most states do protect homeowners' rights to install solar, but there's still an HOA review process our team manages. It usually adds a few weeks to the timeline, and I'll flag this for our operations team."
- D) "HOA approval isn't part of our process — that's something you'd handle separately."
*(Correct answer: C)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner mentions they've had two other solar companies come out, and one quote was significantly lower. How should the rep use this information — and what question should they ask next?
*(Expected answer: The rep should not panic or immediately react to the price difference. Acknowledge the comparison with genuine interest: "That's helpful context — I'd love to understand what they proposed so I can make sure what I'm putting together actually makes sense for your home." Ask what was in the lower proposal — system size, equipment type, financing — before drawing any conclusions. Use the information to position the SeptiVolt proposal based on quality and fit, not just price.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
Technical discovery serves three purposes: setting accurate _______ before the close, informing the _______ and design process, and giving the survey team the _______ they need to do their job effectively.
*(Correct: expectations; proposal; context)*

---

## 8. Slide Deck Outline

1. **Title Slide** — "Technical Discovery Questions: Surface the Risk Before It Surfaces You"
2. **Why Technical Discovery Is Different** — Motivation discovery vs. risk discovery; both are required
3. **The Tone That Makes It Work** — Curious professional vs. auditor; how the same question lands differently
4. **Roof Age & Condition Questions** — The question bank with follow-up language
5. **Electrical Panel Questions** — 100A vs. 200A; the MPU risk; natural question framing
6. **Outage History & Backup Priority Questions** — Opening the battery conversation without forcing it
7. **Future Load Growth Questions** — EVs, appliances, additions; sizing implications
8. **HOA, Permitting & Contractor History Questions** — Pipeline friction signals; how to respond
9. **Detached Structures & Property Complexity** — Scope questions; when to flag
10. **Weaving Technical Discovery Into Natural Conversation** — The transition phrase; pacing principles
11. **The Documentation Habit** — Why written notes protect deals; what to capture
12. **Key Takeaways + Module Recap**

---

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"I want to start with a scenario. You close a deal on a Tuesday. Two weeks later, the survey team comes out. They find the roof is original from 1988 and needs to be replaced before install. The homeowner is blindsided. They're upset. They feel like nobody told them this was a possibility. Now — here's the question. Was any of that visible at the sales visit? Usually, the answer is yes. The roof was old. The rep just didn't ask. Today we're going to make sure that never happens to you."

**Concept Explanation (15 min)**
Walk through the question banks by category. Don't read them as a list — demo them. Pick a volunteer homeowner from the room and walk through a natural 5-minute discovery conversation using the questions. Let the group watch how the questions flow in real conversation vs. how they look on a slide.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's the difference between the wrong version and the right version. Wrong: [read the interrogative version from Exercise 3.7A-2 in a flat, checklist tone]. Right: [deliver the same questions naturally, with follow-ups, as a genuine conversation]. Same information. Completely different experience. The homeowner in version one wants you to leave. The homeowner in version two just told you everything you needed to know."

**Activity Transition (8 min)**
Exercise 3.7A-3 (Discovery Response Practice) — reps write their follow-ups individually, then pair up and do live back-and-forth role play with a partner playing the homeowner. Debrief: which follow-ups naturally opened more conversation? Which ones closed it down?

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Every question in this module exists because a rep not asking it caused a problem downstream — a surprised homeowner, a stalled deal, a cancelled contract. You're not asking these questions to find problems. You're asking them so that when the survey team finds something, the homeowner already knows it's possible. That's the difference between trust and betrayal. That's the difference between a rep who builds a referral business and a rep who doesn't."

---

### Module 3.7b: Site Survey Prep for Reps

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 3.7B — Site Survey Prep for Reps

**Insert after Module 3.7A | Day 3 — Discovery & Site Awareness**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
The site survey is one of the most important moments in the solar project pipeline — and one of the most misunderstood by reps. Most reps think their job is done once the contract is signed and the survey is scheduled. Module 3.7B reframes that entirely: the rep's preparation before the survey is what determines whether the survey runs smoothly, whether the homeowner is ready for it, and whether the project is set up to complete without unnecessary delays or cost surprises.

### Why It Matters
Poor survey handoffs are one of the leading causes of post-close project delays and homeowner frustration. When the survey team arrives at a home without context, without prepared homeowners, and without documented red flags, they're starting from scratch — and any surprises they find land harder on the homeowner because the rep didn't prepare them. A well-prepped survey visit feels like a professional operation. A poorly-prepped one feels chaotic, and that chaos becomes the homeowner's first post-close experience.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module is the direct application layer for both Module 3.7A (Technical Discovery Questions) and Module 1.7A (Basic Site Engineering Awareness). Discovery questions identify the flags; this module teaches reps what to do with them. It also connects forward to Module 5.5A (Post-Sale Project Lifecycle) and Module 5.5B (Common Installation Pipeline Issues) — because the survey is where most pipeline issues originate.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Explain what the site survey is actually validating — and what that means for the rep's preparation.
2. Know exactly what information to gather from the homeowner before the survey is conducted.
3. Prepare the homeowner for the survey visit in a way that reduces anxiety, prevents no-shows, and sets accurate expectations.
4. Document red flags and discovery findings in a format that is actually useful to the survey team.
5. Understand how poor survey prep creates downstream damage — and how good prep protects deals.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — What the Site Survey Is Actually Validating
Many reps think of the site survey as "someone coming to measure the roof." That is a significant underestimation of what the survey team is doing and why it matters.

**What the site survey validates:**

**Roof structural integrity and suitability:**
- Can the roof structurally support the weight of solar panels and mounting hardware?
- Is the roofing material compatible with the proposed mounting system?
- Is the remaining roof lifespan sufficient to justify installation without replacement first?
- Are there obstacles, setbacks, or positioning constraints not visible from satellite imagery?

**Shading analysis:**
- A proper shading analysis (often conducted with specialized tools like a Solmetric SunEye or equivalent) quantifies exactly how much production will be lost to shading at every hour of the day throughout the year
- This data is what engineering uses to finalize the system design and production estimate
- Significant shade found at survey can change the system size, the equipment recommendation (string vs. micro), and the production estimate presented during the sale

**Electrical system compatibility:**
- What is the panel amperage? Is there available breaker space?
- Is a Main Panel Upgrade required?
- Is the panel brand one with known compatibility issues (some older brands have specific concerns that affect the installation)?
- Where is the optimal inverter location, and what is the conduit path from the roof to that location?

**Site-specific installation planning:**
- Where will the inverter and monitoring equipment be mounted?
- What is the conduit routing path?
- Are there any access constraints for the installation crew?
- Are there any permitting-specific site requirements in this jurisdiction?

**Teaching point:** The survey is not a formality — it is the moment of truth between what was proposed and what is actually buildable. Reps who understand this treat survey prep with the seriousness it deserves.

### Section 2 — What Reps Should Gather Before the Survey
The survey team's job is made dramatically easier — and the project's chance of completing without surprises is dramatically higher — when the rep gathers and communicates the right information before the survey visit.

**The Pre-Survey Rep Information Package:**

**1. Roof information:**
- Approximate age (from discovery conversation)
- Material type (composition, tile, metal, flat, other)
- Any known condition issues, prior repairs, or areas of concern
- Any unusual features: multiple stories, steep pitch, complex geometry

**2. Electrical information:**
- Known or estimated panel amperage (100A or 200A — if the homeowner could confirm)
- Any recent electrical work or upgrades
- Location of the electrical panel (garage, utility room, exterior, basement)
- Any high-draw loads the homeowner mentioned (EVs, hot tub, workshop)

**3. Site and property information:**
- HOA involvement (yes/no, and any known HOA restrictions or prior solar application outcomes in the neighborhood)
- Detached structures being included in the project
- Any known permit or jurisdiction complications the homeowner mentioned
- Access constraints: locked gates, aggressive animals, limited driveway access

**4. Homeowner context:**
- Is the homeowner aware of any red flags that were discussed during the sales visit?
- What did the rep tell the homeowner to expect from the survey visit?
- Any homeowner sensitivities (anxious, very detail-oriented, expects a quick in-and-out, prefers to be present)
- Are there any competing quotes or homeowner concerns the survey team should be aware of?

**Teaching point:** A rep who sends this information to the operations team before the survey is demonstrating professionalism that the operations team will remember — and reciprocate when deals need prioritization or escalation help.

### Section 3 — Pre-Survey Homeowner Preparation
How the rep prepares the homeowner for the survey visit has a direct impact on the survey's success and the homeowner's post-close experience.

**What to tell the homeowner before the survey:**

**What it is:**
"The site survey is when our technical team comes to your home to confirm all the details we'll need to finalize your system design. It's standard for every project — think of it as the engineering team doing their homework so everything goes smoothly at installation."

**Who comes:**
"One of our site assessment technicians will come out. They'll be on your roof, in your attic if accessible, and they'll take a look at your electrical panel. The whole visit typically takes [X minutes/hours — localize to your company's actual survey duration]."

**What to have ready:**
- Access to the electrical panel (unlocked, clear of obstructions)
- Attic access if the home has one (hatch should be accessible)
- Any utility bills the rep didn't already collect — the surveyor may want to reference usage data
- If there's a gated entrance or a dog that needs to be secured

**What they'll find out:**
"After the survey, our engineering team will finalize your system design and production estimate. If the survey turns up anything that affects the design or scope, we'll reach out to you right away to talk through it — I'll be your point of contact for that conversation."

**What not to say:**
- Do not promise the survey will find nothing unexpected
- Do not promise the survey will take a specific amount of time
- Do not tell the homeowner the survey is "just a formality" — it isn't, and if something comes up, this framing will damage trust

### Section 4 — Documenting Red Flags Correctly
The most common rep documentation failure is not the absence of notes — it is notes that are vague, incomplete, or filed in a place the survey team never sees.

**What "correct" documentation looks like:**

**Specific, not vague:**
- Bad: "Roof might be old."
- Good: "Homeowner believes roof was installed around 1998. Original composition shingles. No known repairs but mentioned some curling near the north-facing section."

**Actionable, not descriptive:**
- Bad: "Electrical panel could be an issue."
- Good: "Homeowner confirmed main breaker is labeled 100A. Panel is in the garage, easily accessible. Two open slots visible. Rep flagged potential MPU requirement and homeowner is aware."

**Communicated to the right people:**
- CRM notes that the survey team doesn't see = not communicated
- Email to the project coordinator = communicated, but not archived
- CRM note AND direct message to the project coordinator = communicated and documented
- Best practice: Know your company's specific documentation SOP and follow it

**The four categories to document after every sales visit:**
1. Roof flags
2. Electrical flags
3. HOA/permitting flags
4. Homeowner context notes (sensitivities, competing quotes, specific expectations set during the sales visit)

### Section 5 — How Poor Survey Prep Damages Trust and Deals
This section is about making the consequences of poor survey prep concrete — so reps treat it as a revenue-protecting activity, not an administrative burden.

**Scenario A: The MPU Surprise**
Rep closes a deal. During the visit, the homeowner mentioned the panel "looked pretty old." Rep didn't document it. Survey team arrives, discovers a 100A panel with no available breaker space. Survey team notifies operations, who calls the homeowner with a $3,200 MPU quote. Homeowner is blindsided. They call the rep angrily. They feel like they weren't told about this possibility. They are now a cancellation risk.

What went wrong: Rep didn't document the flag. Survey team had no context. Homeowner wasn't prepared.

What good prep looks like: Rep notes "Homeowner mentioned panel looks old — recommend survey team assess amperage and breaker space. I've verbally flagged to homeowner that panel condition will be confirmed at survey and may affect scope."

**Scenario B: The No-Show Survey**
Survey team arrives at the scheduled time. Nobody home. Locked gate. Dog in the yard with no warning. Survey has to be rescheduled — adding 1–2 weeks to the timeline. Homeowner is apologetic but frustrated. Operations is irritated.

What went wrong: Rep didn't prepare the homeowner for the survey visit specifics or confirm logistics.

What good prep looks like: Rep confirms the survey date with the homeowner, confirms what access will be needed (panel, attic, clear path to roof), confirms no access barriers. Sends a reminder text the day before.

**Scenario C: The Redesign That Kills Enthusiasm**
Survey team finds significantly more shading than the satellite analysis suggested, due to a large tree on the neighbor's property that wasn't visible in imagery. System production estimate drops 25%. Engineering issues a redesign. Operations contacts the homeowner with the updated numbers. Homeowner feels misled about the original proposal.

What went wrong: The shading source was visible during the sales visit (rep walked around the property). Rep didn't note it or flag it. Homeowner wasn't prepared for the possibility of a production revision.

What good prep looks like: Rep notes the neighbor's tree, flags it to the survey team: "Large deciduous tree on north side of adjacent property may create afternoon shade — recommend detailed shade analysis of southeast-facing panels." If flagged, the survey report doesn't feel like a surprise — it feels like the system working as designed.

### Section 6 — The Pre-Survey SOP: Rep's Checklist
This is the operational checklist reps use between close and survey.

**Within 24–48 hours of signing:**
- [ ] Confirm site survey is scheduled and homeowner has the date, time, and what to expect
- [ ] Submit the Pre-Survey Rep Information Package to the project coordinator (or equivalent per company SOP)
- [ ] Document all red flags in the CRM with specific, actionable language
- [ ] Confirm homeowner has cleared any access barriers (gates, attic hatch, panel access)

**3–5 days before the survey:**
- [ ] Send a brief reminder to the homeowner: date, time, what to have ready, who will be coming
- [ ] Confirm no changes to the homeowner's availability or any new site concerns they've identified
- [ ] Check with the project coordinator for any pre-survey questions from the engineering or design team

**Day before or day of the survey:**
- [ ] Send a brief "your survey is tomorrow/today" reminder via text or call — keep it warm and brief
- [ ] Make sure the homeowner knows they can call or text you if anything comes up on the day

**After the survey:**
- [ ] Follow up with the homeowner within 24 hours to confirm the survey went well and ask if they have any questions
- [ ] Check with the project coordinator for any findings or flags from the survey team
- [ ] If there are any scope changes, production revisions, or cost implications — the rep hears it first and delivers it to the homeowner (not operations)

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The pre-survey SOP is not optional administrative work — it is active deal protection. Every item on the checklist exists because its absence has caused a deal to stall or cancel. Make this connection explicit.
- The documentation section is where most reps need the most behavioral change. Specific, actionable notes are a skill — vague notes are the default. Practice writing specific notes in this session.
- Scenario A (the MPU surprise) should be delivered as a story, not a bullet point. Make it real. The cost to the rep — in commission risk, in relationship damage, in reputation — needs to be felt, not just understood.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "The survey is operations' responsibility — I don't need to prepare for it." → The rep is the homeowner's advocate through the entire project. The survey is their deal on the line.
- "I told the homeowner the survey is just a formality to move things forward." → This framing is the most dangerous thing a rep can say pre-survey. It sets up every finding as a betrayal of trust.
- "I put it in my notes in [CRM/app] — operations will see it." → Reps must know their company's specific process for survey team communication. A CRM note the survey team doesn't actively review is not communication.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- The pre-survey SOP should be presented as a simple, practical checklist — not a multi-page process document. Reps need to be able to execute it in 15–20 minutes of total time between close and survey.
- The documentation language examples are the teaching tool. Have reps rewrite vague examples into specific ones — repetition builds the habit.

**Where to slow down:**
- The "what the survey is actually validating" section. Many reps genuinely don't know what happens at a site survey. Take time here. Ask the room: "What do you think the survey team is actually doing when they're at the house?" Correct misconceptions before moving on.
- The three damage scenarios. Spend time on each. Ask the room: "Has anyone seen something like this happen?" The shared experience of recognition makes the lesson stick.

---

- Who is coming and approximately how long it will take
- What they need to have accessible (panel, attic, any gated access)
- What they'll hear afterward and from whom
- Any expectations you're resetting based on discovery findings (HOA process, potential MPU flag)

---

### Exercise 3.7B-3 — Documentation Rewrite Drill

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "The Survey Team Found Something"
**Homeowner type:** Signed enthusiastically, has not heard from the rep since
**Setup:** The survey team completed their visit and found a significant shading issue that will reduce the system's production estimate by 20%. The rep needs to call the homeowner to explain the finding before operations sends the revised design.
**Skill being tested:** Delivering a post-survey scope change communication with transparency and composure — taking ownership of the finding and reframing it as the system working correctly rather than the rep having misled the homeowner.
**Pass criteria:** Rep calls the homeowner proactively (before operations does), explains the shading finding in plain language, acknowledges the impact on the production estimate honestly, does not minimize the change, and keeps the homeowner's confidence in the project and the rep intact.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The Survey No-Show"
**Homeowner type:** Busy professional — agreed to the survey appointment but didn't put it in their calendar
**Setup:** The rep receives a call from the project coordinator: the survey tech arrived at the Chen home this morning and nobody was home. The gate was locked. The survey needs to be rescheduled. The rep needs to call the homeowner.
**Skill being tested:** Handling a survey no-show without blame, frustration, or pressure — rescheduling professionally while reinforcing the importance of the appointment.
**Pass criteria:** Rep calls the homeowner with warmth, not frustration. Explains what happened without assigning fault. Reschedules immediately. Uses the opportunity to reinforce what the homeowner needs to have ready (gate code, panel access, availability). Commits to confirming the new appointment closer to the date.

---

### Scenario 3 — "The Homeowner Calls During the Survey"
**Homeowner type:** Anxious, called the rep during the survey visit because the tech asked to go into the attic and the homeowner wasn't expecting it
**Setup:** The homeowner calls the rep mid-survey: "Your guy wants to go into our attic and look at some wiring up there. I didn't know that was part of this. Is this normal? Should I let him?"
**Skill being tested:** Fielding an anxious mid-survey call, normalizing the attic access request, and reassuring the homeowner without making specific promises about what will be found.
**Pass criteria:** Rep answers calmly, explains that attic access is a standard part of the survey process (used to assess structural framing, wiring paths, and ventilation near panel mounting areas), reassures the homeowner it's normal and expected, and thanks them for calling rather than just refusing access.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
A rep closes a deal on Monday. The site survey is scheduled for the following Wednesday. What should the rep do within 24–48 hours of the close?
- A) Nothing — the survey is operations' responsibility from here
- B) Confirm the survey date with the homeowner, submit the pre-survey information package to the project coordinator, and document all red flags in the CRM
- C) Call the homeowner to make sure they haven't changed their mind
- D) Email the survey team the homeowner's contact information and nothing else
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
What are the four items the site survey team is assessing during a residential solar site survey? For each one, explain in one sentence why it matters for the project.

**3. Multiple Choice**
A rep tells the homeowner before the survey: "The site survey is just a formality — it's really just someone coming to measure the roof and take a few photos." This is:
- A) Accurate — the survey is mostly visual documentation
- B) A compliance violation
- C) Problematic — it sets the homeowner up to feel misled if the survey reveals any significant findings that affect scope, cost, or design
- D) Fine as long as the survey doesn't actually find anything
*(Correct answer: C)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
The survey team finds that the homeowner's panel is 100-amp and will require an MPU before installation. The rep didn't document any electrical flags before the survey. Operations wants to call the homeowner with the news. What should the rep do?
*(Expected answer: The rep should call the homeowner first — before operations. The rep owns the homeowner relationship and the homeowner should hear this from their rep, not from an operations call they weren't expecting. The rep should explain what an MPU is, why it's needed, and what it means for the project — using the language framework from Module 5.5B. The rep then coordinates with operations on the cost and timeline specifics.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
The difference between vague and specific documentation is the difference between _______ and _______. Vague notes like "roof might be an issue" leave the survey team without context; specific notes like "_______ [example]" give the survey team what they need to prepare.
*(Correct: risk and protection; any specific example such as "Homeowner estimates roof is approximately 25 years old — original composition shingles, visible wear near north section, no known repairs")*

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"Let me ask you something. What do you think the site survey team is actually doing when they're at a homeowner's house? [Pause for answers.] Here's what I hear most: 'They're measuring the roof, taking photos, checking out the panel.' That's part of it. But here's what's really happening: they're validating everything you proposed. Every panel you placed, every production estimate you showed, every design assumption — the survey team is confirming whether that proposal is actually buildable. That's why survey prep is one of the most important things you do between the close and the install."

**Concept Explanation (15 min)**
Walk through what the survey validates (Section 1) with specific examples from real projects if possible. Then move into the pre-survey information package (Section 2) and the homeowner preparation language (Section 3). Use real documentation examples — show the vague version, then show the specific version side by side.

**Example (5 min)**
Walk through Scenario A (the MPU surprise) as a story — not a bullet point. Set the scene: the rep who closed the deal, the homeowner who signed, the operations call that went wrong, the angry voicemail. Then rewind: "Here's what the rep could have done at the sales visit. Here's what the documentation would have looked like. Here's the call they would have made before the survey. Here's how a different homeowner experience unfolds." Let the contrast do the work.

**Activity Transition (8 min)**
Exercise 3.7B-3 (Documentation Rewrite Drill) — do the first two as a group on the whiteboard. The whole room rewrites the vague note into a specific one together. Then reps complete the remaining three individually and share their best one with a partner. Group debrief: what makes a documentation note actually useful?

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Survey prep is not administrative busywork. It is you protecting your deal, your commission, and your homeowner's experience with the most important handoff in the entire project pipeline. The rep who does this well never has a homeowner who feels blindsided after the survey. The rep who skips it is playing Russian roulette with every close. This is a five-minute habit that protects everything you've built."

## DAY 4: Presentation Mastery & Financing
### Module 4.1: Utility Bill Deep Dive: Advanced Analysis
- **ID:** mod_4_1
- **Subtitle:** Perform surgical bill analysis in front of homeowner Build credibility through technical mastery Identify hidden savings
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Bill Autopsy Process**  
When you analyze a utility bill in front of a homeowner, narrate everything you're doing. Let me show you exactly where your money's going. This transparency builds trust. You're not hiding behind a proposal \u2014 you're showing them their own data in real time. Start with the twelve-month usage history. Add up the total kilowatt-hours. Calculate the daily average. Then say: so you're using about thirty-three kilowatt-hours per day \u2014 that's typical for a home this size in this area. You've immediately shown expertise without selling anything.

**Rate Structure Identification**  
The rate structure determines how much solar actually saves. A flat rate is the simplest \u2014 same cents per kilowatt-hour all month. A tiered rate gets more expensive as usage increases, which means solar saves more per kilowatt-hour because it eliminates the most expensive usage tiers first. Time-of-use rates charge different amounts by time of day \u2014 morning off-peak is cheap, afternoon on-peak is expensive. For time-of-use customers, solar production during peak hours is most valuable, and a battery can maximize those savings even further.

**Inflation Projection**  
Here is the most powerful number in your presentation. Look up your utility's historical rate increase \u2014 typically five to six percent per year. Then show the compounding effect. A current bill of one hundred eighty dollars per month becomes two hundred forty dollars in five years, three hundred twenty in ten, and over four hundred dollars in fifteen. Over twenty-five years, the homeowner will pay the utility over one hundred thousand dollars \u2014 and own nothing. Solar locks their rate. The contrast between those two futures is where decisions get made.

**True-Up Logic & Offset Explained**  
For homeowners on net metering, true-up is the annual settlement. During the year, credits accumulate when the system overproduces. At year-end, the utility settles the account. If you overproduced, they pay you \u2014 typically at wholesale rates. If you underproduced, you pay the difference at retail. That's why we size systems at one hundred five to one hundred ten percent of annual usage \u2014 to build in a production cushion for degradation and future increases. And they'll still have a small monthly bill \u2014 usually ten to twenty dollars \u2014 for connection fees. That's the honest expectation to set.

#### SLIDE DECK OUTLINE
*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Utility Bill Deep Dive: Advanced Analysis: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA
