### Module 5.3: Closing Techniques
- **ID:** mod_5_3
- **Subtitle:** Ask for the sale confidently Use proven closing techniques without pressure Handle last-minute resistance
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The 5 Closing Techniques**  
You have five closing techniques to deploy based on the homeowner's personality and readiness. The Assumptive Close \u2014 proceed to logistics as if the decision has been made: let's get your application started. What day works for your site survey? The Summary Close \u2014 recap the entire value proposition and ask a simple yes or no question. The Decision Matrix \u2014 draw a T-chart showing the cost of going solar versus the cost of waiting. The Silence Close \u2014 ask for the sale once, clearly, then stop talking. Whoever speaks first loses. And the Last Objection \u2014 is there anything else holding you back, or are you ready to move forward?

### Module 5.4: Contract Walk-Through & Compliance
- **ID:** mod_5_4
- **Subtitle:** Walk through contract clearly and confidently Prevent buyer's remorse before it starts Meet all legal/compliance obligat
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The 5-Section Contract Walk-Through**  
Never say just sign here. Walk through the contract section by section. System description \u2014 here are your exact panels, inverter, and system size. Exactly what's going on your roof. Pricing and financing \u2014 here's your total cost, your tax credit, and your monthly payment. Warranties \u2014 twenty-five year panel warranty, ten year workmanship, twelve year inverter. Cancellation policy \u2014 you have a three-day right of rescission. No penalty within seventy-two hours \u2014 just email us. Installation timeline \u2014 sixty to ninety days post-permit. Then say: this isn't the scary part. This is the starting line. From here, we do all the work.

---

### Module 5.5: Cancellation Prevention & Buyer's Remorse
- **ID:** mod_5_5
- **Subtitle:** Understand why cancellations happen Prevent remorse before it sets in Lock in commitment post-close
#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Why Homeowners Cancel**  
Cancellations happen for five predictable reasons. A skeptical family member talked them out of it after you left. They found a competing quote and panicked. They felt rushed or pressured during the sale. They didn't fully understand what they signed. Or time passed and the urgency faded. Every one of these is preventable. Rushing the close causes the first and third. Skipping contract walk-through causes the fourth. Failing to lock in next-step excitement causes the fifth. Prevention starts during the sale, not after.

**Post-Close Protocol**  
At the moment of close, before you leave: congratulate them and inoculate them. Say: congrats! Here's what's going to happen next \u2014 you'll probably get a call from a competitor and a family member who questions the decision. That's completely normal. The math we looked at together doesn't change. Call me anytime with questions. Then send a text within one hour recapping next steps and expressing excitement. Schedule a check-in call at forty-eight hours. And if they do call to cancel \u2014 don't panic, don't argue. Ask what changed since you spoke, listen fully, and address the actual concern.

*No specific slides defined for this module.*
#### WORKBOOK PROMPTS
- Reflect on Cancellation Prevention & Buyer's Remorse: How will you apply this?
#### QUIZ DATA

### Module 5.5a: Advanced Closing Scripts

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 5.5A — The Post-Sale Project Lifecycle

**Insert after existing Module 5.5 | Day 5 — Closing, Retention & Project Lifecycle Awareness**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Most reps are trained to close deals. Very few are trained on what happens after. Module 5.5A gives reps a clear, working understanding of the full project lifecycle — from the moment a contract is signed to the moment a homeowner's system goes live. This knowledge does two critical things: it allows reps to set accurate expectations before the close, and it keeps reps relevant and trusted throughout the post-sale experience.

### Why It Matters
The #1 cause of solar cancellations is not buyer's remorse at signing — it is broken trust during the installation pipeline. Homeowners who don't hear from their rep after signing, who don't know what to expect, and who are surprised by delays become cancellations. Reps who proactively manage post-sale expectations preserve their deals and earn referrals.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module follows Day 5's closing and retention content and opens the post-sale block. It gives reps the foundational map they need before Module 5.5B (Pipeline Issues), 5.5C (Escalation Paths), and 5.5D (Save-the-Deal Communication). Without this module, the downstream modules have no anchor.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Name and describe each stage of the solar project lifecycle from contract to Permission to Operate (PTO).
2. Communicate realistic timeline expectations to homeowners before and immediately after the close.
3. Identify the stages where homeowner trust is most commonly lost — and why.
4. Understand the rep's role in the post-sale process, including what they own and what belongs to operations.
5. Use the post-sale timeline as a proactive tool for relationship management and cancellation prevention.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — The Project Lifecycle: A Rep's Map
Every solar project goes through the same general sequence of stages from contract to live system. The exact timing varies by market, utility, and jurisdiction — but the stages themselves are consistent.

**The seven stages:**

**Stage 1 — Contract Signed**
- The homeowner signs the agreement. The deal is live.
- Internal kickoff begins: the file moves to operations, financing is verified, site survey is scheduled.
- Homeowner expectation: "What happens next?"
- Rep's job: Explain the next step immediately. Never let the homeowner leave the table without knowing what comes after signing.

**Stage 2 — Site Survey**
- A technician visits the home to assess the roof, electrical panel, attic, and site conditions.
- This confirms whether the system design from the sales visit is buildable as proposed.
- Timeline: typically 1–3 weeks after contract signing depending on market and company capacity.
- Homeowner expectation: Someone will come to my house and look at things.
- Rep's job: Set the expectation that this is normal and important — not a red flag.

**Stage 3 — System Design**
- The engineering team creates or finalizes the system design based on the site survey data.
- If the site survey reveals issues (shade, panel placement constraints, electrical upgrades needed), the design may be revised.
- This is where redesigns happen — and where reps often have no visibility unless they ask.
- Timeline: 1–4 weeks depending on design complexity and team capacity.

**Stage 4 — Permitting**
- The solar company submits the design to the local jurisdiction (city, county, municipality) for permit approval.
- This is the stage most outside of anyone's direct control.
- Timeline: Highly variable. Can range from 1 week to several months depending on the jurisdiction.
- Homeowner expectation: "This is taking too long." (Very common.)
- Rep's job: Set permit timeline expectations early. Do not let this be a surprise.

**Stage 5 — Installation**
- The crew installs the solar panels, inverters, wiring, and any battery equipment.
- Most installations are completed in 1–2 days for a standard residential system.
- Homeowner expectation: Finally — something is happening!
- Rep's job: Congratulate the homeowner. Check in the day of or day after installation.

**Stage 6 — Inspection**
- The local jurisdiction sends an inspector to verify the system was installed according to the approved permit.
- If the inspection passes, the system moves to utility interconnection.
- If it fails, corrections must be made and a re-inspection scheduled.
- Timeline: Typically 1–3 weeks after installation.

**Stage 7 — Permission to Operate (PTO)**
- The utility company officially authorizes the homeowner to turn on their solar system.
- This is the true finish line — not installation.
- Timeline: 1–6 weeks after passing inspection, depending on the utility.
- Homeowner expectation: "Why can't I turn it on yet? It's already on my roof."
- Rep's job: This is the most misunderstood stage. Prep homeowners for it at signing.

### Section 2 — Total Timeline Expectations
- From contract signing to PTO: typically **3 to 6 months** in most markets.
- Some markets are faster (streamlined permit jurisdictions, responsive utilities).
- Some markets are significantly slower (complex permit authorities, utility backlogs).
- **Teaching point:** Never promise a specific timeline you cannot guarantee. Use ranges and use localized data. "In our market, most projects go live within X to Y months from signing" is safe language.

### Section 3 — Where Trust Is Most Commonly Lost
The post-sale pipeline is not just an operations process — it is a trust management process. Reps who understand this are significantly more effective at protecting deals.

**The three most common trust-breaking moments:**

**Moment 1 — The Silence After Signing**
Many reps close the deal and disappear. The homeowner hears nothing for weeks. Anxiety builds. Cancellation risk rises.
- Prevention: Contact the homeowner within 48 hours of signing to confirm their site survey is scheduled.

**Moment 2 — The Permit Surprise**
The homeowner expects the system to be installed within a few weeks. They learn the permit is taking 6–10 weeks. No one warned them.
- Prevention: Set the permit timeline expectation explicitly at the time of signing.

**Moment 3 — The PTO Confusion**
The system is installed. The homeowner can see the panels on their roof. They can't turn it on. They weren't told about PTO.
- Prevention: Explain PTO clearly before and right after installation. It is the finish line — not installation day.

### Section 4 — The Rep's Role After the Close
Understanding the boundaries of the rep's post-sale role is essential. Reps who try to do too much create confusion. Reps who do too little lose deals.

**What the rep owns post-close:**
- Homeowner relationship and communication
- Expectation management at each stage
- Early problem identification and escalation (before the homeowner gets frustrated)
- Referral relationship cultivation throughout the pipeline

**What belongs to operations:**
- Site survey scheduling and execution
- System design decisions
- Permit submission and tracking
- Installation scheduling and crew coordination
- Inspection and PTO processes

**Teaching point:** The rep is the homeowner's advocate inside the company — not the project manager. Knowing the difference prevents the rep from making promises they can't keep and from creating confusion about who owns what.

### Section 5 — Using the Lifecycle as a Proactive Tool
The post-sale lifecycle is not just a defensive play (preventing cancellations) — it is an offensive play (building referrals). Reps who stay in contact throughout the pipeline are the ones who get the neighbor referrals.

**The 7-touchpoint model:**
1. **Day of signing** — "Here's exactly what happens next."
2. **Site survey confirmation** — "Your site survey is confirmed for [date]."
3. **Post-site survey** — "Survey went well. Design is being finalized."
4. **Permit submitted** — "Your permit is in. Here's what that stage looks like."
5. **Install date confirmed** — "Great news — your install is scheduled!"
6. **Day of/after install** — "Panels are up! Here's the last step before you go live."
7. **PTO received** — "You're officially live. Congratulations. Now — do you know anyone else who should be having this conversation?"

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- PTO is the finish line — not installation. This needs to be drilled explicitly. Reps who set this expectation before signing prevent the most common post-install cancellation trigger.
- The "silence after signing" is the most avoidable trust-killer. A single 48-hour check-in call costs the rep almost nothing and dramatically reduces cancellation risk.
- The 7-touchpoint model is a framework, not a script. Reps should adapt it to their market, company process, and homeowner communication style.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "Once I close, it's operations' problem." → Wrong. The rep owns the homeowner relationship through PTO. If the homeowner cancels because of a pipeline issue, the rep loses the commission.
- "I don't want to bother the homeowner." → Homeowners want to hear from their rep. Silence is what bothers them.
- "I can't control what operations does, so there's nothing I can do." → The rep can't control operations timelines, but they can control communication. Communication is the deal-saver.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Reps don't need to know the legal or technical details of each stage. They need to know the stages exist, what happens in each, and what the homeowner will feel.
- Don't turn this module into a project management training. Keep it rep-focused and relationship-focused.

**Where to slow down:**
- The three trust-breaking moments. Have reps personally identify which of these they've experienced (as a rep or as a customer of any service). Make it real.
- The rep's role vs. operations' role. This boundary matters. Spend time on it.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "It's Been Three Weeks"
**Homeowner type:** Anxious, detail-oriented, checking email daily for updates
**Setup:** Three weeks after signing, the homeowner calls and says: "It's been three weeks and I haven't heard anything. What's going on? I'm starting to regret this."
**Skill being tested:** Responding to post-sale anxiety with calm, organized communication — without panicking or making promises about operations timelines.
**Pass criteria:** Rep does not blame operations, correctly identifies where the homeowner is in the pipeline, provides accurate next-step information, reaffirms the relationship, and schedules a follow-up rather than leaving the homeowner hanging again.

---

### Scenario 2 — "My Panels Are Up — Why Can't I Turn It On?"
**Homeowner type:** Excited about installation, now confused and frustrated
**Setup:** The homeowner calls two weeks after installation saying: "The panels have been on my roof for two weeks. My neighbor's system was live the day they installed. Why isn't mine on?"
**Skill being tested:** Explaining the PTO process calmly, correctly identifying where the delay is (inspection vs. utility), and managing expectations without dismissing the frustration.
**Pass criteria:** Rep accurately explains the inspection and PTO stages, empathizes with the frustration, confirms the next step, and does not throw operations under the bus.

---

### Scenario 3 — "Walk Me Through What Happens After I Sign"
**Homeowner type:** On the fence, wants to feel organized and informed before committing
**Setup:** A homeowner says: "Before I sign anything, walk me through exactly what happens from here to when the thing is actually on and running. I've heard horror stories."
**Skill being tested:** Proactively setting accurate, organized timeline expectations as a closing tool — not just a post-close communication technique.
**Pass criteria:** Rep walks through all seven stages clearly and confidently, uses honest timeline ranges, acknowledges the stages that can be unpredictable (permitting), and frames the process in a way that builds rather than reduces confidence.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
What does PTO stand for, and when does it occur in the project lifecycle?
- A) Power Transfer Operation — it occurs at installation
- B) Permission to Operate — it occurs after inspection, when the utility authorizes the homeowner to turn on their system
- C) Project Timeline Optimization — it occurs during permitting
- D) Panel Transfer Order — it occurs when the equipment ships
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
Name the three most common post-sale trust-breaking moments. For each one, describe what the rep can do to prevent it.

**3. Multiple Choice**
A rep closes a deal on a Tuesday. The best next action is:
- A) Wait for the homeowner to contact them with questions
- B) Contact the homeowner within 48 hours to confirm the site survey is scheduled and review what comes next
- C) Send a generic "thank you for your business" email and move on to the next lead
- D) Call the homeowner only if there's a problem
*(Correct answer: B)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner's permit is taking 10 weeks and they are frustrated. They haven't heard from their rep in three weeks and are considering canceling. What should the rep have done differently — and what should they do now?
*(Expected answer: Should have set permit timeline expectations at signing, should have maintained regular contact throughout. Now: call immediately, acknowledge the delay honestly, take ownership of the communication gap, provide a current status update, and schedule a specific next check-in.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
The true finish line of a solar project is not _____________ day — it is the day the homeowner receives _____________, which is when the _____________ officially authorizes the system to operate.
*(Correct: installation; Permission to Operate / PTO; utility)*

## 8. Slide Deck Outline

1. **Title Slide** — "The Post-Sale Project Lifecycle: Your Map from Contract to Live"
2. **Why Post-Sale Matters** — The #1 cause of solar cancellations is not buyer's remorse — it's broken trust
3. **The Seven Stages** — Visual pipeline: Contract → Survey → Design → Permit → Install → Inspection → PTO
4. **Stage Deep Dive: Contract to Design** — What happens, realistic timelines, homeowner expectations
5. **Stage Deep Dive: Permit** — Why it varies, what to set at signing, safe timeline language
6. **Stage Deep Dive: Install to PTO** — The most misunderstood sequence; PTO is the finish line
7. **Total Timeline Expectations** — Safe ranges, market localization reminder
8. **Where Trust Is Most Commonly Lost** — The three trust-breaking moments
9. **The Rep's Role vs. Operations' Role** — Boundaries that protect deals and relationships
10. **The 7-Touchpoint Model** — From signing to PTO
11. **Key Takeaways + Module Recap**

---

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"Quick question: What percentage of your deals do you think cancel or go sideways after the close — before the system is even installed? [Pause for answers.] In most markets, post-close cancellations are one of the biggest revenue leaks in the business. And the most common cause isn't the homeowner changing their mind about solar. It's the homeowner losing trust because nobody told them what was coming."

**Concept Explanation (12–15 min)**
Walk through the seven-stage lifecycle using the visual pipeline slide. Pause at each stage to ask: "What do you think the homeowner is feeling right here?" This makes the content human and relatable before the technical explanation.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's what the PTO stage looks like from the homeowner's perspective: Their panels have been on the roof for three weeks. Their neighbor's system was live the day of installation. They're calling the company every day. They're getting voicemails. Nobody told them this stage exists. By the time someone calls them back, they've already told two people at work that solar is a scam. That's the referral that never happened. The commission that's at risk. And it all started with one conversation that didn't happen at the signing table."

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 5.5A-3 (At-the-Table Timeline Setting) — Have reps write their timeline-setting script, then pair up and deliver it to a partner at role-play speed. Give feedback on tone and completeness.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Your job doesn't end at the close. The close is the beginning of a 3-to-6-month trust relationship. The reps who understand that — who stay in contact, who set expectations, who show up at PTO — those are the reps who build referral businesses. That's the goal."

### Module 5.5b: Referral Science: Self-Sustaining Pipeline

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 5.5B — Common Installation Pipeline Issues

**Insert after Module 5.5A | Day 5 — Closing, Retention & Project Lifecycle Awareness**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Even the best sales reps close deals that run into trouble. Roof problems, electrical panel surprises, permit corrections, HOA delays — these are not rare. They are routine in the solar industry. Module 5.5B trains reps to recognize the most common pipeline issues by stage, understand why they happen, and respond to them without panic, blame, or false promises.

### Why It Matters
A rep who doesn't know why a deal stalled can't help save it. A rep who knows what's happening — and can explain it calmly and accurately to the homeowner — is the difference between a canceled deal and a preserved relationship. Pipeline issue awareness is a direct revenue protection skill.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module follows 5.5A's lifecycle overview and provides the "what can go wrong" layer. It sets up 5.5C (Escalation Paths) and 5.5D (Save-the-Deal Communication) directly. Reps need to know what the issues are before they can learn how to escalate or communicate around them.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Identify the most common installation pipeline issues by lifecycle stage.
2. Understand at a basic level why each issue occurs — without needing to become an expert in roofing, electrical, or permitting.
3. Communicate about pipeline issues honestly and clearly with homeowners — without creating panic or assigning blame.
4. Recognize when a competitor may re-enter during a delay — and know how to counter it.
5. Distinguish between issues that are normal/expected and issues that require immediate escalation.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — The Mindset: Issues Are Normal, Silence Is Not
The first thing reps need to internalize about pipeline issues is this: most of them are normal. They happen on a significant percentage of deals in most markets. The issue itself is rarely what kills a deal. What kills deals is poor communication around the issue.

**Key principle:** An informed homeowner who knows there's a problem and knows the next step is dramatically less likely to cancel than a surprised homeowner who hears nothing.

### Section 2 — Issues by Stage

**Stage: Site Survey**

*Issue: Roof Condition Failure*
- What it is: The site survey reveals the roof is in poor condition, has less than the required remaining lifespan, or has structural issues that need to be addressed before solar installation.
- Why it happens: Sales visits don't always include a roof inspection. Survey teams find what wasn't visible from the street.
- Impact: Installation cannot proceed until the roof is repaired or replaced. This can add significant cost and delay.
- Rep communication approach: "When we surveyed your roof, our team found [issue]. This is actually a protection for you — we don't want to put panels on a roof that will need repair in a few years. Here's what needs to happen next..."

*Issue: Shade Discovery*
- What it is: On-site shade analysis reveals more shading than the initial sales assessment estimated, potentially reducing system production significantly.
- Why it happens: Shade from trees, chimneys, or neighboring structures can be difficult to assess from the street or satellite imagery alone.
- Impact: May require system redesign, production estimate revision, or in some cases tree trimming.
- Rep communication approach: Honest production expectation reset; involve operations before talking to homeowner.

---

**Stage: System Design / Redesign**

*Issue: Panel Placement Changes*
- What it is: Engineering cannot place panels on the originally planned roof sections due to structural, shading, or setback issues.
- Why it happens: Site survey data often reveals constraints not visible during the sales visit.
- Impact: System may be smaller than originally proposed, potentially changing production estimates and economics.
- Rep communication approach: "The engineering team reviewed the survey data and made some adjustments to maximize what your specific roof can do. Here's what the updated design looks like..."

*Issue: Main Panel Upgrade (MPU) Required*
- What it is: The home's existing electrical service panel cannot safely support the solar system and/or battery addition. An upgrade is required before installation can proceed.
- Why it happens: Older homes often have 100-amp service panels. Modern solar + battery systems often require 200-amp service.
- Impact: Significant additional cost (often $1,500–$5,000+) and 2–6 weeks of additional timeline depending on permitting and utility scheduling.
- This is one of the most common surprise costs in the solar industry and one of the most common deal killers.
- Rep communication approach: "Our team identified something that actually protects your home and your system — your current electrical panel needs to be upgraded before we can safely install. Here's what that means for the project..."
- **Important:** If the rep had visibility into 100-amp service at the sales visit, this should have been flagged earlier. Module 1.7A covers site engineering awareness for this reason.

---

**Stage: Permitting**

*Issue: Permit Correction / Resubmission*
- What it is: The local jurisdiction reviews the permit application and requests corrections, additional documentation, or design changes before approving.
- Why it happens: Every jurisdiction has different requirements. Permit examiners have varying levels of solar familiarity. Requirements change.
- Impact: Additional 2–6 weeks added to the permit stage.
- Rep communication approach: "The permit office came back with a few items they want clarified — this is normal for solar projects. Our team is handling it and I'll update you when it's resubmitted."

*Issue: HOA Delay or Rejection*
- What it is: Homeowner's HOA has an approval process for exterior modifications — including solar. The HOA may require a separate application, aesthetic review, or specific panel placement.
- Why it happens: HOAs have independent authority over exterior modifications. State solar access laws vary, and while many states protect homeowners' right to install solar, HOA processes still add time.
- Impact: 2–8 weeks of additional delay; in rare cases, design modifications required.
- Rep communication approach: "Your HOA has a review process for solar installations. Many states actually protect your right to install — but the HOA still has a process we need to follow. Our team is managing that with you."

---

**Stage: Financing**

*Issue: Financing Stipulations*
- What it is: The financing company places conditions on the loan that must be met before funds are disbursed — such as proof of income, updated credit check, or property verification.
- Why it happens: Lenders conduct verification at multiple stages. Conditions are common and usually manageable.
- Impact: Delay in project authorization until stipulations are cleared.
- Rep communication approach: "Our finance team is working through a few standard verification items with your lender. These are routine — here's what they'll need from you..."

---

**Stage: Installation**

*Issue: Equipment Delays or Backorders*
- What it is: Specific equipment (panels, inverters, batteries) is on backorder or has a longer lead time than anticipated.
- Why it happens: Supply chain variability, high demand periods, or product substitutions.
- Impact: Installation pushed back by 2–8 weeks.
- Rep communication approach: "Equipment scheduling sometimes shifts — your install team wants to make sure your system has everything ready before they show up. I'll keep you updated as we get closer."

---

**Stage: Inspection**

*Issue: Failed Inspection*
- What it is: The local inspector identifies a code issue, installation problem, or documentation gap that prevents passing inspection.
- Why it happens: Varies. Can be crew error, code interpretation differences, or documentation issues.
- Impact: Corrections must be made and a re-inspection scheduled — often 2–4 additional weeks.
- Rep communication approach: "The inspector flagged one item that the crew needs to correct before we get the approval. This is a quality control step that protects you — our team is already scheduling the correction."

---

**Stage: PTO (Post-Inspection)**

*Issue: Utility PTO Delay*
- What it is: After passing inspection, the utility takes longer than expected to approve the interconnection and issue Permission to Operate.
- Why it happens: Utility processing times vary significantly by company, region, and time of year. Some utilities have long backlogs.
- Impact: System is installed and inspected but cannot be turned on. 2–8 weeks of frustration.
- Rep communication approach: "Your system passed inspection — that's the big one. Now it's in the utility's hands for final authorization. [Utility name] typically processes these in X–Y weeks. I'll reach out the moment we have your PTO."

---

**Stage: Any Stage**

*Issue: Competitor Re-Entry During Delays*
- What it is: While a homeowner's project is delayed, a competitor approaches them — often with promises of faster timelines, lower prices, or better equipment.
- Why it happens: Competitors actively prospect neighborhoods with solar activity. Delays create vulnerability.
- Impact: Deal cancellation risk; homeowner cancels to start over with the competitor.
- Prevention strategy: Consistent rep communication throughout the pipeline makes competitor re-entry much harder. A homeowner who trusts their rep and understands their project status is not a prospect for a competitor.
- Rep communication approach if it happens: "I hear you — let me make sure you have all the information you need to compare these options clearly. Here's where your project is today and what the finish line looks like from here..."

### Section 3 — Normal vs. Escalation-Level Issues
Reps should develop a sense for which issues are "this happens, here's the update" and which issues require urgent escalation to management or operations leadership.

**Normal — handle with communication:**
- Permit correction requiring resubmission
- HOA review process
- Equipment scheduling shift
- PTO processing wait
- Minor design adjustment

**Escalation-level — involve manager or ops leadership:**
- MPU discovered with no prior disclosure to homeowner (cost surprise)
- Failed inspection with unclear resolution path
- Homeowner explicitly threatening cancellation
- Competitor has already entered the picture and homeowner is wavering
- Financing stipulation the homeowner cannot meet

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The Main Panel Upgrade (MPU) issue is the most impactful surprise cost in the pipeline. Every rep should know what it is, why it happens, and how to communicate it. This is also the best argument for site engineering awareness (Module 1.7A).
- The competitor re-entry issue is not just a communication problem — it's a relationship problem. Reps who stay close to homeowners throughout the pipeline make themselves nearly impossible to displace.
- Framing matters enormously when communicating about pipeline issues. "We found a problem" versus "Our team found something that actually protects your home" lands completely differently.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "If there's a problem, I should wait until I have a full answer before calling the homeowner." → Wrong. Call immediately. "I know something came up, I'm working on getting you the full picture, and I'll call you back by [time]" is far better than silence.
- "The MPU is operations' problem to explain." → The rep owns the homeowner relationship. They don't have to know the technical details, but they need to make the call.
- "If I tell the homeowner about a problem, they'll cancel." → The opposite is usually true. Homeowners cancel when they feel abandoned or surprised. They stay when they feel informed and supported.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Reps do not need to know the technical details behind every issue type. They need to know the issue exists, what it means for the timeline, and how to talk about it. Operations handles the technical resolution.
- Don't turn this into a construction training. Keep it rep-focused and communication-focused.

**Where to slow down:**
- The MPU section. Make sure every rep understands what it is and can explain it to a homeowner. This surprises homeowners more than almost anything else in the pipeline.
- The competitor re-entry section. Have reps share experiences — either as reps or as customers who were re-approached during a delay. The emotional resonance makes the lesson stick.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "Nobody Told Me About the Panel Upgrade"
**Homeowner type:** Frustrated, feeling blindsided and financially stressed
**Setup:** Six weeks after signing, the homeowner calls in distress: "Your company just told me I need a $3,500 panel upgrade before you can install. I signed a contract based on a certain price. This wasn't disclosed. I want to know what my options are."
**Skill being tested:** Handling a cost-surprise situation with empathy, accuracy, and deal-preservation language — without making unauthorized financial promises.
**Pass criteria:** Rep empathizes genuinely, explains what an MPU is and why it's required, does not promise to waive the cost, escalates to the right person for the financial resolution, and keeps the homeowner in the relationship while that happens.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The Permit Has Been Pending for Three Months"
**Homeowner type:** Patient initially, now exhausted and questioning the whole decision
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "It's been almost three months and you keep telling me the permit is still pending. My kids are going back to school and I was hoping to be saving on bills by now. I don't know how much longer I can wait."
**Skill being tested:** Acknowledging the real emotional cost of a long permit delay, providing an honest status update, and finding a way to rebuild patience and commitment without making promises about permit timelines.
**Pass criteria:** Rep validates the frustration genuinely, provides the most accurate current status available, does not give a specific promise on permit timing, reinforces the value of staying the course, and establishes a specific next communication point.

---

### Scenario 3 — "The Competitor Came Back"
**Homeowner type:** Wavering, comparing options under delay stress
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "Another company came by. They said they could get us live in 45 days. We're already 10 weeks into this and not even permitted yet. We're seriously considering switching."
**Skill being tested:** Competitor re-entry handling during a delay — calm, honest, non-defensive comparison and deal protection.
**Pass criteria:** Rep does not disparage the competitor, helps the homeowner understand what "starting over" actually means (new contract, new survey, new permit), provides an honest timeline comparison, and makes the strongest possible case for staying — without pressuring.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner's site survey reveals their electrical panel is 100-amp service. Why does this matter for the solar project?
- A) It has no impact on solar installation
- B) A 100-amp panel may need to be upgraded to 200-amp service to safely support the solar system, which can add cost and time to the project
- C) 100-amp panels always need to be replaced, and the homeowner knew this at signing
- D) This only matters if the homeowner is adding a battery
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
A homeowner's HOA requires an approval process for solar installation. The homeowner is frustrated and wants to know why it's taking so long. Write a 2–3 sentence response that is honest, calm, and preserves trust.

**3. Multiple Choice**
A competitor approaches a homeowner whose project has been delayed for 8 weeks. The most effective rep response is to:
- A) Tell the homeowner the competitor is lying about their timeline
- B) Tell the homeowner to ignore the competitor and trust the process
- C) Help the homeowner understand what starting over actually means and where their current project stands — without disparaging the competitor
- D) Offer to reduce the price to match the competitor
*(Correct answer: C)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner calls and says their inspection failed. They're frustrated. They don't know what a failed inspection means. What should the rep say?
*(Expected answer: Acknowledge the frustration, explain that a failed inspection means the crew needs to correct a specific item before getting re-approved — frame it as a quality control step. Confirm the correction is being scheduled. Give a timeline estimate if available. Don't assign blame to the crew.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
The pipeline issue most likely to catch a homeowner completely off guard — and the one reps should set expectations for earliest — is the _____________, which can add significant cost and _____________ to _____________ weeks of additional timeline.
*(Correct: Main Panel Upgrade / MPU; 2; 6)*

---

## 8. Slide Deck Outline

1. **Title Slide** — "Common Installation Pipeline Issues: Know What's Coming"
2. **The Mindset** — Issues are normal. Silence is not.
3. **Issues at Survey** — Roof condition, shade discovery
4. **Issues at Design** — Panel placement changes, Main Panel Upgrade (MPU)
5. **Issues at Permitting** — Permit corrections, HOA delays
6. **Issues at Financing** — Stipulations and verification
7. **Issues at Installation & Inspection** — Equipment delays, failed inspection
8. **Issues at PTO** — Utility processing delays
9. **The Competitor Re-Entry Problem** — How to handle it, how to prevent it

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"I want to start with a question. How many of you have had a deal go sideways in the pipeline — after the close? [Hands.] And how many of you knew it was coming before the homeowner called you about it? [Fewer hands.] That gap — between the problem happening and the rep knowing about it — is where deals die. Today we're going to close that gap."

**Concept Explanation (15 min)**
Walk through the issues by stage. Don't rush. For each major issue (especially MPU and competitor re-entry), stop and ask the room: "Has anyone seen this? What happened?" Use their real experiences as case studies.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's how the MPU conversation goes wrong: Operations calls the homeowner directly with the cost surprise. The rep finds out from the homeowner's angry voicemail. The homeowner feels misled. The rep feels blindsided. The deal is now at serious risk — and the rep didn't even know about the issue before the homeowner did. Now here's how it goes right: The rep gets the heads-up first, calls the homeowner before the official notice goes out, and frames the conversation."

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 5.5B-3 (MPU Explanation Practice) — write the response individually, then deliver it to a partner as a live phone call simulation. Time it: it should be deliverable in under 3 minutes.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Every one of these issues is survivable if the rep communicates well. Most of these issues kill deals only when the homeowner hears about them from operations — not from their rep. Own the communication. Own the relationship. That's how you protect your pipeline."

### Module 5.5c: Post-Sale Communication & Reviews

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 5.5C — Troubleshooting & Escalation Paths

**Insert after Module 5.5B | Day 5 — Closing, Retention & Project Lifecycle Awareness**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Knowing what's wrong isn't enough. Reps need to know what to do about it — specifically, who to contact, what information to have ready, and how to get resolution moving without creating more chaos. Module 5.5C gives reps a structured approach to troubleshooting and escalation so they can act decisively when deals run into trouble.

### Why It Matters
The rep who escalates with clarity gets faster results. The rep who escalates with panic or incomplete information adds to the problem. When a homeowner is frustrated and a deal is at risk, the rep's job is to become the most organized person in the room — internally and externally.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module follows 5.5B's issue identification training and provides the "now what?" framework. It feeds directly into 5.5D (Save-the-Deal Communication), which covers the homeowner-facing side of deal rescue. 5.5C is the internal side: how reps navigate their own company when things go wrong.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Identify which department or role owns resolution for each common type of pipeline issue.
2. Know what information to gather before escalating — so the escalation is efficient and taken seriously.
3. Determine when to involve their manager vs. when to handle the situation directly.
4. Know what not to say to the homeowner while a resolution is in process.
5. Use a clear escalation structure without creating internal conflict or overstepping their role.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — Issue Identification: What Kind of Problem Is This?
Before escalating, the rep needs to correctly categorize what's happening. Different types of issues route to different departments and have different urgency levels.

**Category A — Design or Technical Issue**
Examples: Roof condition failure, panel placement revision, MPU requirement, shading problem.
Who owns it: Engineering / Site Assessment team.
What info to gather: Survey date, original design specs, specific issue identified.

**Category B — Permit or Jurisdiction Issue**
Examples: Permit correction, HOA delay, code interpretation dispute.
Who owns it: Permitting team.
What info to gather: Permit application date, jurisdiction name, specific correction requested, HOA contact or requirements.

**Category C — Financing or Contract Issue**
Examples: Loan stipulation, lender verification hold, contract modification needed.
Who owns it: Finance team or contract administration.
What info to gather: Loan application status, specific stipulation or hold reason, homeowner's preferred contact for finance questions.

**Category D — Installation or Inspection Issue**
Examples: Equipment delay, failed inspection, crew scheduling issue.
Who owns it: Operations / Installation management.
What info to gather: Scheduled install date, specific inspection failure item, equipment on order (if applicable).

**Category E — Customer Relationship or Cancellation Risk**
Examples: Homeowner threatening to cancel, competitor re-entry, homeowner is unreachable, trust has broken down.
Who owns it: Rep first, manager if rep is unable to stabilize.
What info to gather: Full deal history, homeowner's stated reason for frustration, last contact date and outcome, current project stage.

### Section 2 — Information to Gather Before Escalating
A well-prepared escalation resolves faster and gets more attention from operations and management. An unprepared escalation wastes everyone's time and delays resolution.

**The Pre-Escalation Checklist:**
Before escalating any issue, the rep should have:
- [ ] Homeowner name and deal/project ID
- [ ] Current project stage
- [ ] Specific issue description (what happened, when discovered, who communicated it)
- [ ] What the homeowner knows so far
- [ ] What the homeowner's current emotional state is (frustrated, patient, threatening cancellation)
- [ ] What the rep has already communicated or promised
- [ ] What resolution the rep believes is needed
- [ ] Whether there is a deadline (e.g., homeowner has given a decision window)

Teaching point: Walking into a manager's office or sending an urgent email with "the homeowner is upset, can you help?" is not escalation — it's a panic relay. Walking in with the pre-escalation checklist completed is escalation.

### Section 3 — When to Involve Your Manager
Reps should handle the vast majority of pipeline communication themselves. There are specific situations where manager involvement is required or strongly advisable.

**Involve your manager when:**
- The homeowner is explicitly threatening cancellation and the rep cannot stabilize the situation
- A cost surprise (especially MPU) is going to require a financial accommodation or negotiation
- The homeowner has been unreachable for more than 10 business days
- A competitor has entered the picture and the homeowner is wavering
- An internal team has not responded to a rep's escalation within 48 hours on a time-sensitive deal
- The issue involves a potential compliance or contract dispute

**Handle at rep level:**
- Routine pipeline updates and stage communications
- Initial response to permitting delays (information, not resolution)
- First contact after a failed inspection (information, not resolution)
- Standard timeline expectation resets

### Section 4 — Department Contact Structure
*Note to trainers: This section must be localized for your company's specific team structure and contact protocols before delivery. The framework below is representative.*

**Rep's escalation path (general):**
1. Rep handles homeowner communication directly.
2. If technical or operational issue: Rep contacts project coordinator or operations point of contact with pre-escalation checklist completed.
3. If no response within agreed-upon SLA (e.g., 24–48 business hours): Rep escalates to manager.
4. If manager-level engagement needed: Rep and manager connect before manager contacts homeowner or operations.
5. If deal is at serious cancellation risk: Escalation goes to sales manager or customer success team (if your company has one).

**Teaching point:** The rep should not route around their manager directly to senior leadership on most issues — this creates relationship and authority problems. The exception is a true emergency (homeowner is in immediate distress, a safety concern, etc.).

### Section 5 — What Not to Say to the Homeowner While a Resolution Is in Process
This is one of the most important — and most commonly violated — escalation principles.

**Do NOT say:**
- "I'm not sure what's happening — I'm trying to find out." → Creates doubt without providing comfort. Replace with: "I'm getting you a clear answer by [specific time]."
- "Operations is dropping the ball on this." → Assigns blame internally. Destroys homeowner confidence in the company.
- "I'll see what I can do." → Non-committal and vague. Replace with a specific action and timeframe.
- "This isn't my department." → True but unhelpful. Rep owns the relationship regardless.
- "Don't worry about it — everything will be fine." → Dismissive. Homeowner's concern is valid.

**DO say:**
- "I'm working on getting you a specific answer by [time]. I'll call you back then regardless of what I know."
- "I want to make sure I get you the right information — let me loop in the right person on my team so we can give you a clear update today."
- "I hear you — and I'm not going to leave you without a response. Here's what happens next..."
- "I understand this isn't what you were expecting. I'm taking ownership of this right now."

### Section 6 — The Escalation Call Structure
When escalating to a manager or internal team, the rep should follow a clear structure to be taken seriously and get fast results:

**The four-part escalation message:**
1. **Who:** Homeowner name, deal ID, project stage
2. **What:** Specific issue — describe it factually, not emotionally
3. **State:** Homeowner's current emotional state and cancellation risk level
4. **Ask:** What specific action or response is needed, by when

Example: "I need help with the [Homeowner Name] deal — project ID [X]. They're at the permitting stage, currently in week 10 of the permit wait. The homeowner is frustrated and has told me they're 'thinking about their options.' I need an updated permit status from the permitting team by end of day today so I can call them back with something concrete. If I don't have an update, I think this deal is at real cancellation risk."

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The pre-escalation checklist is the rep's most important internal tool in a deal-at-risk situation. Reps who show up to an escalation prepared get faster responses and better results.
- The language guardrails ("what not to say") need to be taken seriously. Reps who blame operations to homeowners create permanent trust damage.
- Remind reps that their manager wants to know about at-risk deals early — not after the cancellation has already happened.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "I should figure it out myself before bothering my manager." → Sometimes true. But waiting too long to escalate a cancellation-risk deal costs the rep their commission.
- "If I tell the homeowner I'm escalating internally, they'll lose confidence." → The opposite is usually true. "I'm bringing in [role] to make sure we get this right for you" builds confidence.
- "I can just email operations and hope they respond." → Email without a specific ask and deadline gets deprioritized. Escalations need clarity: who, what, when.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- This module is about process and communication, not technical problem-solving. Reps are not expected to fix permit issues or redesign systems. They are expected to navigate their internal resources effectively.
- The department structure section must be localized. Don't deliver a generic org chart — make it your company's actual escalation path.

**Where to slow down:**
- The "what not to say" section. Read each phrase and discuss why it fails. Have reps suggest better alternatives before revealing the correct language. This is active learning, not passive absorption.
- The four-part escalation message. Have reps practice writing it for a real or hypothetical deal before the session ends.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "I Need Answers by Tomorrow"
**Homeowner type:** Analytical, organized, has given the rep a deadline
**Setup:** A homeowner calls and says: "I've been waiting three months and I still don't have a permit. My husband and I have decided — if we don't have a clear answer by Friday, we're going to cancel and go with someone else. I need you to tell me exactly what's happening."
**Skill being tested:** Triggering the escalation path correctly — gathering the right information, communicating clearly with the homeowner about what happens next, and escalating with urgency internally.
**Pass criteria:** Rep does not panic, does not make a promise about the permit timeline, commits to a specific callback time with a concrete status update, and correctly identifies this as a manager-level escalation situation.

---

### Scenario 2 — "Can You Just Call Operations for Me?"
**Homeowner type:** Non-confrontational, doesn't want to be the one pushing
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "I don't want to cause any trouble, but I'm getting worried. The install was supposed to be this month and I haven't heard anything. I know you're busy — can you just check on it for me?"
**Skill being tested:** Taking ownership of the homeowner's concern and escalating appropriately — without making the homeowner feel like a burden and without making promises about operations scheduling.
**Pass criteria:** Rep enthusiastically takes ownership, gathers the relevant project information, follows up with operations using the pre-escalation framework, and reports back to the homeowner within a committed timeframe.

---

### Scenario 3 — "I Called Your Office and They Said They Didn't Know"
**Homeowner type:** Frustrated, feeling lost in the system
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "I called your main office number and the person I talked to said they didn't have any information about my account. I've been trying to get answers for two weeks. I don't know who I'm supposed to be talking to."
**Skill being tested:** Taking ownership after an internal communication failure — not defending the company, not blaming the other rep or team member, and re-establishing the rep as the homeowner's single point of contact.
**Pass criteria:** Rep takes full ownership without defensiveness, establishes themselves as the single point of contact going forward, gathers the full status of the homeowner's project immediately, and delivers a clear update within a committed timeframe.

---

- A) Is honest and appropriate
- B) Creates doubt without providing comfort — the rep should replace it with a specific action and timeframe
- C) Is the correct approach because transparency builds trust
- D) Is only a problem if the homeowner reacts badly
*(Correct answer: B)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A rep has escalated a permitting issue to the operations team via email three days ago and has heard nothing back. The homeowner has called twice. What should the rep do?
*(Expected answer: The rep should escalate to their manager immediately. Three days with no response on a time-sensitive deal is not acceptable. The manager should be looped in with the four-part escalation message. The rep should also call the homeowner with a "I'm working on your behalf to get a clear answer — I'll have something for you by [specific time]" update.)*

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

"Think about the last time you had a deal that was in trouble. Did you know exactly who to call? Did you know what to say to the homeowner while you were working on it? We're going to build a system so that when a deal is at risk, you're the most organized person in the room."

**Concept Explanation (12–15 min)**
Walk through the five issue categories and the department routing for each. Pause on Category E (customer relationship) to reinforce that the rep owns this one — it doesn't route to operations. Walk through the pre-escalation checklist and the four-part escalation message structure.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's the difference between a good and bad escalation. Bad: [Rep rushes into manager's office] 'The Santos deal is in trouble — the homeowner is upset and I don't know what's going on.' Good: [Rep schedules a 10-minute meeting] 'I need to flag a cancellation risk — the Santos deal, project ID 4821. They're at week 12 of permitting. The homeowner called twice this week and said they need answers by Friday or they're canceling. I need a current permit status from the permitting team today so I can make a credible call. Can you help me get that?'"

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 5.5C-4 (Write the Escalation Message) — have reps write their message for the Maria Santos scenario, then read it aloud to a partner who plays the role of manager. The partner gives one piece of feedback.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Escalation isn't failure. Escalation isn't admitting you can't handle something. Escalation is the skill of knowing when you need more firepower and knowing how to ask for it in a way that gets results. The reps who master this protect more deals — and get more help when they need it."

### Module 5.5d: Change Order Management

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 5.5D — Save-the-Deal Communication

**Insert after Module 5.5C | Day 5 — Closing, Retention & Project Lifecycle Awareness**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Module 5.5D is the most human of the four Day 5 V2 modules. After understanding the pipeline (5.5A), knowing what can go wrong (5.5B), and knowing how to escalate internally (5.5C), reps now need to learn how to face the homeowner directly when a deal is under friction — and bring it back. This module is about communication, empathy, and the specific language skills that preserve trust and protect revenue when projects hit real-world turbulence.

### Why It Matters
The solar industry's biggest revenue leak is not lost sales — it is lost deals that were already closed. Every cancellation after the close is a commission lost, a referral that won't happen, and a homeowner who may speak negatively to neighbors. Reps who can save a deal mid-pipeline are protecting not just a single commission but an entire neighborhood's potential.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This is the final module in Day 5's post-sale block and the practical application layer for everything taught in 5.5A, 5.5B, and 5.5C. It also connects forward to Day 6's referral and pipeline discipline modules. Deal-saving communication is both a retention skill and a reputation-building skill.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Communicate about project delays clearly and empathetically — without blaming operations or making promises they can't keep.
2. Successfully handle a homeowner who has gone silent (ghosting) after the close.
3. Navigate spouse or family pushback that emerges after signing.
4. Respond to a competitor re-entry attempt during a delay — without panic or pressure.
5. Protect the homeowner relationship through friction in a way that turns the experience into a referral story rather than a cancellation.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — The Foundational Principle: Own the Communication, Not the Problem
The most important thing a rep needs to understand about save-the-deal communication is this distinction:

**The rep does not own the operational problem.**
The rep does not control permit timelines, utility queues, or roofing conditions.

**The rep does own the communication around the problem.**
The rep controls when and how the homeowner hears about it, how it's framed, how the homeowner is treated during it, and whether the relationship survives it.

When the rep conflates these two — when they feel responsible for fixing the operational problem — they either make promises they can't keep, or they avoid the homeowner entirely because they feel like failures. Both responses accelerate cancellations.

The rep's job is to be the homeowner's steady, honest, engaged partner through a difficult process. That is what saves deals.

### Section 2 — Communicating Delays Clearly
When a project hits a delay, the rep's first call should happen before the homeowner calls them — if at all possible.

**The proactive delay call framework:**

**Step 1 — Lead with acknowledgment, not defense.**
"I'm calling because I have an update on your project — and I want to give it to you directly rather than have you hear it through the pipeline."

**Step 2 — State what happened factually and simply.**
"The permit office has come back with a correction request. That means our team needs to resubmit, and it's going to add approximately [X] weeks to the permitting stage."

**Step 3 — Validate the impact.**
"I know that's not what either of us wanted to hear. I want to acknowledge that this extends the timeline and that's genuinely frustrating."

**Step 4 — Provide the next concrete step.**
"Here's what happens next: our permitting team is already working on the correction. I'll update you by [specific date] with where that stands."

**Step 5 — Reaffirm the relationship.**
"And regardless of what comes up in the process — I'm your point of contact. If you have questions or you hear something that worries you, call me first."

**Teaching point:** This five-step framework works for almost any delay communication. The specific issue changes; the structure stays the same.

### Section 3 — Handling Homeowner Ghosting
One of the more unsettling post-close experiences for reps is when a homeowner who was enthusiastic at signing goes completely silent. Calls go to voicemail. Texts aren't answered. The rep doesn't know if the homeowner is still in — or already shopping around.

**Why homeowners ghost post-close:**
- Buyer's remorse that they haven't fully processed
- Spouse or family member has raised concerns they don't want to surface
- Life events (job change, family situation, financial concern) that feel embarrassing to bring up
- They've been contacted by a competitor and are "keeping their options open"
- They simply got busy and the rep's calls feel like pressure

**The ghost-handling framework:**

**Attempt 1 — Warm, no-pressure check-in (call or text):**
"Hey [name] — just wanted to touch base and make sure everything is going smoothly on your end. Nothing urgent, just keeping you updated. Give me a call when you have a few minutes."

**Attempt 2 — Value-add outreach (if no response to Attempt 1 within 5 business days):**
"Wanted to share a quick update on where your project stands: [brief factual status]. Happy to answer any questions you have. You can always reach me at [number]."

**Attempt 3 — Honest, direct outreach (if no response to Attempt 2 within 5 business days):**
"[Name] — I want to make sure I'm serving you well here. I haven't been able to reach you and I want to make sure everything is okay on your end. If something has come up or if you have any concerns, I'd rather know about it so we can work through it together. Please give me a call."

**If no response after three attempts:** Escalate to manager for guidance. Do not continue calling in a way that feels like pressure. Some homeowners need manager-level outreach; some need a pause.

**Teaching point:** Ghosting is almost always an emotional situation — not a logistical one. The rep who responds with warmth, no pressure, and a clear door for the homeowner to raise concerns is far more likely to re-open the conversation than the rep who escalates to urgency.

### Section 4 — Handling Spouse or Family Pushback Post-Close
One of the most common post-close scenarios: the homeowner who signed is fully committed, but a spouse, family member, or trusted advisor has raised concerns — and now the deal is at risk.

**Why this happens:**
- The non-signing spouse was not at the table or was not fully engaged during the presentation
- A family member with "expertise" (an accountant, a contractor, a neighbor) has questioned the numbers
- The homeowner did more research and found contradictory information online
- Financial anxiety surfaced after signing when the full scope of the commitment felt real

**The spouse/family pushback framework:**

**Step 1 — Do not get defensive.**
"I completely understand. These are important decisions and it makes total sense that your [spouse/family member] wants to weigh in."

**Step 2 — Find out what the specific concern is.**
"Can you help me understand what's coming up for them? I want to make sure I address the right thing."

**Step 3 — Offer a direct conversation — not a re-presentation.**
"I'd love to connect with both of you — not to go through everything again, but just to answer whatever questions are on the table. Would a 20-minute call work?"

**Step 4 — Address the concern specifically and honestly.**
If the concern is financial: revisit the numbers honestly. If the concern is about a third-party opinion: "That's a fair thing to verify. Here's how I'd encourage them to think about it..."

**Step 5 — Give the homeowner space if needed — but keep the door open.**
"I don't want you to feel any pressure. This should be a decision you both feel good about. I'm available to answer anything — and if you decide to move forward, we're right there with you."

**Teaching point:** The goal of the spouse-pushback conversation is not to "re-close" — it is to provide honest answers to legitimate questions. Reps who pressure into re-closing post-pushback are the leading cause of post-install buyer's remorse and cancellation.

### Section 5 — Handling Competitor Re-Entry
When a competitor approaches a homeowner during a pipeline delay, the rep faces a high-stakes communication challenge: the homeowner has a live alternative in front of them.

**What NOT to do:**
- Disparage the competitor ("They have terrible reviews") — unverifiable and petty
- Panic and immediately offer a price reduction — signals desperation, not value
- Go silent or hope the competitor goes away — the worst possible response

**What TO do:**

**Step 1 — Acknowledge the offer without dismissing it.**
"I appreciate you telling me about that. That's a significant decision and you should absolutely weigh your options."

**Step 2 — Help the homeowner understand the real cost of switching.**
"Before you make a decision, I want to make sure you have all the information. Switching to a new company means starting over — a new contract, a new site survey, a new permit application. In most markets, that resets your timeline by at least three to four months."

**Step 3 — Be honest about where they are in the current project.**
"Here's where you are right now: [specific stage]. From here, here's realistically what the path to completion looks like: [honest timeline]."

**Step 4 — Let the homeowner make an informed decision.**
"I want you to make the decision that's right for you. I'm not going to pressure you. But I want to make sure you're comparing the full picture — not just the price."

**Step 5 — Reinforce the relationship without leverage.**
"Whatever you decide, I'm here. If you have questions about either option, I'm happy to help you think through it."

**Teaching point:** The competitor re-entry conversation is won on honesty, not pressure. Reps who try to "out-sell" the competitor usually lose. Reps who help the homeowner think clearly about their real situation usually win.

### Section 6 — Protecting Trust Without Blaming Operations
Throughout every save-the-deal scenario, the rep must maintain one non-negotiable: they do not assign blame to their own operations team — even when operations is responsible for the delay.

**Why this matters:**
- Blaming operations destroys homeowner confidence in the company — not just in operations
- It makes the rep look disorganized and out of control
- It creates an adversarial dynamic that is very hard to recover from

**The "own without blame" framework:**
- "Our team" owns the issue — not "they" or "operations" or specific individuals
- "We're working on this" — not "I'm trying to figure out why they haven't done this"
- "I'm taking personal ownership of getting you an answer" — rep steps forward, doesn't point backward

**Example language:**
"Our team ran into an issue with the permit submission that we're correcting now. I want to own this — I should have had a more specific update for you sooner. Here's where things stand and here's what I'm doing to make sure we move forward."

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The foundational principle (own the communication, not the problem) needs to be said explicitly and drilled before any of the specific frameworks. If reps internalize this principle, the specific scripts become intuitive rather than mechanical.
- The spouse/family pushback section is critically important and rarely covered in sales training. The instinct to "re-close" is almost always wrong. Lean into this point.
- The ghosting framework is counterintuitive for reps who are used to aggressive follow-up. Emphasize that warmth and low pressure are more effective than urgency in post-close ghost situations.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "If I tell the homeowner I don't control the timeline, they'll lose confidence in me." → Honesty about what the rep can and cannot control actually builds trust. The illusion of control collapses fast — and when it does, trust goes with it.
- "I should re-present to the resistant spouse to close them again." → This almost always makes things worse. A conversation to answer questions is very different from a re-presentation.
- "If I don't respond to competitor re-entry aggressively, I'll lose the deal." → Aggression confirms the homeowner's doubts. Calm, organized honesty is what wins.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- The five-step delay call framework should be practiced until it feels natural — not memorized word-for-word. Reps need to internalize the structure, not recite a script.
- The ghosting framework is about tone and cadence, not specific word choices. Reps should adapt the language to their own voice.

**Where to slow down:**
- Section 1 (own the communication, not the problem). This is the foundation. Don't rush past it. Ask reps to articulate what it means in their own words before moving forward.
- Section 6 (protect trust without blaming operations). Have reps identify a time they've heard a rep (or company employee) blame internal teams to a customer. Discuss what it felt like from the customer's side.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "My Wife Wants to Cancel"
**Homeowner type:** Committed but caught between his decision and his spouse's concerns
**Setup:** Four days after signing, the homeowner calls and says: "I'm still in, but my wife talked to her dad who's a contractor and he said solar never pays off. She wants to cancel. I don't know what to do."
**Skill being tested:** Navigating spouse pushback with empathy, not pressure — addressing a specific third-party objection honestly and creating space for a two-party conversation.
**Pass criteria:** Rep does not dismiss the father-in-law's opinion, asks to understand the specific concern, offers a direct low-pressure conversation with both spouses, addresses the "solar never pays off" objection with honest financial framing, and leaves the door open without pressuring.

---

### Scenario 2 — "I've Been Trying to Reach You for Two Weeks"
**Homeowner type:** Frustrated, feeling abandoned, close to canceling
**Setup:** A homeowner who has been unreachable for three weeks finally picks up and says: "I've actually been trying to reach your company too. Nobody calls me back. I'm paying interest on a loan for a system that isn't installed. I want to talk to someone who can tell me what's actually happening."
**Skill being tested:** Taking full ownership of a communication failure, recovering the relationship, and providing a clear and honest project status update — all in one call.
**Pass criteria:** Rep does not make excuses, takes personal ownership of the communication gap, gets the homeowner a current project status within a committed timeframe, and re-establishes themselves as the single point of contact going forward.

---

### Scenario 3 — "My Contractor Friend Said We Got Ripped Off"
**Homeowner type:** Homeowner is in, spouse is skeptical, outside "expert" has added doubt
**Setup:** Homeowner says: "My brother-in-law works in construction. He looked at our contract and said we paid too much and the equipment is cheap. My wife is now asking me if we made a mistake. I trust you, but I don't know what to tell her."
**Skill being tested:** Handling a price and equipment objection from a third-party influencer post-close — without becoming defensive, dismissing the concern, or re-presenting everything from scratch.
**Pass criteria:** Rep acknowledges the brother-in-law's perspective respectfully, addresses the price and equipment concerns with honest, factual language, offers a specific channel for verification (monitoring data, equipment specs, company reviews), and invites a direct conversation with the spouse to answer questions.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner hasn't responded to two calls and a text in 10 days. The best next action is:
- A) Call every day until they pick up
- B) Send a warm, low-pressure third outreach acknowledging you haven't been able to connect and inviting them to raise any concerns
- C) Mark the deal as lost and move on
- D) Immediately escalate to the manager to call them
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
What is the most important principle behind save-the-deal communication, and what does it mean for how a rep approaches a homeowner whose project is delayed?

**3. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner's spouse is resistant after signing. The best approach is:
- A) Re-present the full sales pitch to the spouse
- B) Offer to reduce the price to ease the concern
- C) Ask to understand the specific concern and offer a direct, low-pressure conversation to answer questions
- D) Tell the signed homeowner to handle it and stay out of it
*(Correct answer: C)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A competitor is offering a homeowner a lower price and a faster timeline during a delay. The homeowner is wavering. Write out the core of what the rep should say — including how they address the price difference and the timeline comparison.
*(Expected answer: Acknowledge the offer without dismissing it. Help the homeowner understand what switching actually means — new contract, new survey, new permit, full timeline reset. Be honest about where the current project stands and what the realistic path to completion looks like from here. Do not disparage the competitor. Do not offer to match the price without authorization. Let the homeowner make an informed decision.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
When a homeowner's project hits a delay, the rep should ideally make the delay call _____________ the homeowner calls them. The five-step framework for this call begins with leading with _____________, not defense.
*(Correct: before; acknowledgment)*

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"I want to talk about one of the most expensive moments in this business — and it's not a lost lead. It's a closed deal that cancels. Think about what that costs: the commission, the referral pipeline, the word-of-mouth in the neighborhood. Now think about this: almost every one of those cancellations had a moment where it could have been saved. A call that didn't happen. A conversation that went the wrong way. A competitor who got in the door because the rep had gone quiet. Today we're building the skills to win those moments."

**Concept Explanation (15 min)**
Lead with Section 1 (foundational principle) before any frameworks. Pause and have reps articulate what it means in their own words: "If you own the communication but not the problem — what does that change about how you feel when a permit is delayed?" Then walk through each scenario section, spending the most time on delay calls and spouse pushback.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's the ghosting scenario in real life. Rep closes a deal Friday. Doesn't call again. Three weeks later, a competitor knocks on the door. The homeowner, who has heard nothing from their rep, picks up the brochure. By the time the rep calls — which they finally do because operations flagged a site survey — the homeowner is already three conversations into a relationship with a new company. The rep lost a deal they had closed. What's the cost? The commission. The HOA referral they were about to ask for. The two neighbors on the street who would have been the next appointments. That's the real cost of going quiet."

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 5.5D-1 (Delay Call Script Practice) — have reps write their scripts individually, then deliver them live to a partner who plays the homeowner and is allowed to push back. Time the call: it should land within 3 minutes.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"The rep who shows up when things get hard — who calls first, who tells the truth, who stays engaged through the friction — is the rep that homeowners tell their neighbors about. Not 'they installed my solar.' But 'when things got complicated, they handled it.' That's the reputation that builds a referral business. That's what we're training toward."
