## DAY 6: Post-Sale Excellence & Career Growth
### Module 6.1: The Referral System

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 6.1A — Day 0 Referral & Reputation Protection

**Insert after existing Module 6.1 | Day 6 — Referrals, Performance & Operator Discipline**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Most reps think of referrals as something that happens after a project completes — a reward for a job well done. Module 6.1A reframes this entirely. The most powerful referral conversations happen on Day 0: the day the homeowner signs. That is the moment of highest emotional buy-in, before the pipeline delays, before the permit wait, before any friction enters the picture. Reps who know how to ask at the right moment — and protect that reputation through the lifecycle — build referral businesses. Reps who wait until PTO are leaving revenue on the table.

### Why It Matters
A single referral from a happy homeowner costs the rep almost nothing and produces a warm introduction that closes at dramatically higher rates than cold outreach. The solar business runs on trust, and trust is highest the moment someone decides to move forward. Reputation protection through the pipeline is what keeps that trust intact long enough for the referral to actually materialize.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module sits at the start of Day 6 and connects backward to the post-sale lifecycle work from Day 5 (Modules 5.5A–5.5D). It builds on the trust-through-transparency principles established in 5.5D and gives reps the practical language to turn that trust into referral conversations at every stage of the project — not just at PTO.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Identify the three highest-leverage moments in the project lifecycle to make a referral ask.
2. Deliver a natural, non-pressured referral ask at the moment of signing that converts consistently.
3. Use emotional buy-in moments throughout the pipeline (survey confirmation, install day, PTO) to generate additional referral conversations.
4. Protect their reputation actively during pipeline delays so trust remains intact for the referral ask.
5. Distinguish between referral asks that feel transactional and those that feel like a natural extension of trust.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — Why Day 0 Is the Most Powerful Referral Moment
The signing moment is the emotional peak of the homeowner's solar journey — at least until their first live bill. At this moment:
- The homeowner has just made a decision they feel good about
- They have expressed trust in the rep by committing financially
- Their enthusiasm is at its highest
- They haven't yet experienced any pipeline friction
- They are most likely to be thinking: "I should tell [name] about this"

This is the moment most reps waste by packing up and leaving.

**The Day 0 referral ask is not aggressive — it is appropriate.** The homeowner is already in a giving emotional state. A well-framed ask simply gives that impulse a direction.

**What makes a Day 0 referral ask work:**
- It comes after the signing is complete — not during it
- It is framed around who in their life would benefit — not who can help the rep's numbers
- It is specific (neighbors, family members, coworkers) — not generic ("anyone you know")
- It does not require an immediate answer — it plants a seed and gives the homeowner permission to think

**Simple Day 0 referral language:**
"Before I head out — now that you've decided to move forward, I want to make sure the people you care about have the same opportunity. Is there a neighbor on the street, or a family member, who you think would want to have this conversation? I'm not asking you to sell them anything — just to make an introduction if it makes sense."

### Section 2 — The Three Referral Moments in the Project Lifecycle
Beyond Day 0, there are three additional natural moments in the pipeline where referral conversations arise organically — without the rep having to force them.

**Moment 1 — Site Survey Confirmation**
Trigger: Rep calls to confirm the site survey went well and the design is moving forward.
Why it works: The homeowner feels progress. The project is real. Their confidence in the decision is reinforced.
Natural language: "Great news — the survey went smoothly and your design is moving forward. This is when I love calling customers because it's the moment where everything starts to become real. Quick question — has anyone else on the street noticed the survey crew? Sometimes neighbors get curious. I'm happy to answer questions if you want to just connect us."

**Moment 2 — Install Day**
Trigger: Rep checks in on installation day — or the day after.
Why it works: Seeing panels on the roof is the highest-visibility moment of the project. Neighbors notice. Family members come by. The homeowner is proud.
Natural language: "How's it looking? I love install day — it's when the whole neighborhood gets curious. If any neighbors have asked about it or if you want me to come back out to talk to anyone while the crew is still there, just say the word."

**Moment 3 — PTO (Permission to Operate)**
Trigger: The homeowner receives PTO and the system goes live.
Why it works: This is the emotional finish line — the moment the homeowner has been waiting for. They are at peak satisfaction.
Natural language: "Congratulations — you're officially live. This is the moment I've been working toward with you. I've genuinely enjoyed being part of this. One last thing — now that you've been through the whole process, who in your life do you think should have this experience? I'd love an introduction."

### Section 3 — Emotional Buy-In Moments: Reading the Room
Referral asks only work when the emotional climate is right. Reps who ask during pipeline friction — during a permit delay, after an MPU surprise, in the middle of a homeowner complaint — damage both the referral relationship and the primary relationship.

**Green light moments for referral asks:**
- Immediately after signing (Day 0)
- After a positive survey outcome
- When the homeowner proactively expresses excitement or gratitude
- On install day — when energy and visibility are both high
- At PTO — the emotional finish line

**Red light moments — do not ask for referrals:**
- During any pipeline delay or complaint conversation
- Immediately after delivering bad news (MPU, redesign, failed inspection)
- When the homeowner has gone quiet or seems disengaged
- When the deal is still at cancellation risk
- When the rep hasn't spoken to the homeowner in weeks

**Teaching point:** A referral ask is the rep demonstrating confidence in the relationship. If the relationship is under strain, asking for a referral signals tone-deafness. Protect the primary relationship first — the referral will follow.

### Section 4 — Referral Generation Through Trust
The reps with the highest referral rates are not the ones with the best referral scripts. They are the reps who do what they say, communicate proactively, and show up when things get hard. Referrals are the output of trust — scripts are the mechanism for inviting it.

**The trust behaviors that generate referrals:**
- Calling before the homeowner has to call you
- Being honest about delays before they become surprises
- Following through on every commitment made during the sale
- Staying present through the pipeline — not just at signing and PTO
- Being the first call the homeowner makes when something comes up

**The referral ask is simply permission.** The trust has already been built through behavior. The ask gives the homeowner a clear way to act on the positive feeling they already have about the rep.

**What referral conversations that feel transactional look like:**
- "Do you know anyone else who might want solar?"
- "I get a bonus for referrals so I'd really appreciate it"
- "If you know three people I can talk to, I'll give you a gift card"
- Any ask that leads with the rep's benefit rather than the homeowner's network

**What referral conversations that feel natural look like:**
- Starting with who in the homeowner's life would genuinely benefit
- Framing the rep as a resource — not a salesperson looking for leads
- Making the homeowner feel like an advisor to their network — not a recruiter

### Section 5 — Reputation Protection After the Close
Referrals are only as good as the reputation behind them. A homeowner whose project ran into problems — and whose rep disappeared — will not refer. A homeowner whose project ran into problems — and whose rep stayed present, communicated honestly, and helped resolve them — often becomes the most enthusiastic referral source because they've seen the rep at their best.

**The reputation protection behaviors:**
- Proactive communication through every pipeline stage (the 7-touchpoint model from Module 5.5A)
- Owning delays before the homeowner finds out from someone else
- Being honest when things don't go as planned — without blaming operations
- Following through after PTO: a post-live check-in 30 days after the system goes live is one of the highest-leverage reputation protection behaviors available to a rep
- Using social proof carefully: sharing monitoring data or production milestones is a natural excuse to re-engage and reinforce the relationship

**The 30-day post-PTO check-in:**
"Hey — just wanted to check in now that you've had a month on the system. How is it feeling? Are you seeing the difference on your bill yet? I'm here if anything comes up — and if you know anyone who should be having this conversation, I'd love the introduction."

This single touchpoint accomplishes four things: it reinforces the relationship, it catches any early system concerns before they become complaints, it opens a natural referral moment, and it positions the rep as a long-term partner rather than a transactional closer.

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The Day 0 referral ask is the single highest-leverage behavioral change reps can make from this module. Run the specific language from Section 1 as a drill — have every rep in the room say it out loud at least twice until it sounds natural, not rehearsed.
- The red light/green light framework is essential for preventing reputation damage. Reps who ask for referrals at the wrong moment don't just miss the referral — they damage the primary relationship.
- The 30-day post-PTO check-in is a simple habit that most reps never build. It should be made a standing SOP requirement in your company's post-close process.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "I should wait until PTO to ask for referrals — that's when they're happiest." Day 0 and install day are equally powerful moments. PTO is often delayed and by the time it arrives the emotional peak from signing has passed.
- "Asking for referrals feels pushy." The language in this module is designed to feel like care, not a pitch. The framing is always about who in the homeowner's network would benefit — not about what the rep needs.
- "My job is done at PTO — if they want to refer, they will." Referrals almost never happen without a specific ask. The homeowner may have the impulse but without a clear invitation from the rep, it doesn't convert into action.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- Reps don't need a five-step referral system. They need three things: the right language, the right moment, and the habit of actually using both.
- The trust behaviors section should be a recognition exercise — not a lecture. Ask reps: "What's the most impressive thing a service provider has ever done for you that made you want to tell someone about it?" Use their answers.

**Where to slow down:**
- The Day 0 referral language. Have reps deliver it in pairs — one playing the rep, one playing the homeowner who just signed. Give feedback on tone: it should sound warm and natural, not scripted.
- The red light/green light moments. Have reps identify the last time they asked for a referral and assess whether the timing was right based on this framework.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "The Day 0 Referral Moment"
**Homeowner type:** Just signed, enthusiastic, knows several neighbors who have expressed interest in solar
**Setup:** The contract is signed. The rep has just walked through what happens next. The homeowner is in a great mood and says, "We've been wanting to do this for two years — I'm so glad we finally did it." The rep has a clear green-light opening.
**Skill being tested:** Delivering the Day 0 referral ask naturally and effectively — without sounding transactional, without pressure, and with the right framing around who in the homeowner's network would benefit.
**Pass criteria:** Rep delivers the Day 0 ask smoothly and in their own voice, frames it around the homeowner's network rather than the rep's benefit, does not pressure for an immediate answer, and thanks the homeowner genuinely regardless of whether they produce a name.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The Reputation Test"
**Homeowner type:** Previously enthusiastic, now frustrated after a 10-week permit delay with minimal rep communication
**Setup:** The homeowner has been waiting 10 weeks for a permit. The rep has been sporadic in communication. The homeowner finally reaches out and says: "I referred my brother-in-law to you and now he's asking me how things are going. I don't know what to tell him. This has taken forever and I feel embarrassed."
**Skill being tested:** Handling a reputation risk that emerged from poor post-close communication — recovering the relationship, addressing the referral's experience, and resetting the communication standard going forward.
**Pass criteria:** Rep takes full ownership of the communication gap without excuses, provides an honest current status update, addresses what to tell the brother-in-law specifically, and establishes a clear communication cadence going forward. Does not deflect blame to the permit office.

---

### Scenario 3 — "The Install Day Referral"
**Homeowner type:** Proud, social, already taking photos and sharing on social media
**Setup:** It's installation day. The rep checks in by call. The homeowner says: "Three neighbors have already stopped by to ask what's happening! My next-door neighbor said she's been thinking about solar for years."
**Skill being tested:** Capitalizing on a live, active referral signal during the highest-visibility moment in the project — converting a casual comment into a concrete introduction.
**Pass criteria:** Rep immediately recognizes the referral signal, asks a specific qualifying question about the neighbor, offers to come back by or connect directly, and makes the homeowner feel like a connector rather than a recruiter.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
Why is the moment immediately after a homeowner signs (Day 0) one of the highest-leverage moments for a referral ask?
- A) Because the homeowner is locked in and can't say no
- B) Because the homeowner is at peak emotional buy-in, before any pipeline friction has entered the picture
- C) Because it's the only time the rep will see the homeowner in person
- D) Because referral bonuses are highest on the day of signing
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
What is the difference between a referral ask that feels transactional and one that feels like a natural extension of trust? Give one example of each.

**3. Multiple Choice**
A homeowner just received news that their permit is taking an additional 6 weeks due to a correction request. They are frustrated. What should the rep do regarding referrals?
- A) Ask for a referral immediately — the rep needs to make up for the delay
- B) Make no referral ask — this is a red light moment; focus entirely on the primary relationship
- C) Ask for a referral but frame it around the homeowner's network
- D) Wait until next week, then ask
*(Correct answer: B)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A homeowner's neighbor has been asking questions about their solar project. The homeowner mentions this casually during the install day check-in call. What should the rep say and do next?
*(Expected answer: The rep should immediately recognize this as a live referral signal. Ask a qualifying question: "What's their situation — are they a homeowner? Do you think they'd want to have a conversation?" Offer to connect directly: "If you're comfortable, I'd love to come by and say hello, or I could send them a quick text if you share their number. I'll make sure they get the same level of attention you got." Make the homeowner feel like they're doing their neighbor a favor, not doing the rep a favor.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
The 30-day post-PTO check-in accomplishes four things: it reinforces the _______, catches early _______ before they become complaints, opens a natural _______ moment, and positions the rep as a _______ rather than a transactional closer.
*(Correct: relationship; system concerns; referral; long-term partner)*

---

## 8. Slide Deck Outline

1. **Title Slide** — "Day 0 Referral & Reputation Protection: The Referral Business Starts at Signing"
2. **Why Most Reps Wait Too Long** — The cost of waiting until PTO to ask for referrals
3. **Why Day 0 Is the Highest-Leverage Moment** — Emotional buy-in, no friction, peak enthusiasm
4. **The Day 0 Referral Ask** — The specific language; how to frame it around the homeowner's network
5. **The Four Referral Moments in the Pipeline** — Day 0, survey confirmation, install day, PTO
6. **Reading the Room: Green Light vs. Red Light** — When to ask and when to protect the relationship
7. **Referral Generation Through Trust** — The behaviors that earn referrals before the ask
8. **What Makes a Referral Ask Feel Natural vs. Transactional** — The framing difference
9. **Reputation Protection After the Close** — The post-sale behaviors that keep trust alive
10. **The 30-Day Post-PTO Check-In** — The single highest-leverage post-close touchpoint
11. **Key Takeaways + Module Recap**

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"How many of you have asked for a referral in the last week? [Hands.] How many of you asked on the day someone signed? [Fewer hands.] Here's what I want you to understand: the moment someone signs is the warmest they will ever be toward you in this relationship — before any delays, before any friction, before they've had to wait ten weeks for a permit. Most reps pack up and leave. Today we're going to make sure you never leave that moment without planting a referral seed."

**Concept Explanation (12 min)**
Walk through the Day 0 referral language and the four pipeline moments. Spend significant time on the green light/red light framework — this is where most reps make mistakes in both directions (asking at the wrong time or never asking at all).

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's what the install day referral looks like in real life. You call to check in. The homeowner says, 'Three neighbors already stopped by.' That's a live referral signal — right there, in that sentence. Most reps say 'That's great!' and move on. Here's what you say instead: [walk through the install day language from Section 2]. The difference between those two responses is a commission."

**Activity Transition (5 min)**
Exercise 6.1A-1 (Day 0 Referral Ask Practice) — write individually, then deliver to a partner in a roleplay. Partner plays the homeowner who just signed. Trainer listens for tone: warm and genuine vs. scripted and transactional.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"The referral business is not built at PTO. It's built one conversation at a time, starting at the signing table. Every homeowner you close is either a one-time transaction or the beginning of a network. Which one they become is almost entirely determined by how you treat them after the close — and whether you give them a natural way to bring the people they care about into the conversation."

---

---


#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Why Referrals Are Worth More**  
Referrals close at forty to sixty percent \u2014 compared to twenty to thirty percent for cold leads. The trust is already established before you say a word. And the customer acquisition cost is zero. A single referral pipeline can eventually replace cold prospecting entirely. The consultants who build six-figure incomes treat referral generation as a system \u2014 not an afterthought. You are going to leave today with a repeatable three-step referral process that runs every time you close a deal.

**The 3-Step Referral System**  
Step one: plant the seed at the close. Before you leave: the biggest thank-you is a referral. If anyone you know has been complaining about their electric bill, I'd love an introduction. Step two: activate post-install. When their system goes live, send an excited message and ask: who's the first person you'd want to tell about this? Step three: personalized follow-up. Reach out to each referral by name, mentioning the mutual connection. A referral outreach that says your neighbor mentioned you've been thinking about solar opens at a completely different level than a cold call.

### Module 6.2: Reputation Building & Online Presence

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 6.2A — CRM Discipline & 7-7-7 Pipeline Management

**Insert after Module 6.1A | Day 6 — Referrals, Performance & Operator Discipline**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Revenue doesn't disappear at closing — it disappears in the pipeline between closing and PTO when reps lose track of their deals, fail to follow up at the right moments, and let good leads go cold. Module 6.2A gives reps a concrete, repeatable system for managing their pipeline with the same discipline a business operator brings to managing a portfolio — not the passive approach of hoping operations keeps everything moving.

### Why It Matters
A solar rep's income is a portfolio of open projects at different pipeline stages, plus new leads being developed in parallel. Without a system to track both, reps either focus entirely on new sales (and watch post-close deals erode) or get consumed by pipeline management (and stop generating new business). The 7-7-7 framework and CRM discipline standards taught in this module are how reps manage both simultaneously without dropping either.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module directly builds on the post-sale lifecycle (Module 5.5A), the escalation framework (Module 5.5C), and the referral system (Module 6.1A). It is the operational backbone that makes all of those frameworks executable at scale. A rep who completes this module has a personal operating system for their business — not just sales skills.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Implement the 7-7-7 pipeline management framework to track active deals across all lifecycle stages.
2. Write CRM notes that are specific, actionable, and useful to the entire project team.
3. Build a personal follow-up cadence that keeps deals moving without becoming administrative overhead.
4. Reactivate stalled deals using a structured re-engagement approach.
5. Treat PTO — not installation — as the true finish line in all pipeline tracking and communication.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — The 7-7-7 Pipeline Management Framework
The 7-7-7 framework is built around a simple principle: a rep's attention to a deal should be proportional to how recently it moved. Deals that moved recently need light tracking. Deals that haven't moved need intervention.

**The framework:**

**7 days — Check in on every deal that hasn't had a status update**
Any deal that has not had a meaningful status update (from the survey team, operations, or the homeowner) in seven days should receive a rep-initiated check-in with the project coordinator or homeowner. Seven days of silence in the pipeline is the early warning signal.

**7 weeks — Flag any deal that has been in the same stage for more than 7 weeks**
A deal stuck at the same pipeline stage for more than seven weeks without forward movement is at elevated cancellation risk. This is the threshold for manager escalation per the framework from Module 5.5C. Flag it, escalate it, and document what's blocking it.

**7 touches — Every homeowner should receive at least 7 meaningful touches from contract to PTO**
The 7-touchpoint model from Module 5.5A is not just about preventing cancellations — it is the minimum viable pipeline management cadence. A rep who hits all seven touchpoints has a dramatically lower cancellation rate than one who checks in only at crisis moments.

**Teaching point:** The 7-7-7 framework is not a rigid rule — it is a calibration system. Markets with faster permit timelines may use a 5-5-5 cadence. Markets with utility backlog may extend to 10-10-10. Reps should localize the numbers to their market while preserving the principle: track recency, flag stagnation, maintain touchpoints.

### Section 2 — Milestone Tracking: What to Track and Why
Reps who track milestones don't get surprised. Reps who don't track milestones rely on operations to tell them when something is wrong — which is always too late.

**The six milestones every rep should be tracking for every active deal:**

1. **Survey completed** — Date completed, any findings flagged by the survey team
2. **Design approved** — Date finalized, any design changes from the original proposal
3. **Permit submitted** — Date submitted, jurisdiction, expected processing time
4. **Permit approved** — Date approved, any corrections required before approval
5. **Install scheduled** — Date scheduled, homeowner confirmed
6. **PTO received** — Date received, homeowner notified, 30-day check-in scheduled

**The simplest tracking tool:** A rep doesn't need a sophisticated system. A spreadsheet with one row per active deal and columns for each milestone date is sufficient. What matters is that it is updated consistently — not that it is elaborate.

**What to do when a milestone is overdue:**
- Survey not scheduled within 10 business days of signing → Contact project coordinator
- Design not finalized within 4 weeks of survey → Contact project coordinator
- Permit not submitted within 3 weeks of design approval → Contact project coordinator
- Permit stuck for more than 8 weeks with no update → Escalate to manager
- Install not scheduled within 2 weeks of permit approval → Contact project coordinator
- PTO not received within 6 weeks of passing inspection → Contact project coordinator

*Note to trainers: Localize these timelines to your market and your company's actual SLAs before delivering this section.*

### Section 3 — CRM Note Quality Standards
A CRM note that a project coordinator cannot act on is not a CRM note — it is a timestamp.

**The three standards for quality CRM notes:**

**Standard 1 — Specific**
Every note must answer: what happened, when, and who was involved.
- Bad: "Called homeowner — update given."
- Good: "Called [homeowner name] on [date]. Informed them permit is at week 9 — no correction yet. Homeowner frustrated but stable. Committed to calling back by [date] with an update. No cancellation language used."

**Standard 2 — Actionable**
Every note must make the next step clear — for the rep and for anyone else who reads it.
- Bad: "Roof issue discussed."
- Good: "Survey team flagged possible roof condition issue on SE-facing section. Rep informed homeowner on [date] that survey team will provide written assessment within [X days]. Homeowner aware — no concerns raised. Rep to follow up once written assessment received."

**Standard 3 — Tagged to stage**
Every note should indicate what pipeline stage the deal is in at the time of the note.
- Format: [Stage: Permitting] — [Date] — [Note content]
- This makes it instantly clear where each deal stands when someone reviews the CRM record

**The 2-minute rule:** After every homeowner interaction (call, text, email), reps should spend 2 minutes writing a quality CRM note while the conversation is fresh. Waiting until end of day produces vague, incomplete notes that serve no one.

### Section 4 — Follow-Up Cadence: Active vs. Passive Deals
Not all deals require the same attention at the same time. The follow-up cadence should be calibrated to the deal's current stage and risk level.

**Active cadence (weekly contact minimum):**
- Deals in the permitting stage (highest friction period)
- Deals where the homeowner has expressed any concern or frustration
- Deals where a competitor has been mentioned
- Deals where the homeowner has been hard to reach

**Standard cadence (bi-weekly contact):**
- Deals in design or pre-permit stages moving at normal speed
- Deals where the homeowner is patient and engaged
- Deals in the post-install/pre-PTO stage

**Light cadence (monthly check-in):**
- Deals in very early stages (contract signed, survey not yet scheduled)
- Deals that are clearly progressing normally with no flags
- Post-PTO deals being cultivated for referrals

**Teaching point:** The cadence should flex with the deal's status. A deal that was on light cadence can move to active cadence the moment a flag emerges. A deal that was on active cadence can step back to standard cadence once stability returns. The rep should assess cadence on every interaction.

### Section 5 — Reactivation Habits: Bringing Stalled Deals Back to Life
Every rep has deals that have gone quiet — homeowners who have stopped responding, projects that seem to have stalled with no clear reason. Reactivation is the art of re-engaging these deals before they formally cancel.

**The reactivation sequence:**

**Contact 1 — Warm re-engagement (no mention of the stall):**
"Hey [name] — wanted to touch base and make sure everything is still going well on your end. I have an update on your project: [specific status update]. Give me a call when you have a few minutes."

**Contact 2 — Value add (if no response to Contact 1 within 5 business days):**
"Just wanted to share a quick project update: [status]. Also wanted to mention — [something relevant to their situation: rate change, incentive update, install schedule opening]. Happy to answer any questions."

**Contact 3 — Direct honest outreach (if no response to Contact 2 within 5 business days):**
"[Name] — I want to make sure I'm still serving you well. I haven't been able to reach you and I want to make sure everything is okay. If something has come up or if you have any concerns, I'd much rather know so we can work through it together. Please reach out."

**If no response after 3 contacts:** Escalate to manager per the framework from Module 5.5C. Do not continue cold outreach — it creates pressure and may accelerate cancellation decisions.

### Section 6 — PTO as the True Finish Line
The most common pipeline tracking failure among reps is treating installation day as the finish line. This is operationally incorrect and behaviorally damaging.

**Why this matters:**
- Reps who stop tracking after install are unaware when PTO is delayed
- Homeowners who don't hear from the rep after install — and whose system sits uninspected and inactive — become frustrated and cancel
- Referral conversations triggered at PTO (the emotional finish line) are lost when the rep has already moved on

**PTO is the finish line. Everything else is a milestone.**

**Post-install rep behaviors that protect deals and referrals:**
- Check in within 24 hours of installation to confirm the homeowner's experience
- Track the inspection scheduling — confirm it happened and know the outcome
- Track the PTO application — know when it was submitted and what the utility's expected processing time is
- Contact the homeowner the day PTO is received — make it a celebration, not an administrative notification
- Schedule the 30-day post-PTO check-in at the time PTO is confirmed

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The 7-7-7 framework is a calibration tool, not a rigid rule. Help reps understand the principle (track recency, flag stagnation, maintain touchpoints) and localize the numbers to your market.
- CRM note quality is a team sport. When reps write poor notes, the survey team, project coordinators, and managers pay the price. Make this cost concrete with examples from your own company.
- PTO as the finish line is the most important framing shift in this module. If installation is treated as the finish line, post-install communication collapses — and with it, the referral pipeline.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "I track my deals in my head / in my phone." Not scalable, not shareable, not recoverable if the rep is sick or leaves the company. Structured tracking is non-negotiable.
- "The project coordinator is tracking all this — I don't need to." The project coordinator tracks operational milestones. The rep tracks the homeowner relationship and the deal's health. These are different — both are required.
- "My deals don't stall — I close clean." Every rep has stalls. The rep who thinks they don't have stalls just doesn't know where their deals are.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- The tracking system doesn't need to be a sophisticated CRM with custom fields. A well-maintained spreadsheet is sufficient for most reps at the stage where they're learning this system.
- The cadence numbers (7 days, 7 weeks, 7 touches) are anchors, not absolutes. The principle matters more than the specific numbers.

**Where to slow down:**
- CRM note quality standards. Have reps look at their actual recent CRM notes (or write representative examples) and grade them against the three standards. The gap between what they're writing and what's useful is often significant.
- The reactivation sequence. Have reps identify a real stalled deal and draft the three reactivation contacts for it in the session. Immediate application.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "The 7-Week Stall"
**Homeowner type:** Patient but losing faith — project has been in permitting for 11 weeks
**Setup:** A rep does a 7-7-7 audit and realizes one of their deals has been in the permitting stage for 11 weeks with no update in 8 days. The homeowner last communicated 3 weeks ago and sounded tired. The rep needs to assess the deal's health and take action.
**Skill being tested:** Using the 7-7-7 framework to identify a stall, taking the right internal escalation action, and contacting the homeowner with an honest update — all within the same workflow.
**Pass criteria:** Rep correctly identifies the deal as needing manager escalation per the 7-week flag, gathers a current permit status before calling the homeowner, calls the homeowner proactively with an honest update and a specific next step, and schedules a follow-up rather than leaving the homeowner in uncertainty.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The CRM Audit"
**Homeowner type:** N/A — this is an internal process scenario
**Setup:** A manager reviews a rep's CRM notes on three active deals and finds: "Called HO — update given." / "Permit still pending." / "Homeowner happy." The manager asks the rep to explain the current status of each deal. The rep doesn't have clear answers.
**Skill being tested:** Recognizing the cost of poor CRM documentation and understanding what quality notes should look like — applied to real situations.
**Pass criteria:** Rep acknowledges the gap, can articulate what information is missing from each note, rewrites the three notes with specific and actionable language, and commits to the 2-minute rule going forward.

---

### Scenario 3 — "The Post-Install Silence"
**Homeowner type:** Good relationship through the project; rep went quiet after installation
**Setup:** A homeowner calls the rep three weeks after installation to say: "My panels have been on the roof for three weeks. Nobody has called me. I don't know what's happening. Is my system going to turn on? My neighbor's system was live the same day they installed." The rep hasn't been tracking post-install milestones.
**Skill being tested:** Recovering from post-install communication failure by immediately re-establishing tracking, explaining the PTO process, and restoring homeowner confidence — while committing to the correct finish-line definition going forward.
**Pass criteria:** Rep does not make excuses. Immediately gets the homeowner a current status on the inspection and PTO process. Explains the PTO timeline accurately. Establishes a specific next communication point. Internally updates their tracking to treat PTO as the finish line from this point forward.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
In the 7-7-7 framework, what does the "7 weeks" component indicate?
- A) Reps should check in with homeowners every 7 weeks
- B) Any deal stuck at the same pipeline stage for more than 7 weeks should be flagged and escalated — it is at elevated cancellation risk
- C) Permits take approximately 7 weeks in most markets
- D) Reps should complete 7 deals before receiving their full commission
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
What are the three standards for a quality CRM note? Write a brief example of a note that fails all three and a note that meets all three — for the same homeowner interaction.

**3. Multiple Choice**
A rep's CRM note reads: "Homeowner seemed worried. Will follow up." This note fails because:
- A) It uses informal language
- B) It doesn't specify what the homeowner was worried about, what the rep said, what follow-up action will be taken, or when
- C) CRM notes should never include emotional observations about homeowners
- D) It is too short
*(Correct answer: B)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
A rep closed 12 deals last month. They are tracking them "in their head" and relying on the project coordinator to flag issues. Three deals are now 9 weeks into permitting with no update. The homeowners haven't complained yet. What should the rep do?
*(Expected answer: Immediately run a 7-7-7 audit on all 12 deals. The three deals at 9 weeks in permitting have already exceeded the 7-week flag threshold — they should have been escalated two weeks ago. The rep should contact the project coordinator immediately for a status on all three, escalate the ones with no movement to their manager, and contact each homeowner before they reach out first. Going forward, the rep must implement structured milestone tracking.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
PTO is the _______ of a solar project — not _______. Reps who stop tracking after _______ leave homeowners without communication during the most confusing stage of the pipeline — the period between _______ and when the system actually turns on.
*(Correct: finish line; installation day; installation; installation)*

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"I want to ask you something. Right now, without looking at your phone or your CRM — how many active deals do you have in the pipeline? [Pause.] Now — for each one, do you know what stage it's in, when the last update happened, and what the next action is? [Longer pause.] If you hesitated on any of those, you have a pipeline management gap. Not a motivation gap, not a skills gap — a systems gap. That's what we're fixing today."

**Concept Explanation (15 min)**
Introduce the 7-7-7 framework clearly before moving to milestone tracking and CRM standards. Use real examples from your market — actual permit timelines, real CRM note examples (anonymized) showing the gap between what reps write and what's useful.

**Example (5 min)**
Show a real (anonymized) CRM record from a deal that went sideways. Walk through the notes. Show where the information was missing, where the rep should have flagged something, and what a quality record for the same deal would have looked like. Make the cost of poor tracking visible and concrete.

**Activity Transition (8 min)**
Exercise 6.2A-1 (7-7-7 Audit) — reps audit their own real pipeline, not a hypothetical. This is the most impactful exercise in the module because it produces immediate, actionable awareness. Give reps 5 minutes to complete, then ask for one volunteer to share what they found. The room invariably finds something that needs attention today.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"Your pipeline is your business. Every deal in it is revenue that can be protected or lost — and the difference is almost always whether the rep was paying attention. The 7-7-7 framework is how you pay attention at scale. The CRM habits are how you make sure your attention translates into action that the whole team can build on. These are not administrative tasks. These are how you protect your income."

#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**Google Reviews & Neighborhood Authority**  
Ask every closed customer for a Google review and send the direct link \u2014 make it as frictionless as possible. Reviews compound over time. When a homeowner in your territory searches for solar companies and sees forty-seven five-star reviews including names they recognize from the neighborhood, the trust is already established before you knock. Pair that with yard signs on completed installs and you've created passive social proof at scale. Neighborhood authority is built rep by rep and install by install \u2014 it's one of the most powerful long-term assets you can build in this career.

### Module 6.3: KPI Tracking & Self-Coaching

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 6.3A — Self-Coaching Through Technical & Financial Review

**Insert after Module 6.2A | Day 6 — Referrals, Performance & Operator Discipline**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Most reps improve through feedback from managers and trainers. The best reps improve through feedback from themselves — by developing the ability to audit their own appointments, identify the specific moments where they lost credibility or created confusion, and make targeted corrections before the pattern costs them more deals. Module 6.3A teaches reps how to self-coach on technical and financial conversations specifically — the two areas where V2 knowledge is most likely to surface errors in the field.

### Why It Matters
Technical and financial missteps compound over time. A rep who consistently over-promises on backup capability, consistently uses outdated incentive language, or consistently presents vague financial stories doesn't just lose individual deals — they build a reputation that is hard to recover from. Self-coaching prevents the pattern from becoming permanent by catching individual errors before they calcify into habits.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module is the internal quality control layer for everything taught in V2. Modules 1.5A–1.5B (technical foundation), 4.1A–4.5A (financial fluency), and the compliance content throughout the curriculum all require field application. This module gives reps the framework to monitor and improve that application on their own — between formal coaching sessions.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Conduct a structured post-appointment self-audit focused on technical and financial language quality.
2. Identify specific moments in a sales conversation where vague or risky language was used.
3. Write corrected versions of their own statements using the compliance and accuracy standards from V2.
4. Use a simple self-grading rubric to assess their technical and financial credibility on each appointment.
5. Build a personal improvement priority list based on their self-audit findings.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — Why Self-Coaching Works Better Than Waiting for Feedback
Formal coaching is episodic. Self-coaching is continuous. A rep who waits for their manager's weekly feedback session before correcting a mistake in their incentive language has repeated that mistake on every appointment since the last session. A rep who audits themselves after every appointment catches errors within 24 hours.

**The self-coaching advantage:**
- Immediate — errors are caught before they compound
- Private — reps can be honest with themselves without performance anxiety
- Continuous — improvement happens between formal coaching sessions, not only at them
- Targeted — reps can focus specifically on the areas where they know they're weakest

**The self-coaching habit:**
After every appointment (whether or not it results in a close), the rep should spend 10 minutes answering four questions:
1. What technical question came up, and how did I answer it?
2. What financial claim did I make, and was it accurate and compliant?
3. Did I use any language I'm not confident in?
4. If I could do one thing differently, what would it be?

This is not a performance review — it is a learning practice. The bar is honesty, not perfection.

### Section 2 — Reviewing Technical Mistakes: Common Field Errors
These are the most common technical errors reps make after V2 training — areas where the gap between training and field execution tends to be widest.

**Error Type 1 — Overconfident production claims**
- What it sounds like: "Your system will produce about 12,000 kWh per year."
- Why it's risky: Production estimates depend on verified site data — orientation, shading, panel placement. A rep giving production numbers before the site survey is guessing.
- The corrected version: "Based on your roof's orientation and your current usage, the preliminary estimate is around 12,000 kWh — but that gets finalized after our site survey confirms the actual conditions."

**Error Type 2 — Inverter over-recommendation**
- What it sounds like: "Microinverters are just better — you want those."
- Why it's risky: The design team makes equipment decisions based on site data. Reps who pre-commit to equipment create expectation mismatches.
- The corrected version: "Microinverters work really well on complex or shaded roofs — the design team will recommend the right inverter type for your specific situation."

**Error Type 3 — Battery backup oversell**
- What it sounds like: "With the battery, you'll have power through any outage."
- Why it's risky: Backup capability depends on system configuration, battery size, and critical load panel design. "Any outage" is an unqualifiable promise.
- The corrected version: "With a battery, you'll have backup for your most critical circuits — the specific appliances depend on how the backup system is configured. We'll design that around what matters most to you."

**Error Type 4 — Shade minimization**
- What it sounds like: "A little shade won't really affect your production much."
- Why it's risky: Shade impact is highly variable and depends on inverter type, shading source, and timing. Minimizing it sets up post-survey disappointment.
- The corrected version: "Shading does affect production — how much depends on the specific analysis the survey team runs. That's one of the things they'll quantify when they come out."

### Section 3 — Reviewing Financial Explanations: Common Field Errors
These are the most common financial language errors reps make — areas where compliance risk and credibility risk are both highest.

**Error Type 1 — Guaranteed savings language**
- What it sounds like: "You'll save $200 a month with this system."
- Why it's risky: Savings depend on rate structure, production, financing terms, and usage — none of which are fixed. Guaranteed savings language is inaccurate and potentially misleading.
- The corrected version: "Based on your current bill and rate structure, the estimated monthly savings are around $200 — though actual savings depend on your usage, rate changes, and the final system design."

**Error Type 2 — Incentive over-claim**
- What it sounds like: "You'll get 30% back from the government — that's basically a third of the system for free."
- Why it's risky: Federal incentive terms have changed. Tax credit benefits depend on individual tax liability. No rep can guarantee what a homeowner will receive.
- The corrected version: "There are incentive programs that may apply to your situation — the federal tax credit is one of the most significant. How much you benefit depends on your specific tax situation, and your accountant would be the right person to confirm that."

**Error Type 3 — Payback period precision**
- What it sounds like: "Your payback period is exactly 8.3 years."
- Why it's risky: Payback period calculations depend on multiple variables, including future utility rates, system production, and financing costs. Precision implies certainty that doesn't exist.
- The corrected version: "Based on the assumptions we're working with — your current rate, the system design, and the financing terms — the estimated payback period is in the range of 8 to 10 years. If utility rates continue the trend we've seen in this area, it could be shorter."

**Error Type 4 — TOU oversimplification**
- What it sounds like: "Your panels produce power right when the utility charges peak rates, so you're saving at the highest rate."
- Why it's risky: This is often backwards — peak production is around solar noon; peak rates are typically in the late afternoon/evening. Saying this to a homeowner on a TOU plan creates incorrect expectations.
- The corrected version: "Solar produces most of its power around midday — which is actually before the peak rate window in most TOU plans. That's part of why a battery can add real financial value in your situation."

### Section 4 — Spotting Vague or Risky Language: The Self-Audit Rubric
The self-audit rubric gives reps a simple scoring framework to assess their own technical and financial credibility on each appointment.

**The four-question rubric:**

**Question 1 — Did I make any specific claims I couldn't verify?**
Examples: specific production numbers before survey, specific savings numbers without assumptions, specific incentive dollar amounts.
Self-score: 0 (yes, I made unverifiable claims) / 1 (I used appropriate qualifying language) / 2 (I stayed fully within verified data)

**Question 2 — Did I use any outdated language?**
Examples: old federal incentive assumptions, 1:1 net metering language in NEM 3.0 markets, backup battery language that doesn't distinguish critical load from whole-home.
Self-score: 0 (yes, I used outdated language) / 1 (I'm not sure — I need to check) / 2 (I used current and accurate language throughout)

**Question 3 — Did I answer a technical question I wasn't confident about?**
Examples: specific shading percentages, specific inverter brand comparisons, structural assessment of the roof.
Self-score: 0 (yes, I guessed at something I wasn't sure about) / 1 (I hedged appropriately but wasn't fully sure) / 2 (I correctly deferred to the survey team or said "let me verify that")

**Question 4 — Did I oversell a product, outcome, or guarantee?**
Examples: whole-home battery backup, guaranteed payback period, specific net metering credit rates.
Self-score: 0 (yes, I over-promised) / 1 (I qualified but could have been clearer) / 2 (I presented accurately with appropriate caveats)

**Scoring:**
- 7–8: Strong appointment — minimal risk language
- 5–6: Average — one or two areas to improve
- 3–4: Needs work — multiple risk areas to correct
- 0–2: Significant compliance or credibility risk — review with manager

### Section 5 — Improving Through Self-Audit and Feedback
The self-audit is only useful if it produces a specific improvement commitment. The final step of every self-audit is a one-line improvement statement.

**Format:** "In my next appointment, when [trigger situation], I will say [corrected language] instead of [previous language]."

**Example:** "In my next appointment, when a homeowner asks about backup capability, I will say 'A battery backs up your most critical circuits — the specific appliances depend on how the system is configured' instead of 'You'll have power through any outage.'"

This specific format forces the rep to:
- Identify the trigger (when the situation arises)
- Commit to a specific behavior change (what they will say)
- Acknowledge the previous behavior (what they were saying before)

Without all three elements, the improvement commitment is too vague to act on.

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The self-audit rubric is the most practical deliverable in this module. Have reps apply it to a real recent appointment — not a hypothetical — during the session.
- The improvement commitment format (when / I will say / instead of) should be practiced until every rep in the room has written at least one specific commitment before leaving.
- Self-coaching is most effective when it is normalized — not positioned as remediation. The best reps in any organization self-audit. Frame it as a professional practice, not a correction for underperformers.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "I'll know if I said something wrong — I don't need to write it down." Without structured reflection, reps are subject to confirmation bias. They remember what went well and forget what didn't.
- "This is for reps who are struggling." The best reps improve fastest precisely because they self-audit most rigorously. Frame this as a professional discipline, not remediation.
- "I don't have time to do this after every appointment." The self-audit takes 10 minutes. The cost of not doing it — a single deal lost to a compliance issue or a credibility failure — is measured in thousands of dollars.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- The rubric should be simple enough to complete from memory after every appointment. If it requires a reference document every time, it won't be used.
- The goal is improvement, not perfection. A rep who scores 4/8 on the rubric and identifies one specific improvement is making real progress.

**Where to slow down:**
- Error Types in Sections 2 and 3. Read each one aloud, pause, and ask: "Has anyone said something like this? I'm not asking for a raise of hands — just be honest with yourself." The recognition moment is the learning moment.
- The improvement commitment format. Have every rep write one before the session ends. This is the difference between understanding the concept and actually changing behavior.

---

## 6. AI Simulation Scenario Ideas

### Scenario 1 — "The Post-Appointment Debrief"
**Homeowner type:** N/A — this is a rep self-review scenario
**Setup:** After an appointment with a homeowner who asked detailed questions about backup power, incentives, and production estimates, the rep runs a self-audit. They realize they said three things they're not fully confident in: a specific backup duration, an incentive dollar amount, and a production number before the survey.
**Skill being tested:** Running an honest self-audit, identifying specific errors, scoring the appointment accurately, and writing specific improvement commitments for each error.
**Pass criteria:** Rep correctly identifies all three errors, matches each to the appropriate error type from this module, writes corrected versions for each, and produces three specific improvement commitments in the correct format.

---

### Scenario 2 — "The Manager Review"
**Homeowner type:** N/A — this is an internal coaching scenario
**Setup:** A manager listens to a recorded (or role-played) appointment and identifies two technical errors and one financial error. The rep doesn't initially see the errors. The manager asks the rep to self-audit the appointment using the rubric.
**Skill being tested:** Applying the self-audit rubric to an appointment with guidance — moving from externally-identified errors to internal recognition and self-correction.
**Pass criteria:** Rep works through the rubric methodically, identifies the three errors (with some guidance), writes corrected versions, and produces improvement commitments for each. Demonstrates that they understand why each statement was risky, not just that they were told it was.

---

### Scenario 3 — "The High-Stakes Technical Homeowner"
**Homeowner type:** Engineer or financially analytical — asks probing follow-up questions on every claim
**Setup:** During a simulated appointment, a homeowner pushes back on the rep's production estimate, asks for clarification on the backup capability claim, and wants to know the exact terms of the tax credit. The rep feels pressure to answer confidently.
**Skill being tested:** Maintaining technical and financial accuracy under pressure from an analytical homeowner — using qualifying language without appearing evasive or uncertain.
**Pass criteria:** Rep uses appropriate qualifying language on all three topics (production estimate with survey caveat, backup capability with configuration caveat, tax credit with tax advisor referral), maintains confidence and composure throughout, and does not retreat into vagueness or over-claim to satisfy the homeowner's desire for specificity.

---

## 7. Quiz / Assessment Ideas

**1. Multiple Choice**
A rep tells a homeowner: "Your system will produce about 14,000 kWh per year." Before the site survey has been conducted, this statement is:
- A) Accurate — production estimates are based on satellite data which is reliable
- B) Risky — production estimates before the site survey are preliminary and depend on verified site conditions that the survey will confirm
- C) Fine — the rep is just giving a ballpark estimate
- D) A compliance violation that should be reported
*(Correct answer: B)*

**2. Short Answer**
What is the format for a self-coaching improvement commitment, and why does it need all three components (trigger / new behavior / old behavior)?

**3. Multiple Choice**
A rep scores 3 out of 8 on the self-audit rubric after an appointment. The most appropriate next step is:
- A) Disclose the score to their manager immediately
- B) Identify the specific statements that scored 0 or 1, write corrected versions, and create improvement commitments before the next appointment
- C) Accept that some appointments don't go well and move on
- D) Stop using the rubric since a low score is discouraging
*(Correct answer: B)*

**4. Scenario Judgment**
During an appointment, a homeowner asks: "What exactly is the federal tax credit for solar right now?" The rep isn't fully certain of the current program details. What should the rep say?
*(Expected answer: The rep should use verification-first language: "There's a federal tax credit that's been one of the most significant incentives for solar — the current terms are something I want to make sure I give you accurately. Let me confirm the current details and include them in your proposal. Your accountant would also be the right person to talk to about how it applies to your specific tax situation." This is always better than guessing at a dollar amount or percentage.)*

**5. Fill in the Blank**
The self-audit rubric's four questions assess: whether the rep made _______ claims they couldn't verify, whether they used _______ language from outdated programs, whether they answered technical questions they weren't _______ about, and whether they _______ a product, outcome, or guarantee.
*(Correct: specific; outdated; confident; oversold)*

## 8. Slide Deck Outline

1. **Title Slide** — "Self-Coaching Through Technical & Financial Review: Improve Between the Sessions"
2. **Why Self-Coaching Beats Waiting for Feedback** — Immediate, private, continuous, targeted
3. **The 10-Minute Post-Appointment Habit** — The four questions every rep should ask
4. **Technical Error Types** — The four most common field errors with corrected versions
5. **Financial Error Types** — The four most common financial language errors with corrected versions
6. **The Self-Audit Rubric** — The four-question scoring framework; how to use it
7. **Rubric Scoring Guide** — What 7–8 / 5–6 / 3–4 / 0–2 means and what to do about each
8. **The Improvement Commitment Format** — When / I will say / Instead of
9. **Applying the Audit to Real Appointments** — Live example walk-through
10. **Building the Habit** — How often, how long, and how to make it stick
11. **Key Takeaways + Module Recap**

---

## 9. Trainer Script Outline

**Opener (2–3 min)**
"Think about the last appointment where something came out of your mouth and you immediately thought, 'I'm not sure that was exactly right.' Maybe it was a production number. Maybe it was something about the tax credit. Maybe it was a backup promise. [Pause.] Every rep has had that moment. The question is: what do you do with it? Do you move on and hope it doesn't come back? Or do you use it? Today we're going to build the habit of using it."

**Concept Explanation (12 min)**
Walk through the error types in Sections 2 and 3. For each one, read the risky version aloud — let reps recognize it — then read the corrected version. The recognition is the learning. Then introduce the self-audit rubric and walk through all four questions with a sample appointment scenario.

**Example (5 min)**
"Here's a real example. A rep is sitting with a homeowner who asks about backup power. The rep says, 'With the battery, you'll have power through any outage.' That homeowner signs. Three months later, the power goes out for 16 hours. The battery — which is set up on a critical load panel — keeps the refrigerator and lights running but the HVAC shuts off. The homeowner calls, furious: 'You told me I'd have power through any outage.' That's not just a complaint — it's a potential cancellation, a potential refund demand, and a reputation hit in that neighborhood. All of it started with one sentence. Here's the corrected version of that sentence: [deliver it]. That's the difference between a promise and an accurate description."

**Activity Transition (8 min)**
Exercise 6.3A-2 (Real Appointment Review) — reps self-audit a real recent appointment. Give 5 minutes for the self-audit, then 3 minutes for reps to write their improvement commitment. Ask 2–3 volunteers to share their commitment with the group. This normalizes the practice and shows reps that high performers have things to improve too.

**Wrap-Up (3 min)**
"The rep who improves the fastest is not the one who gets the most coaching. It's the one who coaches themselves the most honestly. Ten minutes after every appointment. Four questions. One improvement commitment. That's the habit. Build it this week."

---

---


#### CONTENT SECTIONS
**The Performance Funnel**  
Every rep's results start with one metric at the top: doors knocked. From there the funnel flows down \u2014 conversations, appointments set, appointments sat, proposals presented, deals closed. Your job is to know your conversion rate at each step. If you're knocking a hundred doors and only having five conversations, your opener needs work. If conversations convert to appointments at a low rate, your transition needs work. If appointments show at low rates, your pre-appointment communication needs work. If your close rate is low, objection handling needs work. Know your numbers and you'll always know exactly what to practice.

### Module 6.4: Long-Term Mindset: Career vs. Job

> **V2 Full Content — SeptiVolt Solar Sales Rep Accelerator™**

# MODULE 6.4A — Technical Authority Exam Prep

**Insert after Module 6.3A | Day 6 — Referrals, Performance & Operator Discipline**

---

## 1. Module Overview

### Purpose
Module 6.4A is the preparation gateway for the V2 Technical Authority certification — the knowledge gate that validates whether reps are ready to operate at the V2 standard in the field. It is not a review of basic knowledge. It is structured preparation for scenario-based evaluation across the five V2 knowledge domains: technical, financing, incentive/compliance, battery/NEM, and post-sale operations. Reps who prepare well here leave Day 6 ready for the Day 7 certification assessments.

### Why It Matters
The V2 curriculum represents a significant upgrade in what reps are expected to know and say in the field. The Technical Authority exam is the mechanism that confirms the upgrade has actually landed — not just been heard. Reps who pass demonstrate that they can apply V2 knowledge accurately and confidently in realistic scenarios. Reps who don't pass identify specific gaps before those gaps cost them deals.

### How It Fits the Rep Journey
This module is the final module of Day 6 and connects directly to the Day 7 certification modules: Module 7.4A (Technical Authority Role-Play) and Module 7.7A (Post-Sale Rescue Scenario). The preparation done in this module directly determines how well reps perform in those assessments.

---

## 2. Learning Objectives

By the end of this module, the rep will be able to:

1. Demonstrate working knowledge across all five V2 knowledge domains under scenario-based conditions.
2. Identify their own weakest domain(s) and focus preparation effort accordingly.
3. Use the practice question bank to self-test before the Day 7 assessment.
4. Understand what the Day 7 assessors will be evaluating — and what passing looks like.
5. Approach the Day 7 certification with confidence grounded in preparation — not anxiety grounded in uncertainty.

---

## 3. Full Teaching Outline

### Section 1 — The Five V2 Knowledge Domains
The Technical Authority exam draws from five domains. Reps should know which modules map to which domain and where their preparation gaps are.

**Domain 1 — Solar Technical Foundation**
*(Modules 1.5A, 1.5B, 1.7A)*
- How sunlight becomes electricity; DC vs. AC; the production chain
- String inverters vs. microinverters; what each does and when each is appropriate
- Battery fundamentals; what a battery does and does not do; backup expectations
- Site engineering awareness; roof, panel, electrical, shade, conduit, detached structures

**Domain 2 — Financial Fluency**
*(Modules 4.1A, 4.2A, 4.2B, 4.5A)*
- Time-of-Use rates; flat vs. TOU; how rate structure affects the savings story
- Financing structures; cash, loan, PPA, lease; ownership vs. third-party
- Incentive strategy; tax credit vs. rebate; verification-first language; what not to promise
- ROI concepts; offset, savings, payback, energy inflation, system degradation; the "do nothing" cost path

**Domain 3 — Battery & NEM**
*(Module 4.4A)*
- Net metering evolution; NEM 3.0; export value vs. self-consumption
- Economics-first vs. fear-based battery positioning
- Battery fit criteria; when to lean in on storage and when to be honest about limited ROI
- What not to promise about backup capability or export credits

**Domain 4 — Discovery & Site Awareness**
*(Modules 3.7A, 3.7B, 1.7A)*
- Technical discovery questions; the seven question categories
- Pre-survey information package; the four documentation categories
- Homeowner preparation before the survey; what to say and what not to say
- Documentation quality; specific vs. vague; what makes a note actionable

**Domain 5 — Post-Sale Operations**
*(Modules 5.5A, 5.5B, 5.5C, 5.5D, 6.1A, 6.2A)*
- The seven-stage post-sale lifecycle; where trust is most commonly lost
- Common pipeline issues by stage; MPU, permit corrections, HOA delays, competitor re-entry
- Escalation paths; pre-escalation checklist; the four-part escalation message
- Save-the-deal communication; delay calls, ghosting, spouse pushback, competitor re-entry
- Referral timing; Day 0 referral ask; pipeline referral moments; reputation protection
- CRM discipline; 7-7-7 framework; milestone tracking; PTO as the finish line

### Section 2 — Domain Self-Assessment
Before preparing, reps should honestly assess their confidence in each domain.

**Self-assessment scoring:**
- 3: I can explain this clearly and accurately without notes
- 2: I understand this but might need to check my language
- 1: I know the concept but my field application is uncertain
- 0: This is a gap area — I need significant review before the exam

| Domain | Self-Score (0–3) | Weakest specific topic |
|--------|-----------------|------------------------|
| Solar Technical Foundation | | |
| Financial Fluency | | |
| Battery & NEM | | |
| Discovery & Site Awareness | | |
| Post-Sale Operations | | |

**Preparation priority:** Domains scored 0 or 1 should receive the majority of study time before Day 7. Domains scored 2 should receive targeted language practice. Domains scored 3 should receive scenario-based testing to confirm the confidence is accurate.

### Section 3 — Practice Question Bank

**Domain 1 — Solar Technical Foundation**

Q1: A homeowner asks: "How does solar actually work?" Give a 60-second plain-language explanation.
*(Key elements: panels → DC → inverter → AC → home; excess to battery or grid)*

Q2: A homeowner says they read that microinverters are always better. How do you respond?
*(Key elements: no blanket recommendation; design team decides based on site; explain string vs. micro on appropriate roofs)*

Q3: A homeowner wants to know if their battery will keep their whole house running for 48 hours during an outage. What do you say?
*(Key elements: backup capability depends on system configuration and which circuits are on the backup panel; not a promise you can make; design-forward conversation)*

Q4: During a site visit, you notice the main electrical panel is labeled 100A. The homeowner hasn't asked about it. What do you do?
*(Key elements: proactively flag it, explain what it may mean, frame as something the survey team will confirm, do not guarantee cost or timeline)*

---

**Domain 2 — Financial Fluency**

Q5: A homeowner on a TOU rate plan asks why solar doesn't just cancel out their entire bill. Explain in plain language.
*(Key elements: TOU peak hours vs. peak production timing; midday production vs. evening cost; how battery addresses the gap)*

Q6: A homeowner says they heard they'll get 30% back from the government. How do you respond?
*(Key elements: verification-first language; tax credit vs. rebate distinction; depends on individual tax liability; refer to accountant)*

Q7: A homeowner asks about the difference between a PPA and a solar loan. Explain both.
*(Key elements: ownership vs. third-party; incentive eligibility; PPA rate escalation clause; loan has interest and dealer fees but homeowner owns the system)*

Q8: A homeowner says the financial projections seem too good to be true. How do you respond?
*(Key elements: walk through the assumptions; offer the conservative version; do not become defensive; assumption transparency builds trust)*

---

**Domain 3 — Battery & NEM**

Q9: A homeowner in California asks about NEM 3.0 and whether solar is still worth it. How do you address this?
*(Key elements: NEM 3.0 changed export credit value, not solar's overall value; self-consumption is now more valuable than exporting; battery time-shifts production to match peak cost periods)*

Q10: A homeowner isn't interested in backup power but is on a TOU rate. How do you make the battery case?
*(Key elements: economics-first — not backup; time-shifting solar production to avoid peak rates; stored power at noon used at 7 PM instead of grid power at peak rates)*

Q11: A homeowner is in a legacy NEM market with 1:1 net metering and a small 5 kW system. The company is pushing battery add-ons. What do you do?
*(Key elements: honest battery fit assessment; the economics don't strongly support battery in this scenario; don't oversell; integrity protects referrals)*

---

**Domain 4 — Discovery & Site Awareness**

Q12: You're 10 minutes into a discovery conversation. Name three technical questions you would have asked by now and explain what each one is surfacing.
*(Key elements: any three from the seven question banks with accurate "what you're surfacing" explanations)*

Q13: A homeowner mentions they're in an HOA and that a neighbor was denied solar approval. What do you say?
*(Key elements: acknowledge seriously; most states protect solar rights; HOA review is a process not a permanent barrier; flag for operations; set timeline expectation)*

Q14: What are the four categories of information in the Pre-Survey Rep Information Package?
*(Key elements: roof information; electrical information; site and property information; homeowner context)*

---

**Domain 5 — Post-Sale Operations**

Q15: Name the seven stages of the solar project lifecycle in order.
*(Key elements: Contract → Survey → Design → Permit → Install → Inspection → PTO)*

Q16: A homeowner calls 10 weeks after signing. They haven't heard from the rep and are considering canceling. Their project is in the permitting stage. What should the rep do — and what should they have done differently?
*(Key elements: call immediately; take ownership of the communication gap; provide current status; set next check-in; should have been following the 7-touchpoint model and the 7-7-7 framework)*

Q17: Write the four-part escalation message for a deal at cancellation risk.
*(Key elements: Who — homeowner name/deal ID; What — specific issue; State — homeowner emotional state and cancellation risk level; Ask — specific action needed and by when)*

Q18: A homeowner's spouse is pushing back on the financing terms four days after signing. How do you handle it?
*(Key elements: don't get defensive; find the specific concern; offer a direct conversation — not a re-presentation; give space without pressure; keep the relationship alive)*

### Section 4 — What the Day 7 Assessment Is Evaluating
The Day 7 Technical Authority role-play is not a multiple choice exam. It is a scenario-based evaluation of whether the rep can apply V2 knowledge accurately in a realistic conversation. Assessors are not looking for perfect recitation — they are looking for:

**Accuracy:** Is the technical or financial claim correct?
**Compliance:** Is the incentive or performance language within the verification-first standard?
**Clarity:** Can the rep explain complex topics simply and confidently?
**Composure:** Does the rep maintain accuracy under pressure from a skeptical or analytical homeowner?
**Recovery:** When the rep makes an error, can they catch and correct it naturally?

**What passing looks like:**
- All major claims are verifiable or appropriately qualified
- No outdated incentive language or backup over-promises
- Technical questions are answered accurately or appropriately deferred
- Financial explanations include relevant assumptions and avoid guaranteed outcomes
- The overall conversation would not put the homeowner or the company at legal or reputational risk

**What not passing looks like:**
- Unqualified production estimates before survey
- "Everyone gets the tax credit" language
- "Your battery will keep your house running" language
- Significant factual errors on inverter types, NEM 3.0, or financing structures
- Inability to explain TOU rates, the "do nothing" cost path, or the post-sale lifecycle accurately

### Section 5 — The Day Before the Assessment: Final Preparation Protocol
**Two hours of structured preparation:**

**Hour 1 — Domain review**
- Score yourself on all five domains using the self-assessment from Section 2
- Spend 30 minutes on your two lowest-scoring domains — focused review of specific concepts
- Spend 15 minutes reviewing the error types from Module 6.3A and your improvement commitments
- Spend 15 minutes reviewing the post-sale lifecycle stages and the escalation framework

**Hour 2 — Scenario practice**
- Work through 5–8 practice questions from the bank — specifically from your weakest domains
- Deliver your Day 0 referral ask, delay call framework, and escalation message out loud — not silently
- Practice the hardest question for you out loud until it sounds natural, not rehearsed
- Review your self-coaching improvement commitments from Module 6.3A

**The night before:**
- Review the five domain areas at a high level only — do not try to re-read entire modules
- Get adequate sleep — recall and composure under pressure both depend on it
- Trust the preparation you've done over the full V2 curriculum

---

## 4. Trainer Notes

**What to emphasize:**
- The domain self-assessment is the most important outcome of this module. Every rep should leave knowing specifically which domain is their weakest and what they're going to do about it before Day 7.
- Scenario-based practice is the only practice that transfers to the Day 7 role-play. Reading notes is not sufficient. Reps must deliver answers out loud.
- Frame the exam as a tool, not a threat. The Technical Authority assessment is how reps prove to themselves — and to homeowners — that the V2 training has actually changed how they operate.

**Common rep misunderstandings:**
- "I just need to study the slides." The Day 7 assessment is scenario-based, not a recitation test. Studying slides is necessary but not sufficient — scenario practice is what builds exam readiness.
- "I'm already doing all this stuff in the field." The exam will surface whether field practice aligns with V2 standards. Some reps are surprised to find habits they thought were correct are actually inconsistent with the V2 compliance posture.
- "If I fail, I'm done." The exam is a learning gate, not a termination trigger. Reps who don't pass on Day 7 are given targeted remediation and a re-assessment path. The goal is development, not elimination.

**What not to overcomplicate:**
- The practice question bank should be used as a drill, not a memorization exercise. Reps should be able to answer each question conversationally — not by reciting a script.
- The domain self-assessment numbers are for the rep's own prioritization — not for manager judgment. Create psychological safety around honest scoring.

**Where to slow down:**
- The "what the assessment is evaluating" section. Many reps are anxious about the assessment and that anxiety is best addressed through clarity — not reassurance. Knowing exactly what assessors are looking for removes the unknown.
- The Day 7 preparation protocol. Walk through it together so reps can ask questions and build a personal study plan before they leave the room.

---
